The Argonaut publishes bright weekly letters from
New York, Paris, and London, from skilled correspondents
“ The Bulletin ” says
who are in touch with the intense life of those great cities.
It publishes frequent letters from other points in the
old world, and from the older world of Asia come occa-
sional letters from India, China, and Japan. Its short
the “Argonaut” is
stories are famous throughout the world. It is hard to
describe their peculiar tinge, but an idea may be gathered
from this, our standing injunction to our contributors :
“ Whatever you are, never be commonplace.” The re-
the most readable
suit is that the Argonaut short stories have an individu
ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
weekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
American newspaper.
books, rather than lengthy reviews, together with notes
upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader well
up with the literary gossip of the day.

American mail; price, Bd. ; posted, 9d. ; mailed regularly
to any Australasian address.

THE RED PAGE.
WHEN THE GRASS SHALL COVER ME.
When the grass shall cover me,
Head to foot where I am lying,—
When not any wind that blows,
Summer-blooms nor winter-snows,
Shall awake me to your sighing :
Close above me as you pass,
You will say, “How kind she was,*
You will say, “How true she was,”
When the grass grows over me.
When the grass shall cover me,
Holden close to earth’s warm bosom, —
While I laugh, or weep, or sing,
Nevermore, for anything,
You will find in blade and blossom,
Sweet small voices, odorous,
Tender pleaders in my cause,
That shall speak me as I was—
When the grass grows over me.
When the grass shall cover me!
Ah, beloved, in my sorrow
Very patient I can wait,
Knowing that, or soon or late,
There will dawn a clearer morrow:
When your heart will moan': “Alas !
Now I know how true she was;
Now I know how dear she was ”
When the grass grows over me !
Ina Coolbrith.
About Browning.
Arthur A. D. Bayldon writes—
A man with any pretensions to critical faculty
cannot but feel his heart leap at the name of
Browning. The work of the man—its magnificent
reach and subtle interplay among the mysterious
cog-wheels of mind machinery, its moral force and
vivid gleams of poetry—will ultimately be recog-
nised as the most extraordinary literary output of
this century. And the amount of work done ;
why, it’s prodigious ! Singer, poet, craftsman,
and more besides. His mind travelled with such
lightning-like rapidity that his words stumbled
and he became obscure. And yet—and let this be
the final answer to the parrot-cries of his Lillipu-
tian critics : you can tear out whole poems and
obscure passages from Browning without affecting
his position one iota. Whatever his shortcomings
he has no weakness; no thin querulous whinings
of pessimism ; no pandering to the Philistine ; no
dilettante word-efflorescences for vitiated literary
palates. And what abrupt decision ; what stabs ;
what bursts of rapture :
In at Heaven and out again
Lightning / Where it broke the roof
Blood-like, a few drops of rain.
O lyric love, half angel and half bird,
And all a wonder and a wild desire.
Rapture dying along its verge (on a lunar rainbow).
Lore would have admired some of his pictures :
think of ‘ ‘ Childe Ronald ” ; Dickens would have
envied them: remember “Christmas Eve and
Easter Day ” : Wordsworth would have recognised
him as a master of landscape :
The trees bend o’er it (a pool)
As wise men watch a sleeping girl;
Byron would have loved his force :
Then broke
The thunder like a sea overhead ;
and Heine started at his own vitriol-biting phrases;
Bohemia’s mouth-piece:
We have not sighed deep, laughed free,
Starved, feasted, despaired—been happy.
And finally, as a specimen of royal color, tone,
and splendor of poetic touch all hi& own, take:
How the tresses curled
Into a sumptuous swell of gold and wound
About her like a glory ; even the ground
Was bright as with spilt sunbeams.
Washed by the morning water-gold
Florence lay out on the mountain side.
-* • * *
Hundreds of passage.s stream through the memory.
What a gallery of pictures: tragedy and comedy
elbow us everywhere. The difficulty is to know
where to begin, what to touch upon, how to deaL.
with him. Take some of his poems at random:—
“ The Last Duchess,” with that stinging
Oh, sir, she smiled, no doubt
Whene'er I passed her ; hap who passed without
Much the same smile. This grew; I gave com-
mands,
Then all smiles stopped together.
“The Glove,” with its unique lion-etching; “In
a Gondola, ” with its muffled drum-beat of tragedy;
“A Grammarian’s Funeral,” set to the steady
tramp-rhythm of the climbers’ feet; the patriotic
glow of “Thoughts from the Sea”; the solemn
hush of “After”; the tournament clash of
“ Count Gismond ”; the elaborate friendliness of
“The Laboratory,” with its word-venom; the
infantine mind-mouthing of the primitive “Cali-
ban ” ; the purple saturation of “ Popularity ” ; the
triumphant peal of heroism in “ Herve Riel ”; the
clatter of hoofs in “The Ride to Aix ”; the scath-
ing sarcasm of “Holy Cross Day”; the blood
splashes and bustle of “The Druses”; the pen-
sive autumnal sadness of ‘ ‘ Andrea Del Sarto ”; the
wild shiver of that line, “Who knows but the
world may end to-night ?” in “The Last Ride To-
gether ”; the artistic perfection of “ The Boy and
the Angel ”; the melancholy vision of dead faces
in “A Toccata of Galuppi”; the lyric strength
and sweetness, the very atmosphere and doings of
an ancient people, in “Saul.”
• * * «
Browning is the only man within tilting distance
of Shakespeare. One is objective, the other sub-
jective, in his methods. Browning is the only
English poet that really knows Italy. What a
marvellous rendering of her mountain scenery is
“The Englishman in Italy.” And this colossal
genius was supple, too. See how he could bend
to the wants of the little ones with his “ Pied
Piper.” A great lover, and yet no weakness—no
hankering after the unclean ; an Englishman even
in his heart-trouble : “ The Lost Mistress.” With
one stroke he cleaves the soul of the Bishop of St.
Praxed, and oh ! how true is its revelation of the
Middle Ages. “Rabbi Ben Ezra” is the chant
for the scholar ; the Philistine may have with wel-
come “The Psalm of Life” with its flabby feeble-
ness and topsy-turvy metaphor. This man was a
musician—witness “Abt Vogler”; a thinker—
read “A Death in the Desert ” ; a rollicker full of
grotesqueness; and, to boot, a large-hearted opti-
mist proclaiming the real lesson of life—
If you loved only what were worth your love,
Love were clear gain and wholly well for you :
Make the low nature better by your throes.
Give earth yourself , go up for gain above. ”
* * * *
What were his shortcomings, then ?
He lacked repose and classic severity, the sculp-
tor’s touch of coolness. The myriad wheels of his
intellect were never at rest. He was ever in pur-
suit of a motive. And this very activity oft
drives one from him. The mind craves for
shadowy seclusions when exhausted with life’s tur-
moil. Silence and deep peace—the hush of moun-
tain peaks holding communion with the unknown :
u sei ,' ene dominion of an uplifted statue: the rest
that dwells among the starsthese are not in
Browning. ‘ ‘ Artemis Prologizes ”is emphatically
a failure. He is also deficient in that weird some-
thing, that unearthly bubbling up from the occult
springs of being, that strange glamour that seems
to come from beyond, far, far beyond the western
clouds of sunset, that thrills us through majestic
passages of music and lifts us we know not
whither. The soul crying out to soul across the
barriers of matter that Heine heard in the
Lorelei , ; the indefinable craving for we know
not what that we feel in Keats’—
The same that oft-times hath
Charm’d magic casements, opening on the foam
Of perilous seas, in faery lands forlorn.
* * * *
Browning’s sentimental faculties were subordi-
nate to his intellectual. That streak of Eastern
blood in his veins influenced his coloring—“ The
moon is carried off in purple fire,” and made him
subtle. The Italian language would have suited
him better than the English. Our language is not
for subtle thinkers. In “The Last Confession”
his genius is all nerve, and springs about as though
on pm-wire. What telling breaks and sudden
pauses as he hurries to its close. It is this intense
absorption in piecemealing and refitting the man’s
mind that makes him forget his readers. Words
fly off like chaff from his grinding down to the
centre. In his intense searchlight moods he re-
minds you of Dante. His sharp, smiting picture-
lines are like Dante’s. And he was an admirer of
Dante, too. His physiological touches set the
mmd a-quiver. Consider the last tag in the
Arab Physician ” ; the whole soul of the man is
suddenly thrown to the front, and you see into it
as you can into a woman’s in her postscript. Con-
W the child of the desert’s earnestness and
half-belief m the wonderful with the self-satisfied
philosophy of Cleon, in whose letter Rome’s im-
pending rum is felt.
* * • .
Browning was more than a poet, or a weaver of
phrases, or a sound-sorcerer: he could be all when
he wished, and you oft hear the mellow chiming
of his golden bell: but his home was the soul of
man, feeling each thought-tremor and darting to
its motive.
..* * • *
A critical reader of Browning is struck by his
vocabulary. His fondness for hard craggy words
such as “baulk” crops up everywhere. To one
accustomed to rich full-blooded words or the
mellifluous suavity of several poets this word-
crackmg is unpleasant. (I was thinking of our
own Brunton Stephens when I used the term
mellifluous suavity, ” as I fancy it might agreeably
be applied to the juicy sweetness of his verse )
But these craggy words have a pelting force from
his sinewy mmd. They rattle round you, how-
ever, kicking up a dust of obscurity when he gets
too subtle for the language. But even his worst
passages are sometimes seamed with fire He is
never obscure to himself, and could not understand
this charge against him.
* * • *
One of the most admirable things about Brown-
ing is his sanity, his well-balanced intellect. Lom-
broso is rebuked by the life of this genius. Com-
pared with him most people are prigs or mono-
maniacs. Even Wordsworth’s intellect, steady
as it was, was superlatively egotistical. Matthew
Arnold, the beloved of scholars, with his fastidious-
ness and frock coat and cocksure air of superiority
m company, was merely an overblown school-
master beside Browning. As for Swinburne—he
is insufferable—a fidgety-fingered overstrung
hysteric. Browing had the air of a man of breed-
mg unassuming, genial, not running a conversa-
tion into a literary rut, but touching all things
lightly in simple unaffected English. The wonder
is how he found time for his work, for he was
always in society. And how ready he was to
praise the work of others ! He read every new
volume of Tennyson’s with expressions of admira
tion. Several of his poems exhibit his keen appre-
ciation of Keats and Shelley. For Byron he had
good-humored contempt. Shelley especially
attracted him. That idealist’s life made a profound
impression on him, and no doubt he would have
sponged those spots from his robes of spiritual
whiteness had he been able. The blank verse of
“Pauline” frequently reminds one of Shelley.
What clear, fresh breezes blow through it; what
delight in “Nature”; and that passage about
Spring, how jubilantly lyrical ! Whilst receiving
little influence from others, Browning influenced
his contemporaries. Even Rossetti sometimes
affects his abrupt springy style. There appears to
be a color of bronze about some of Browning’s
poems, or of dull, heavy gold. Every poet has a
Qplor or none at all.
* * * *
Browning, in spite of literary coteries and the
homage paid him by the intellectual giants of his
day, is probably little read. The public doesn’t
read poetry ; in fact, doesn’t understand it;
and certainly the average man never thinks. To
get three meals a day, sleep, procreate his kind,
and have something to spend, is the apex of his
desire. All this literary-circle work is mere sham :
the bow-wowing of "aP Mr. Simpkins to sharp-
featured spinsters about Browning, though it may
create a momentary interest in the poet, must not
be taken seriously. Browning is the poet for
pryers into motives—readers of George Meredith
should be fond of him, if they can stomach verse.
Browping’s influence on Meredith is very marked,
both in his style and mental agility.
* * * *
And so, hardly touching the hem of his gar-
ment, I leave this colossus to his lovers, closing
my garrulity with his own impressive words :
It’s wiser being good than bad ;
It’s safer being meek than fierce ;
It’s fitter being sane than mad.
My oivn hope is, a sun will pierce
The thickest cloud earth ever stretched j
That at the last, returns the first,
Though a wide compass round be fetched :
That what began best can’t be ivorst,
Nor what God once blessed prove accurst.
+
John Sandes (“ Oriel ”) writes for Melb. Argus
topical verses often readable and interesting; and
he has collected two dozen samples in a booklet
full of illustrations and All the advt. and
J. A. Andrews gives his opinion : There seems
to be a connection between the vital decav of the French
race and French poetry. This latter is largely erotic. So,
also, is very much English poetry. But the eroticism of
the English writers is generally objective, appropriately
to a healthy animality. They sing 1 of the charms of some
admired fair one ; the French writers dabble in descrip-
tions of the various subjective emotions which these
charms evoke, and in vague imaginings of the subjective
pleasure of being in love and courting—in the abstract.
This is very noticeable in the works of recent writers, but
One finds the same peculiarity on going as far back as
<Jautier, or even B6ranger. The last-named, perhaps the
least erotic of all French lyricists, devotes his chief, if not
his only sex-poem to pourtraying the sensations aroused
in a young girl’s mind by hearing her pussy-cat meowing
for a tom. Only a worn-out rake and the weakling brood
subjectively upon sex-pleasures ; the vigorous normal man
is occupied with the external object of his affections to
the exclusion of introspective consciousness. The nation,
or class, whose love-poetry is characteristically subjective,
may be safely regarded as in a state of physical de-
generacy.
“GKS.”: Does not Carlyle’s incidental defini-
tion of poetry go close to the mark ? “ Eloquence grown
rhythmic, musical—what we call poetry.” —(French
Revn., Booh 111., Chapter ITI.)
“It is that art, surely, which appeals to the
deepest and commonest feelings of humanity—not exclud-
ing sport, which art generalises under the forms of cour-
age, manliness, heroism : and at the same time idealises
the feelings, views them sub specie aeternitatis, and ex-
presses them in language high, refined, accurate, and
musical- in short, turns them into poetry ; it is that art,
surely, that lives. And the more deeply the poet shares
those feelings, and the more poetically he gives them ob-
jective or articulate form, the more permanently popular
will he be.”— Arnold Haultain, in “Literature
CARBINE’S GOOD-BYE.
Place your palm upon my shoulder, stroke my smooth
and glossy mane,
Let me lay my honest muzzle in my owner’s well-loved
hand ;
I shall never feel my dear old master’s proud caress
again,
Beyond the cruel ocean in a strange and distant land.
And all the illustration and advt. detract little
from the music and pathos of lines (in memory of
Sadie Macdonald) like
THE LAST SONG.
When the scene is lighted brightly, and we watch the
players nightly,
The peasant, and the prince, and the page,
Lo, there comes an apparition, ’tis a little fair-haired
vision
In the middle of the crowded stage.
And across the footlights stealing, to the listening heart
appealing,
Floats the echo of the song that we knew :
“ 1 "'ant you, my honey, yes I want you, want you, want
you,
I want you, ma honey, yes I do.”
Then she passes. Yonder, lonely, she has one true com-
rade only,
Who comes to her with whisperings of hope,
Speeding fast across the ocean with unfaltering devotion,
’Tis the wind from her own Pacific Slope.
And it presses towards her pillow, printing footsteps on
the billow,
Lightly—like one who comes to woo,
And it muniurs low above her, and it answers like a lover,
“ I want you, ma honey, yes I do.”
Ah, the worktime and the playtime ! Ah, the hot and
garish daytime !
She rests undisturbed through it all;
When the sultry hours are creeping, in her darkened room
she’s sleeping,
And she heeds not the loudest call.
But when the lights are glistening, and the silent stars
are listening,
From their places in the steadfast blue,
If we come, our flowers bringing, we may hear her softly
singing,
“ I want you, ma honey, yes I do.”
And when the years have vanished, and more stately songs
are banished,
This simple strain that time cannot kill
Will recall the figure slender, and the accents clear and
tender,
And the tresses that are golden still.
You will listen, little lady, and perhaps you’ll hear us,
Sadie,
Singing—across the years to you,
The song you taught so sweetly, ere you passed from us
completely,
“ I want you, ma honej r , yes I do.”
Between these extremes the Muse of “Oriel”
hovers, her countenance sicklied o’er with the pale
cast of irrelevant illustration and advt.

HEAD THE SYMPTOMS,
and, if you have any of them, you require the remarkable treatment
which lias made both fame and fortune for Drs. FREEMAN &
WALLACE.
In the Laboratory.
Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms :
Do your facil nerves twitch
Are you all run down?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back
Have you pains in head?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms : —•
Do your eyes twitch?
Do your hands tremble ?
Do you feel shaky?
Can you walk without hailing 1
Are you melancholy?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim?
Nervous Debility.
This affliction destroys ambi-
tion, organic stren 41, energy,
and hope. Following are some
of the symptoms :
Do you feel weak?
Have you cold feet?
Have you backache ?
Do you shun society?
Are you losing flesh?
Do you sleep poorly ?
Are you low-spirited?
Are your eyes sunken?
Do you have hot flushes?
Is your memory impaired?

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Lullaby.
—“ You come too late." — Y.J.D.
The sun will smile when the storm has fled '■
What care the leaves on the mould- —
Sleep, oh sleep !
And Love will come when the heart is dead;
How thrills his touch in the. old —
Sleep, oh sleep !
And fame will glare at the blinded mole :
What boots the light in his den —
Sleep, oh sleep !
And Heaven dawns on a perished soul:
My God l what matters it then ?
Sleep, oh sleep!
Q.
VON KOTZE.
Requiescat.
The sunlight beats without the curtained wall;
Within is silence, brooding like a pall.
He is so still—that is the worst of all.
Without, the world is glad and nothing grieves,
The sparrows twitter on the quiet eaves,
The breezes chatter to the rustling leaves.
He sleeps unbroken, though the bright day calls ;
He never heeds the strangers in his halls;
I take his hand in mine—it falls, it falls.
The garden, like a live thing, pants and glows,
Then nestles with the noontide to repose.
A drooping lily stoops to kiss a rose.
His parted lips are smiling meaningful,
The staring eyes gleam—ah ! —so cold and dull,
Beneath the pallid skin I see a skull.
The shadows lengthen into afternoon,
The garden sighs that day declines so soon,
The song-birds in the tree-tops sit and croon.
A lover once I had —a lover such
As dreamers love ; he loved me overmuch,
To-day he sleeps indifferent to my touch.
I stand and gaze, and wonder what it means,
As one wdio, on a threshold, pausing, leans
A little while before he parts the screens.
Without, the stars stream softly into sight,
Within, the tapers shed a ghostly light.
He sleeps on, does he know ’tis night, ’tis night ?
How many nights he soothed my doubts and fears —
How many times he kissed away my tears !
To-night 1 weep, and wonder if he hears.
Just now a star fell from the middle zone,
It flashed across the sky alone—alone.
’Tis gone with him into the vast unknown.
Ah, when shall God the mysteries disclose
That over Life and Death He wraps and throws ?
Those wan lips smile at me—he knows, he knows.
I stoop and listen—did I hear'hiin call?
The Silence answers me, my salt tears fall.
He is so still —that is the worst of all.
N.S.W.
AMBROSE PRATT.
A Western Garden.
Though dusty gray the skies all day,
At night none could be starrier,
And like a sweetheart’s face is this
Green garden on the Barrier.
The sun is king, and round the ring
Of vision all the verdure seems
Licked lifeless by his dragon tongue.
And shrivelled by his murder beams.
Around, about, the dust-storms rout,
Red legions hot and thunderful,
But turn your eyes and deem you dream,
It is so green and wonderful.
For in this close grows red the rose
And violets the tenderest,
And on the soothy velvet lawn
A shadow and a splendor rest.
The plains are spread with hillocks red,
Salt-bush and mirage mockeries ;
But here the drover halts to see
Pink peas in snow-white rockeries.
He stands awhile with sweetening smile
(Ah, long the track and pleasureless),
To him the violet and rose
Are beauty rare and measureless.
Perhaps (who knows ?) the droopy rose
Recalls an old love-verse again ;
And now, as in the long ago,
His cheek lies light on hers again.
Ah, well-a-day! His footsteps stray
Far hence from us who tarry here ;
May we, too, find, when desert-sick,
A garden on Life’s Barrier.
Poolamacca, N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
In the Dawn I Have Harked!
In the dawn I have listened to Earth as she sprang
from her couch of repose,
I have listened to founts of the Deep as they
shouted to clouds that enclose,
I have laughed with the jubilant Sun as he opened
his curtains of rose.
In the dusk I have nestled me down in the deep
of the bourgeoning trees,
And around me and over and under went lapping
the breath of the breeze,
And a million of murmurs of Faery floated from
fabulous leas.
In the night I have sung to the stars, and the stars
have been gracious to greet,
Have wafted me wings to my soul and have lent
me a light to my feet,
Have blended their infinite voices in melody mys-
tic and sweet.
I have stood in the halls and have harked to the
tale that is told by the Wise,
How Nature is all inarticulate, weaving her web
in disguise —
I have laughed at the lips of the sage and have
cried “ Ye are makers of lies ! ”
“ To the heart that can thrill she’s
the breast that is cold she is numb ;
To the soul that she loves and that loveth she
calls with a crystalline ‘ Come ! ’
To the eyes of the clod she is clay—to the ear of
the deaf she is dumb.”
Queensland,
P. LDYTIG.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 19, 1898.
The Rush for “Hoffis."
The sudden throwing-up of Premier Reid’s
Freetrade principles, and the list of new
duties which he recently sprung upon the
staggered Parliament of New South Wales,
has incited the Opposition leader to move the
old, familiar vote of censure. Cornstalk
politics consist very largely of votes of cen-
sure— George Reid, when leader of the
Opposition, moved thirteen or fourteen of
them in three years—and, according to all
good Cornstalk precedent, Edmund Barton
is doing the right thing. But it is a strange
thing, and a very weary and ridiculous one,
and it helps to make up a very curious situa-
tion all round. Mainly, it is a howling
parody on the system of Party Government,
which is a weird conundrum at the best of
times, while in days of political confusion it
becomes a nightmare. On the present occa-
sion Premier Reid is a strong supporter of
absolute Freetrade w'ho is proposing protec-
tive duties because he is solemnly pledged
not to raise the fiscal issue ; and also because
he wants extra revenue—which he has got
already and he got back at last election
to the place where he is able to propose new
duties by warning the -people that Barton
and Lyne would propose new duties if they
get there instead of lnm. And Barton and
Lyne, the Protectionists, call on the Foreign-
trade party to vote against Reid because lie
is imposing protective duties without believ-
ing in them, and to vote instead for the
party which believes in imposing more pro-
tective duties. And meanwhile R kid, who
is paid by the province for being a Foreign-
trade Premier, is drawing his salary under
false pretences.
♦ * * *
It is an understood lie in the system of
representative government that the Par-
liament rules. The present instance is one of
many hundreds which go to show that it
does nothing of the sort. Supposing there
is, as the Calico Jimmy press alleges, a
Foreign-trade majority in the New South
Wales Assembly, that majority obviously
doesn’t want Barton or Lyne as Premier.
Neither does it want Reid, since he has
turned revenue-tariffist and begun to apos-
tatise. So its natural course, as the majority,
would be to fire out its stout apostate, and
appoint in his stead a pure and spotless
Calico Jimmy person from its own side.
And it can’t do that ; it is as absolutely help-
less in the matter as if Parliamentary
Government didn’t exist. It can fire out
Reid certainly, and Reid, while he was
falling, and before his head struck
bottom, would be constitutionally bound to
advise the Governor to send for Barton to
form a Ministry. When Barton formed his
Ministry the Foreign trade majority could
refuse to support him, and might contrive
to leave him in a serious difficulty. Then
he would obtain a dissolution, and going to
the country with the prestige of victory and
newly-acquired office he would probably
rout the Shoddy party and come back with
a good working majority. The workings of
the Party system leave the Assembly to
choose between Reid and Barton. If it
doesn’t like Retd because he is now r a
partial dishonest Protectionist, then it can
take Barton because he is a whole honest
one.
* * * *
If there was any logic in the political sys-
tem the House would either reject Reid’s
proposals and keep him on, or else it would
reject Reid himself. The Protectionists and
the absolute Freetraders and the Land
Nationalisers and Single-taxers would all be
against him for totally different reasons.
Then the House would proceed to elect a
new Premier, each section putting up its
own candidate, and a good many candidates
putting themselves up. By dropping out
the most hopeless candidates and holding
fresh ballots, the applicants for the billet
would be reduced at last to two—the nominee
of the Opposition, probably its present
leader, and the one whom the present Minis-
terial party had put up as a reliable Foreign-
trader, vice G. Reid, fallen into evil courses.
Under such a system, the Ministerial party
could eject its leader, if he went back on the
principles upon which he was appointed,
without ejecting itself at the same time ; as
matters stand, it can’t. Reid, the pure
Freetrader, can levy almost any duties he
pleases, and his party will follow him as a
matter of practical necessity, partly because
it might lose its seat and its salary in the
confusion if it didn’t, and partly because the
alternative is admitting a party which also
believes in putting on duties. It is a sad and
grovelsome position for any party to be in,
but Premier Reid has got it there, and is
sitting on it.
V * * •
For its own part, this paper regards the
New South Wales vote of censure with very
little interest. It was bad and miserable
policy to stop the progress of the Federal
resolutions by any party row at the present
time. Even if Premier Reid put 40 per
cent, duties on everything, and broke up the
last shred of the compact that the fiscal issue
should not be raised till Federation was com-
plete, still it should be remembered that
every party shindy helps to postpone
the making of the Commonwealth, and all
Australia is waiting till New South Wales
gets out of the way. Also, if Barton gets
in he can’t introduce a protective tariff —his
promise to sink the fiscal question pending
Federation stands in the way. But if Reid,
to whom promises don’t matter, is willing to
give New South Wales partial Protection
and take the obloquy of the break of faith,
why shouldn’t Barton take the Protection
and let Reid have the obloquy ? Just now,
when the fiscal issue is supposed to be sunk
in the Federal interest, is the worst possible
time for raising the old, bitter spectre from
its grave. If Federation is really close at
hand, then it isn’t worth while to grab at
office for the little time that remains
before the inevitable re-arrangement comes
to pass. If it doesn’t come—if it is
again wrecked by the treachery of the
Reid-Want combination, then the Barton
party could reach out for office on a big
national question upon the ground that
New South Wales needed a sincerely
Federal Ministry above all things. To
get office on the fiscal issue just now might
simply split Barton’s Federal party into
fragments. It is reasonably solid on the
Federal question ; on the question of im-
ported shoddy v. national industries it is
divided into two camps. The fiscal subject
is just the one subject such a party shouldn’t
interfere with until it (the party) is no longer
wanted for Federal purposes. The wiser
course would have been to take no official
notice of Reid’s apostacy at present ; to let
every man in the party vote as he thought best
on the new financial proposals when they
came before the House, and to leave Reid
sitting on the neck of his own party undis-
turbed. If it burst with the pressure, then
the Barton party could enjoy the explosion
and stand by.
• * * * * *
Supposing the vote of censure is carried,
and Barton -gets into office with the fiscal
ghost aroused and in full working-order—
what then ? Barton, as leader of the Op-
position, is eloquent enough. Barton, as
Chief Justice of Australia or as xAttorney-
General to the Federal Government, would
probably be about the best man obtainable.
Barton, as the one indomitable enthusiast
of the Federal movement—the man above all
others who prevented it dropping into oblivion
—was magnificent. Barton as Premier a year
or two ago, to prevent the shameful treachery
which wrecked the last attempt at union,
would have been invaluable. But now the
fiasco is ancient history. The new Federal
proposals have been carried, so far as the
Assembly is concerned, and though they are
a miserably inadequate and undesirable set
of propositions Barton has so far acquiesced
in them that he could hardly withdraw them
and substitute a complete new set. There is
no reason to suppose that the Federal
apostle is a financier who ivould be of much
use in straightening out the bankrupt
finances of New South Wales. He isn’t a
patient man of infinite detail like the Vic-
torian Turner, who would be of great value
in putting right the crooked ways of the
administration. Barton taking office now
as the head of a Protectionist party which
is pledged to leave the tariff alone, getting
in on the fiscal issue which is supposed to be
sunk, tackling details for which he is pro-
bably almost as unfitted as Reid himself,
and taking over Reid’s half-finished and
more than half-hearted Federal scheme,
which would do him no credit, yet which has
been accepted by such strong majorities that
it could hardly be rescinded or materially
amended by the present Parliament, would
be a man in a false position. In fact
he would be a man in several false
positions at once. If Barton could
get office on the ground that Reid is a luke-
warm and bogus Federalist, a trickster with
provincial sympathies in his inmost soul ; a
man who keeps the avowed anti-Federalist,
Want, in his Cabinet as a guarantee of bad
faith ; then, in the interests of Australia, he
should reach for office with both hands and all
his feet. Reid deserves extinction on all
these counts. But to get office on any side-
issue just now is very dubious policy.
Carruthers' Land Bank.
The N.S.W. Government is inclined to follow the
example of Maoriland, Victoria, Westralia and
S.A. by starting a State Land Bank, and Minister
Carruthers has brought forward the harmless
necessary Bill for the purpose. This valuable
idea was one of the reforms promised, or half-
promised, when the Reid Government took office
in ’94, so the news has been broken to the country
gradually, and all sudden and violent shock has
been carefully avoided. The new institution is to
be clumsily described as the “Advances to Settlers
Board ; ” there is a curious tendency in Austra-
lasia to avoid all mention of a State Bank, and to
draw a thick veil of apology and circumlocution
over an institution which requires no apology
whatever. No province, barring South Australia,
seems inclined to acknowledge straight out that it
owns a State Land Bank, and there is a general
inclination to conceal the establishment under a
bushel. The Reid Government starts its bank-
ing scheme with one great advantage. It has the
experience of four other provinces to guide it; the
business has got past its experimental stage in
Australasia, and it is only necessary to adopt the
good features of the four State Land Banks
already in existence, and reject the bad ones. And
yet Carruthers’ Bill is a very half-hearted affair,
and seems calculated to do as little good as pos-
sible. It is practically a copy of the Westralian
Agricultural Bank—the smallest and most re-
stricted thing in its line up to date.
* * * * .
The measure provides for the borrowing of not
more than £3,000,000, by instalments not exceed-
ing £1,500,000 per annum, at a rate not to exceed
3 per cent. This restriction, of course, matters
very little; the Act can be expanded later on as
occasion requires. The money thus raised is to be
lent out at rates not exceeding 5 per cent., so the
State has a reliable margin of 2 per cent, if
necessary. One-tenth of the interest, equal to 10s.
per cent, per annum or thereabouts on all
the money lent, is to go to an assurance fund to
cover possible losses—a most valuable provision,
which most of the other provinces seem to have
overlooked. And the N.S.W. scheme, like all the
others, provides for the repayment of loans by
regular instalments. The amount advanced on
each security is therefore steadily reduced, and if
a slump in property brings down the value of the
security to any serious extent, and grievously re-
duces the original margin, there is always a pro-
bability that, -by the time the slump arrives, the
loan has been so far diminished that the State
Bank is still amply secured. The chance of loss
by depreciation of property is not half or a quarter
so great where the loan is repayable by regular
instalments as where it falls due in one sum at a
far-off date. Also, the Carruthers scheme pro-
vides that the money received in repayments may
be. lent out again, whereas one at least of the
existing State Banks is worked on the purely
idiotic proviso that the State borrows money on
bonds to lend to farmers, and all money received
back from the agriculturist must be used in repay-
ing these bonds, while the State Bank sells new
bonds to raise more money to lend to other farmers.
The good points in Minister Carruthers’ scheme
end about here, and the bad ones begin.
* * ♦ *
It is provided, in the first place, that the State
institution shall lend no money at all on town and
suburban lands. Why it shouldn’t lend money on
town and suburban lands passes comprehension.
The first object of the measure is certainly to en-
courage agriculture, and the agriculturist needs
assistance much more than the city owner, seeing
that it is the habit of private lenders to charge
him from 7 to 12 per shent, while the city
man can easily get money at six. But the
State can get money enough to do both classes
of business, and so long as it supplies cheap
loans to the farmer there is no reason why it
shouldn’t get whatever profits there are in the
other business also. The State wants all the
profits it oan get, so that it can lend them
out again, and still further enlarge its
business. If the city owner borrows money from
some foreign money-lending institution at 6 per
cent, where the State could lend it to him
at 5, and even then have a profit of, say,
\ per cent., it means that the I 4 per cent, dif-
ference is so much wealth going out of the country,
and whether the money that goes needlessly out
of the country belonged to the man in Sydney, or
the man at Bourke, or Milparinka, the loss is a
loss to the country at large just.' the same. The
first object of a State Land Bank should be to help
the agricultural and pastoral industries with cheap
money. Its other objects should be to help all
other industries with cheap money ; to extinguish
the private usurer—the foi’eign one especially ; to
help the city artisan to own the dwelling he occu-
pies ; and to do the biggest business it can, pro-
vided that business is sound and remunerative.
The obvious reason for these ambitions is that it
wants to make large profits for the State, and pay
off, out of these profits, the borrowed money it
started with, whereby the country may ultimately
own its State Land Bank as an unencumbered
asset of immense value.
* * * #
Then it is further provided that no loan shall
exceed £2OOO, which knocks another large slab off
the usefulness of the prospective bank. Hie first
object being to aid the small settler, who most needs
assistance, it would be quite fair and legitimate to
reverse the usual order of things by making loans
under <£2ooo at less interest than loans above that
amount. But it is quite possible to get money
enough for both classes of business, and it is
eminently advisable to make money for the State
out of the big land-holder, and also to get a hen
on his property, so that when it is foreclosed for
non-payment the State will get the land and cut
it up into small holdings. There is no individual
whom the State should yearn to foreclose upon
more than the big land-holder, and no more legiti-
mate person for the State to make profits out of.
Provided his security is good enough, and he is
charged an extra 10s. or 15s. per cent, on the
ground that he is a social evil, and further pro-
viding that, if funds happen to be short, the small
borrower always gets precedence, the big land-
holder should have money lent to him liberally, in
the sure and certain hope that some of him, at all
events, won’t be able to pay it back, which will
lead to foreclosure, and the casting out of the
social evil on its ear.
* * * *
Finally, the Carruthers scheme provides that
“every advance shall be for the purpose of
enabling the mortgagor to effect or pay for per-
manent improvements on the security, ” and if this
clause is strictly interpreted, it knocks about 50
per cent, more off the usefulness of the scheme.
By confining the advances principally to new
settlers who are starting to make improvements,
it may be possible to create a. sensational adver-
tising boom in settlement. But the men who want
assistance most are the old mortgaged crowd—the
individuals who are tottering under liabilities in-
curred at 10 per cent, or so, and who want cheap
money, not to make improvements, but to pay off
the private usurer. If all these are to be left in
the ditch, comparatively little good will result. It
is quite as essential to keep the old agriculturist on
the land as to put the new one there, though
it may not look so loud and remarkable
in the State Statistics. In fact, it is often more
essential to look after the old specimen, for his
downfall means that the best lands in the pro-
vince—the accessible lands that were first picked
in the rush of settlement —are going into the
hands of the banks and the foreign loan and mort-
gage companies. And why the State Bank should
refuse to assist him, and to make good profits out
of the business, passes all comprehension. If the
old settler is to be thrown overboard and advances
practically confined to the new one whose improve-
ments are still to make, it means less business,
much less profit for the State, and much less
saving of interest burdens to the country at large.
Also, it means that the interest charged must be
greater, because a small business can’t be run so
economically as a big one. It further signifies
that instead of the lands of the old settler, if
he does go down under his difficulties, revert-
ing to the State, the banks and other private
mortgage companies will get them in the same bad
old way as heretofore. Carruthers’ State Land
Bank scheme is a distinct move in the right, direc-
tion, but if there was less anxiety to avoid, dis-
turbing the vested interests of the present private
mortgagee it would be a much larger move.
* W * *
The Bulletin respectfully submits the follow-
ing as the basis of a State Land Bank: —
(1) That the two existing Savings Banks be united
under State management.
(2) The State Bank thus formed to be the sole bank of
issue in N.S.W. That province uses about £1,200,000
worth of notes, but assuming the issue dropped to
£1,000,000 and that £200,000 in gold (quite sufficient with
a Government guarantee behind the issue) was held
against it, this would give £BOO,OOO for the Land Bank to
start operations with. If it paid the Treasury the same
note-tax as the private banks do, the £BOO,OOO would cost
less than 3 per cent. The State notes to he legal tender
except at the head office of the State Bank. The notes
to be kept in circulation, as in Canada, by compelling
every bank to hold a proportion (in Canada it is 40 per
cent.) of its local cash-reserves in State, notes.
(3) The State Land Bank to lend up to, say, 50 or 60
per cent, of the value on country, suburban, or town pro-
perty indiscriminately. No limitation as to amount of
loan, but an extra 10s. per cent, to be charged on loans
above £2OOO, and an extra 15s. per cent, on loans above
£lO,OOO. Ten per cent, of the interest and all this ad-
ditional interest and any other profits to go to the reserve
fund. In case of a scarcit3 r of money small country loans
always to have preference. Interest on small loans to be
kept down, if possible, to 4J per cent.
(4) The State Land Bank to have unlimited powers of
raising money locally for loan purposes at 3 or, if neces-
sary, 3J per cent. Accounts to be audited and securities
valued annually by State officials. The Government to
have power, in times of panic, to make State Bank notes
legal tender for (say) three months without waiting for
the assent of Parliament.
Also The Bulletin suggests that the institution
be called the State Bank of N.S.W. —not the
Advances to Settlers Board, or any other apology
or.circumlocution whatsoever.
Barton’s Buckley’s Show.
Editor Bulletin. —N.S.W.. Premier Reid has
swallowed his Freetrade principles, reversed his
Freetrade policy, and imposed a semi-Protec-
tionist revenue tariff. And Opposition-leader
Federal-leader Barton has moved to censure the
Government on the ground that it is pledged to
keep the Federal movement in the front, and until
Federation is settled —MAiUN’s-way or otherwise—
not to cause delay or attack unanimity by tariff-
tinkering. And Barton has Buckley’s show of
passing nis censure-vote.
* * * *
Reid is a politician, but not a statesman;
Barton is a statesman, but not a politician.
Reid looks first to himself ; Barton looks first to
his country. Reid has the things he calls his
principles, and if you don’t like them they can be
altered ; Barton has the ideals that are his prin-
ciples, and they can’t be altered unless you alter
primary facts of history and geography, honesty
and integrity. Before the country, when there is
question of a great national cause unmangled by the
small-constituency vote, Barton wins easily; before
the small-constituency, when there is question of
nobbling publicans, or of wheedling civil-servants,
or of bribing policemen, Reid scores nearly every
time. And, unluckily, N.S.W. Assembly reflects
the small-eonsti tuency, not the country. Nearly
every member is “on the make.” If he isn’t
actively “on the make” he is passively “on the
keep ” ; if he doesn’t want billets he wants to hold
his billet. How many men in N.S.W. Parliament
would —or could afford to —sacrifice a year’s salary
to get Federation to-morrow ? Not half-a-dozen.
How many Labor members would ? Name
one. In that crowd of self-seekers Barton
is as much out of place as an Apollo in a coal-


hole Half of his Parliamentary brethren can’t
un lerstand his ideas. The other half jeer at
tl, ..n If there are a few who understand and
respect, they follow only when Barton’s ideas are
in line with their personal interests. VV hat do
the constituents of the North-coast members—the
prominent constituents, the men who make or mar
elections —care about I ederation ? Sentimentally,
a lot or a little ; practically nothing. They want
sugar-duties; and Reid is maintaining the sugar-
duties. Plainly, if a North-coast member votes
against Reid, “and in favor of a man who insists
on putting Federation first—that I ederation
which may wipe out sugar-duties, and which, if
Queensland comes in, may wipe out the N.S.W.
cane-sugar industry altogether lie risks losing his
billet. And the North-coast member realises that
Federation may be a very grand, high-sounding
thing, but all the same he’ll feel mighty hollow if
he loses his billet —his three hundred a year and
perks, and his chance of fat committee-pickings.
So Barton will go down, in spite of his fine
cause and his eloquence. What he has. to
do is to convince every mercenary man-jack
in Parliament that Federation will pay him —pay
the man-jack; that he will make six hundred a
year under Federation, and his seat will be surer
than ever. Well, Barton can’t.
*** • . .
Barton thinks politics are a matter of princi-
ples ; Reid knows they’re a question of pockets.
Why, even when Barton was out of a seat, and
wanted one badly, he wouldn’t promise anything
in return to the man who might resign in his
favor—not even a seat in the Council. Reid
would have promised everything. What chance
has Barton against Reid, in the atmosphere of
N.S.W. Assembly? Not a possible.
* * * *
Not a possible—unless he drops Federation-in-
earnest, and adopts the Reid method of using
Federation as a stalking-horse. For Federation
means death to three-fourths of the petty interests
that govern N.S.W. Assembly ; and well the petty
interests know it. There was some sense in Free-
trade v. Protection. That was a controversy which
held pickings for both sides. Barton the Pro-
tectionist might easily beat Reid the Freetrader.
But Barton the Federationist has not the least
show of beating Reid the Freetrading Protec-
tionist, holding out bribes to “ both your houses.”
What is there in this Federation ? Barton can
only say that it is noble, glorious, and so
on. Empty phrases; air; —and your Assem-
bly-man wants cash. Or he can say it
is necessary to Australia’s safety. H’m ! is there
any immediate risk to the carcase of Tom J ones,
M.P. for Bungaboola? Or he can say it is finan-
cially profitable? “To whom? to me?” asks
J ones. Oh, no; to the other fellow; to the
nation ; to the general assembly of three-million-
odd Joneses. And Jones turns sadly away;
“the nation” is too vague—he can’t afford to
sacrifice, or risk sacrificing, the value of the billet
in his hand for the bird in the nation’s bush.
And that is the dead wall against which your pure
Federationist drops beaten every time.
* * * *
After the last N.S.W. general election there
was a Parliamentary feeling that, after all, it
might pay to support Federation a little bit,
really , as well as spout for it. That feeling is
dying—dead. The echo of the popular voice has
been lost in the distance; and N.S.W. Assembly
is back again on the old basis, “ What am
I going to get out of it ? ” Reid answers
clearly, “This—and this —and this ; the rehabili-
tation of your character; the Exhibition com-
mission ership; the next vacancy on the Works
Committee.” That is straight and above-board :
there is some sense, some pleasure, in voting for
Reid. But Barton —-“ I can’t say that you
personally are going to get anything out of it; but
it’s the right thing to do ; it’ll profit the country
and posterity.” Pooh! Barton’s a fool. So
Barton goes down.
* * ♦ *
Any present hope of improvement ? None.
B.B.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Q. N. Bank.
The Queensland National Bank cases came to
an end so suddenly that the directors were all
discharged without a blot on their joint and
several characters almost before the trial had
well started. There were also certain charges
against the auditors, but they were dropped,
and the proceedings ended. Considering that
the Dickson Government totters very much on
its throne, it is better for that politieally-smell-
ful aggregation that the Q.N.B. crowd have
been tried and acquitted in a calm and friendly
manner now, thus ending the matter, than
that a hostile Government should try them
in a very un-calm and unfriendly manner
—later on. Poor old M'llwraith, the loud
and overbearing, who was going to get to
Queensland and overwhelm his enemies even if
he had to be carried on board ship, never
turned up to throw any light on the
proceedings. Palmer is dead, and ex-
Manager Drury is at least officially dead,
and it was generally assumed that what-
ever was very wrong was done by Drury,
the deader of the pair. The case was a
woefully unsatisfactory one all through. There
seems to be no doubt whatever that
when the balance-sheet declared that tlie
paid-up capital was .£BOO,OOO it grossly de-
ceived the shareholders and the public;
they naturally thought it meant that
160,000 shares had been sold at £5 each and
the money actually received, whereas a lot of
the shares were held by the bank itself, and the
money they represented had never been got in
at all. Also, they were absolutely misled
about the reserve fund ; they naturally thought
it was all actual cash, whereas part of it
was made up by the bank taking up its
own shares at a premium and crediting the
premium to reserve. In other words, a good
slice of the bank’s alleged capital and reserve
was, apparently, only the bank’s own 1.0. U.,
given by it to itself. Also, a lot of the alleged
profits were made by charging interest that was
never received on debts that were lost; and
part of the apparent public confidence in the
bank, as evidenced by the demand for its
shares and the price they kept up to, was
caused (unknown to the public, which went in
and invested its money on the strength of that
apparent confidence) by the bank buying up its
own shares. The security of the £lBOO,OOO
uncalled capital was partly bogus, seeing that
a portion of it was simply the money that the
bank was liable to pay to itself, if it called on
itself to do so. But the idiotic banking law of
Queensland, and of other provinces besides,
is built for the express benefit of shady direc-
tors or directors whose incapacity verges on im-
becility. It says nothing against the prac-
tice of reckoning the unrecoverable interest on
dead accounts as profit, or against many oilier
things, and it fixes no definite responsibility
anywhere. The community is too tired in its
in most bones to insist on anew ban king law, which
will make the Q.N.B. variety of practices illegal,
and hold the directors and manager responsible
for them on the sufficient ground that, if they
didn’t know, they ought to. So the only charge
that can be made against the average awful
board is that it “conspired” to do things
“ with intent to deceive or defraud the share-
holders.” Therefore the board is practically
free from all responsibility. It never, or hardly
ever, does conspire to do anything wrong ; it
simply drifts, or the things happen, or it does
them or allows them to be done, without
formally conspiring. And it never in-
tends to defraud the shareholders ; it only
intends to postpone the row as long as possible,
with a placid hope that some incredible
boom will put things right in the interval. Of
course, the shareholders are deceived more or
less, and some of them are very glad to be de-
ceived. The deceived person who sold out bis
Q.N.B. shares in ’9l at £lO, for instance, would
have gob nothing for them if lie and the rest
had been undeceived, and be is very glad be
wasn’t. But the board doesn’t conspire to do
it; even if every director on the board suspects
that the bank is tottering, he never mentions
the painful subject to any other director, and
there is no formal agreement to smother the
subject. Subjects like that tacitly smother
themselves. And the auditors don’t conspire,
either; they only say quite truly that every-
thing is right “according to the books”—how
wrong the books may lie is another matter
about which nothing is said. Until the duties
of directors, managers, and auditors are rigidly
defined by law, it is very little good trying
any of them for anything.
The Adjournment Swindle.
For several weeks past Vic. Leg. Council has
played a funny little game of adjournment
with the One Man One Vote Bill. The 48 fat
landlords didn’t boldly admit on this occasion
that they would rather die of apoplexy than
consent to the abolition of plural voting. As
soon as the Bill came along from the Assembly
they voted for a fortnight’s adjournment to
enable Sargood to “modify” it. Sargood’s
modification scheme was to be based on the
ratepayers’ roll, and one or two further adjourn-
ments were worked ere that sapient softgoods-
man found the statistics he wanted, which
statistics can be seen any day in a dozen pub-
lications. At last he bad the statistics, and
when he got them the Council made a pretence
of considering whether Proputty interests could
be safeguarded by an arrangement for giving
one extra vote to all ratepayers. Sargood bad
not troubled to put bis proposal in a
practical form, as though he really meant
business in the matter. Probably he saw
at a glance that the proposal was ridiculous.
He brought it forward in a solemn tone of voice,
and his brethren tattled drearily on the subject
for a few hours, after which they fell back on
some more adjournment. When the “ debate”
was resumed, Sargood calmly threw aside
his bogus suggestion of two votes for rate-
payers, and asked the muddled old saviours of
the country to adjourn again, in order that
Henry Wrixon might prepare his little scheme
for wasting time. The 48 Rich Landlords
adjourned again—with enthusiasm. Wrixon
is on the “proportional-representation” lay,
which has nothing to do with ratepayers or
proputty. After a long, weary delay he came
forward wilh his scheme, which includes an
idea for converting all the metropolitan dis-
tricts into one huge constituency with twenty
or thirty members, and leaving the rest
of the country in one or two member
electorates as heretofore. The Council heard
Wrixon’s exposition of his new idea, which is
only a very old idea revived. It is so old,
indeed, and so well-known that the ad-
journment while Wrixon thought it out was
utterly absurd ; all the details of it were
worked out years and years ago. Then when
Wrixon brought up bis ancient idea, the
Council said it was so new and startling that
it was necessary to adjourn for a long time to
think over it, and the Council adjourned some
more. It is still adjourned at time of writing.
When it has finished being adjourned, it will
probably either reject Wrixon’s idea, or that
idea will be withdrawn, or more likely there
will be another adjournment. The adjourn-
ment swindle is easier than absolute rejection,
and is quite as good a device for leaving things
as they were.
The Teaching's of Panton, F.M.
Mr. Panton, professional “ beak” of Melb. City
Court, lately brought the proverbial hornet’s
nest about his ears by denouncing Cole’s Book
Arcade as a curse to the human race. The
great evil of the famous Melbourne institution,
from a Pantonic point of view, is in the mana-
gerial assumption that men and women can be
invited to handle books without being incited
to steal them. Cole’s Arcade is the Liberty
Hall of Literature, where beggar or chooser
may get rest for the body, food for the mind,
and music for the soul free of charge. It com-
bines the happiest characteristics of the shop
and the public library, and the attraction of
chamber music is thrown in. All sorts and
conditions of local- residents, strangers and
pilgrims, speak of the place with gratitude. A
humanising place, conducted as much for the
pleasure as for the profit of the proprietor
and his busy wife, is Cole’s Book Arcade.
But Mr. Panton calls it a manufactory
of thieves, or something to that effect. He
alleges that the opportunity to “ shake ” a
book from a shelf or stall causes books to be
shaken, and he never entertains the thought
that Cole’s Arcade also serves to inure needy
folk to temptation and turn mean ones from
meanness by its influence. There is honesty in
the atmosphere of this free, easy, and accessible
book-store. Cole’s stock, considering its size,
and the number of people who roam about the
premises, is possibly the least thefted stock of its
kind in the whole wide world. Certainly, there
is no justification for Mr. Panton’s statement
that the Arcade “ makes thieves,” and he,
being a man of intelligence, could offer lmt one
excuse for having uttered it. Here followeth
the only visible excuse. The custom of every
Melbourne official, since the land-boom went
bung, and “respectable” swindling was un-
masked in many places, lias been to speak of
robbery as the natural outcome of temptation.
“ Everybody feli,” in a cant Collins-street way
of speaking, therefore it was not to be thought
that anyone should have preserved his upright-
ness. Men were dishonest (cried politicians,
parsons, lawyers, and even Judges), because
“ the fever” took hold of them, and the banks
offered them the opportunity to speculate above
their means. The poor fellows were more to
be pitied than blamed, &c. Now, what Mr.
Panton says of Cole’s Book Arcade is merely
an echo of what everybody said about the
Boom. Melbourne is the paradise of the im-
mortal doctrinaire.

Bad Drugs and Bad Druggists.
“ L.R.C.P.” on the impure-drug question :
While agreeing with “ Crotonis Oleum ” as to necessity
for drug-inspection, let me deal with his opinion that
“ medical men should not he allowed to compound their
own prescriptions, because there is no check on blunder-
ing, which is something more than possible with men
who have had, at most, only six mouths’ training in
pharmacy.” A qualified medico will have passed
examinations in practical and theoretical chemistry,
materia medico, —including medical botany; strength,
compatibility, dose, action, uses, and preparation of
drugs; and will have gone through a three to six months’
course of dispensing. With such training anyone not a
fool could dispense accurately, and the directions given
by the pharmacopoeia are so plain and unambiguous
that even an indifferently-educated man can acquire the
essentials of the art. The attainment, of facility and
quickness, of course, needs practice ; but of this the dis-
pensing doctor gets plenty. Also, “O.O.” would have
the doctor, when there is no pharmacist, procure ready-
made-up medicines from a chemist and simply label them.
Utterly opposed to the scientific method of using drugs.
No real doctor believes in stock mixtures. The principle
is bad and unscientific, and as no two cases of even
the same complaint are alike a corresponding varia-
tion is required in the medicine exhibited. The man who
gives a stock mixture is but a step above the quack with
his “ cure-all.” “C. 0.” again would restrict the pro-
fessional part of dispensing to “ Doctors” or “ Masters”
of Pharmacy, who should have undergone a post-graduate
course of two years and have been at least four years in
practice. Bui men who need six years to acquire
the simple art of pharmacy should take to carrying a hod.
The present state of things is certainly not satisfactory,
since the embryo chemist actually commences to make up
prescriptions before passing any examination in pharmacy
and materia medical We have here no guarantee that
the youth knows any pharmacy, and no safeguard against
his blundering, for tire chemist who employs him cannot
minutely supervise every pill or potion he “ makes up.”
From personal knowledge I say that such persons have
simply to blunder into a knowledge of pharmacy. Also,
as a doctor’s reputation depends largely on the result of
his medicines, it follows that his best interest lies in
using the best drugs, but though chemists, as a body,
are as honest and trustworthy as any other class, there
are some who habitually keep inferior drugs ; and who
being short of one ingredient in a prescription will sub-
stitute another; who will themselves prescribe whenever
a chance offers ; who, ignorant of auscultation, will use a
stethoscope—and so one will give an aperient for intesti-
nal obstruction, or plaister up a scalp wound incognisant
of a depressed fracture accompanying it. To partly
protect, himself against charlatanism of this kind, and
to ensure that the right drugs of the right quality go into
his medicines, the medical man has a perfect right to
dispense, and my personal experience has shown me that
it is seldom or never the doctor who makes a preseriptional
blunder.
Staying Out in the Wet.
The four dozen weary personages who form
Victoria’s Leg. Council are apparently going to
again throw out the Exported Products Bill as
a socialistic innovation and a horrid interfer-
ence with the liberty of the subject. Queens-
land, where the Government is also bitterly
Tory, hut which knows occasionally when to
come in out of the wet, has adopted this same
idea, and so, in various forms and degrees, have
Sweden, France, Belgium, Denmark, and Ger-
many. It has been found by sad experience
that the British market is very sensitive.
When a few low, mean democratic cockies send
inferior produce, it is simply published that
Victorian produce is off-color this season, and
prices go down. Then the large, bloated capi-
talist, who generally exports good stuff, be-
cause lie can afford to buy the best appliances,
is a sufferer. Of course, the whole province suf-
fers, but the large, rich exporter suffers most,
because lie has the largest part of the trade. To
obviate this, and get up such a reputation for
Vic. exports that they will command top price
in the British market, and thus bring the
largest possible amount of loot to the Vic. Fat
Man, it is proposed that all produce should be
examined before exportation, and only the best
kind be allowed to go out. The result would
be that the worst kind would presently cease
to be manufactured, because the principal out-
let would be closed up. But Vic. Council can’t
understand, by reason of the cotton in its ear,
that this isn’taSocialistic innovation, but a plain
business idea that is already in good working-
order in many civilised countries. And it is so
shocked at the idea of interfering with the
Liberty of the Subject that the small, ignorant
cockie —who, by reason of his poverty and
ignorance, is really the worst offender—will
probably be allowed to go on damaging the big
exporter’s market for a while longer. And the
big exporter being such a great and notable
ass, it serves him right.

A Curious Committee.
This paper again has the pleasure of hearing
from Mr. Ashton, M.L.A. :
Dear Bulletin.— You die hard when you are after the
scalp of your mortal enemy. [Note : Bulletin’s defini-
tion of “ mortal enemy ” : “A man who is not a Protec-
tionist.”)
1. You ask: “ If writing one’s name on the top of a
document is sisrning it, then why was the petition in
question voted informal on account of bein': unsigned ?”
Answer : The petition was held to be informal because
the law requires that a petition shall be “ subscribed.”
For further illumination of this point see difference in
dictionary definitions between “ sign ’’.and “subscribe.”
2. That certain statements made in the Assembly were
“ not contradicted by Ashton ” is due to the fact that
“ Ashton ” declined (1) to discuss a case which was sub-
judice, or (2) to disclose the private proceedings of a
judicial committee. He did characterise Mr.. Crick’s
major statement as “ absolutely false,” and subsequently
—at the next committee meeting—got the chairman's
ruling to that effect.
If Mr. Crick ever turns Freetrader I shall submit to
The Bulletin a statement of my case against him. To
ask it to find against him under existing circumstances
would, I fear, be resented as an impertinent suggestion
to change the policy of the paper.—Yours, &c., Jas.
Ashton..
There are two or three points in the above
letter, all of them wrong :—(1) The Bulletin
doesn’t “die hard”; it lives easily and
doesn’t die at all. (2) Ashton, M.L.A.,
must have an old, cheap, deceptive dic-
tionary. According to Webster’s Interna-
tional, edition 1894, page 1339 (near bot-
tom of left-hand column), to “sign” is “to
subscribe in one own’s handwriting.” When a
man signs a thing he subscribes it, and the
man who says that a document was signed, but
wasn’t subscribed, is a whatsisname. But to
write your name on the top of a document, or
on the front of the envelope you enclose for
“ Tatte.rsall ” to send your sweep-ticket in, or
on vour visiting-card, isn’t signing it, or sub-
scribing it either. If it was, then you
couldn’t address a letter to another man without
committing forgery. (3) The Bulletin didn’t
go after Ashton’s scalp. It didn’t even men-
tion him in the little article that all the row is
about. It was he who came after the scalp of
his “ mortal enemy,” which seems to be this
paper. [Note.—Ashton’s definition of “ mortal
enemy ”: One who doesn’t always vote with
Geo. Reid.] (4) If Ashton declined to state
his case in the House because the matter was
sub judice , why is he doing it now? The
matter is still sub judice. And if he couldn’t
disclose the private proceedings of a judicial
committee then, why is he disclosing them
now ? (5) If Crick, the Protectionist, turns
Freetrader, Ashton says he will submit his
case against Crick to this paper. All right;
tliis paper will be glad to see the case in ques-
tion. But if Ashton, the Freetrader, votes
for the Reid Government’s new protective
duties, to whom will he submit that case?
Archer, for the Strathmerton murder, is to be
hanged at Melbourne on the 21st inst. The
crime imputed to him was a cruel and cowardly
one, and gain was its motive ; which was all
the more reason why accused, his life being
really and not only formally at stake, should
have had an absolutely careful and dispassionate
trial. Thus the Australasian :
Though the Benalla jury solvprl the problem so neatly,
the question was not one for them at all. The prisoner,
in the circumstances, was entitled to have his defence,
poor as it was, considered by the jury when their minds
were fresh and not at the end of a long and already over-
protracted sitting. How many men in a thousand selected
in the haphazard manner jurors are have the mental
capacity to listen all day on the strain to speeches and
evidence, every word of which may be important, and
then to resume work after a short adjournment for tea?
These jurors were expected, at a few minutes to nine, to
listen with close attention to the criticism which counsel
for the defence was going to offer on the statements
made' by a host of witnesses. “ You can do it in
seven werds,” said the .Judge. Did so astounding a
remark ever before this come from such a quarter?
The mildest thing to say about it is that it is itself
proof that the Court had already been sitting too long.
It was not a remark that Mr. Justice Hood would be
likely to make early in the day, when his faculties are in
a state of elastic freshness. This desire to sit late at Cir-
cuit Courts would be inexplicable did we not know that
there is a great deal of human nature in Judges. They
do not sit late from love of work. For when in town they
sometimes cannot be got to begin till nearly a quarter to
11, and they may sometimes be seen escapin': from the
Law Courts between 3 and 4. It came out before the Law
Commission that one Judge had a fashion of vanishing
from the Practice Court with the alertness of the stage
character who is on occasion required to disappear
through a wall. And, as has been pointed out, there are
actually no means of illuminating the metropolitan Law
Courts by night, though the Judges advised as to every
requisite. Assize business is treated, now and then, like
the climbing of stairs—work to be got over quickly,
because it is out of the routine.

Ex-Naval Officer :
“Onlooker” (8., 5 11/’9B) hasn’t got lashed up in his
’ammick all the knowledge on deck, and forgets that
derision is not confutation. My contention —that rein-
forcements of trained men and officers would he hard to
find by Britain after she had fought two winning sea-
fights—“O.” illogically meets by asserting that any
other Power would be in the same fix. And in any case
the statement is incorrect. Britain’s case is pecu-
liar since, with a navy nearly twice as large as
the French one, she has nearly 100 lieutenants and
sub-lieutenants fewer than France! And if “ O.”
knows what he's talking about, he must he aware
that the B. navy has been shamefully short of
sublieutenants ever since one of Gladstone’s Govt’s
initiated the “commutation ’’ retiring scheme, and that
“ subs.” are now luxuries beyond the reach of an ordinary
commander. Also, as regards the Terrible and Powerful,
“O.” is a little too previous, for the successes since
claimed had not come off at date of my former par.
Neither could the Welsh coal strike have affected the
Powerful’s case, when she doddered out to China,
for the simple reason that she started with bunkers
filled with Welsh coal. Perhaps, while “ O.” is on
the contract, he will kindly explain the Wanderer
and Boscawen smash; the Perseus and the Pactolus
breakdowns; the barely-averted Jupiter collision ; and
the ramming of a cruiser by a coal-tramp; all occurring
in'September last. And, to coil up, “O.’s” remarks
about Admiralty wisdom don’t in any way touch my
assertion that in some old ironclads, the ancient muzzle-
loaders of which are being replaced by modern guns, the
obsolete engines are retained; while in other cases in
which the useless guns are retained the Admiralty, in its
superhuman wisdom, has provided brand-new compound
engines.

“ Chewzer ” on country meat-inspection :
The Central Board of Health prescribed 12 hours’ no-
tice before killing in licensed yard, but the various local
slaughter-houses are, as a rule, situated far apart, one
man having to attend to them all; and as the several
butchers give the requisite notice simultaneously, and all
kill at about 5 or 6 p.m., what is the use of such a fool-
arrangement as this? The remedy is simple enough.
Let each town have a common abattoir, at which all
slaughtering must be done under the supervision of a
man who knows a cancer from a crow 1
Also, re milk :
Whilst the dairyman is being kept strictly to the law,
the big, fat publican (as an additional source of income)
keeps, in some cases, nearly as many cows as the former ;
but, as Bung is not a direct vendor of milk, he may be as
diseased in his cattle and as dirty in his dairy as he
pleases, to the obvious danger of his boarders, to say
nothing of his own family.

“ Shaker,” on the indispensableness of China-
men :
Some facts for J. A. Philp, (8.8/10/’9B). (1) The diggers
on Mt. Macdonald, near Georgetown, Q., are buying
splendid cabbages for 6d. each, irrown and hawked by
white men ; yet Georgetown is credited by Mr. Wraggk
with the Australian record for dryness of atmosphere.
(2) The statement that “ The Palmer would carry a
white population to-day—but for these slant-eyed
gleaners ” does not rest on the ipse dixit of Von KoT7.E,|but
is endorsed by (practically) every living digger who
worked on that field in the early da\ r s. (3) On the Gil-
bert and its tributaries, partly on account of reduced
living and partly by reason of increased stress in the
struggle for existence, ground is now being worked'by
whites which was abandoned by Chinamen, in the
seventies, as too poor. Also, if Chinese were en-
tirely removed now from “ abandoned ” gold-fields, all
those fields, at least in Queensland, would probably carry
a white population within a few years from the causes
above-mentioned.

A man was fined £5 in a Vic. township
recently for kissing a lady acquaintance. For
punching a woman and breaking two of her teeth
another man was fined lo.s. in a neighboring town
the other day. Moral for Man : Take the cheapest.
TO PLAINTIFF.
You sue the man who kissed you, and
Then take the cash with aspect meek ;
What of the scriptural command
That bids you turn the other cheek ?
Simplest—Most Durable—Best.
