The “ King of Crime,” a piece which isn’t
resurrected as often as it deserves, is on deck
for a week or two at Sydney Lyceum. The
Shirley-Landeclt drama is many miles above the
usual melo-dramatic level in various respects. It
is melo-drama pure and simple, with berlud and
crime, and midnight cadaver in it; but it is
artistic melo-drama, with a good story to tell and
a soul to tell it with. The boss criminal of Paris,
who has a happy home and an affectionate blind
wife in an adjacent village, and makes it a rule
not to murder anybody m that vicinity lest he
should disturb the home, is a fine character when
he isn’t played the way Charles Holloway
played him a year or two ago; he is also
a great lesson on the subject of laying to one’s
book. In his own circle, the boss murderer is
supposed to be something in the time-payment
bible-trade, and until he weakly gives way to the
robbery and butchery of an aged capitalist in his
own town, all goes well with him. After that, he
drifts downhill, and a profitable, old-established
business goes to pieces. He has hardly finished
murdering the old man next door and is getting
away with the £BJ) j when his blind wife comes in
and the assassin has to dodge her artfully round
the corpse by candle-light. The “King’s” best
friend, who happens to be eloping with the capi-
talist’s niece in the next room, is fixed on as the
murderer ; the blind woman, who has got things
mixed up while wandering round the dead body,
swears to him, and the usual French gendarme with
the red pants runs him in. Then the capitalist’s
son, who floated the company for the murder of his
Erogenitor in consideration of half the takings,
egins to reflect that if he had let the old man die
naturally he would have had all the takings, and
turns nasty, and requires to be partially strangled
in a cellar to keep him quiet. The “ King,” dis-
guised as the ’tec. who is hunting after himself,
has to interrogate his blind wife as to his own
whereabouts, and to knock his happy home finally
into ruins. The end is a pair of brown hands that
come out from the folds of a curtain and strangle
the capitalist’s son from behind (he can’t turn
round to see who has got him, but he swells up
visibly on a sofa and dies), and the dead body of
the “King” rolling down a flight of stairs and
bouncing off the last step.
* * * *
The “ King of Crime” is an evidence that
even the blood and butchery drama can be a work
of high art provided the man who composed the
remains knows his work. It is full of strong situa-
tions, and Diver, as the boss criminal, plays them
for all they are worth, except in one or two places
where there are signs of insufficient rehearsal.
The blind woman of Miss Burdett is quiet
and convincing, and Misses Alice May
and Marie D’Alton, Cosgrove, Fred Patey,
Harcourt and a few more do excellent
work. Miss May, known until lately as a
pretty and rather crude little actress of intermit-
tent appearances, shows a very remarkable im-
provement of late. The masked ball, into which
the dead body of the “King” bounces when it
rolls down the steps, is a bright piece of irrele-
vance and high-kicking held in the painted halls
of Sin, and the show should be good for an extra
week on the strength of it. But it would be better
—much better—if the King would lie still at the
foot of the stair when he is dead, instead of
struggling to his feet to cry out about his beloved
wife, and then dying over again. The great trou-
ble in melodrama is that the dead people won’t
keep d * id when they are dead.
** * *
The “ Red Robe ” will glare on the aching
eyes of Sydney till Friday night, and then the
emblazoned tog departs from Her Majesty’s stage,
and the “Laly of Lyons ” will supervene for six
nights only. The change has certain advantages
now that the nights are getting hot and gritty,
and the skeeter is abroad, and the locust sings its
strident comic song in the sun-baked tree. For
Claude lies pleasantly about a cool palace beside a
cool lake under the shade of a cold, white Alp,
and that lie is a refreshing cram on a Sydney sum-
mer night. And, on the other hand, Richelieu’s
tom ato skirt strikes one on the head like a portable
bush-fir ■ t rimmed with passementerie, and there
is no relief in it. The “Red Robe” departs with-
out throwing much additional light on the charac-
ter of the spurred and booted warrior-priest who
carried Louis XIII.’s king lorn on his back. The
drama doesn’t make him dark or forbidding or
secretive or especially strong, or anything else
that he is commonly understood to have been.
Probably he didn’t really look any of these things ;
the alert, cheerful, friendly gentleman in the red
frock that Gaston Mervale presents may be the
genuine article as it was iu the small, unwhole-
some flesh and the grim spirit a few centuries ago ;
only it takes some time to assimilate the idea.
Come to think of it, very few great men ever
looked as they ought to have done.
*# * *
A second careful overhaul of the “ Duchess
of Coolgardie, ” which is still une irthing big nug-
gets at Sydney Cri., under Dam pi t's m anagement,
makes this influential organ feel rather more
benevolent than it did at its first attempt. The
“ Duchess ” is a drama which grows on one, in its
own rough, picturesque baok-blox fashion. It
doesn’t say who the hero is, or what drove him to
drink too much bad rum in the West. It picks up
huge nuggets a good deal too recklessly —but then
in a gold-mining drama the leading character has
to pick up nuggets somewhat recklessly. Johnson
Weir and his gang have a rather free and easy
fashion of attempting wholesale butchery; even in
the days when the digger hung his hat promis-
cuously on the outcrop of pure gold W. A. was a
country where persons without political influence
couldn’t make arrangements for murdering other
persons on a large scale, in the presence of wit-
nesses, with impunity. But these things are mere
trifles, anyhow. The “Duchess” is adorned with
fine scenery ; the street in Coolgardie may or may
not be historically accurate, but it is a remarkably
fine piece of architecture, anyhow. And the
red-dusty aspect of the country, the sun-glare,
and the haze, and the tired vegetation which
looks like chucking it up, and the hungry trees,
and the general aspect of God-forgottenness are
all there. If the Dry Countree doesn’t look like
that it certainly ought to. There is about as
much plot as can be put into a drama which leads
a precarious life in tents and bark-public-houses
and on camel-back. The illegitimate infant is a
trifle out of place in a land where there is ob-
viously no milk or other proper sustenance for
it, but it seems to struggle through on tinned-dog
soup well enough, and the tame aboriginal takes
care of it and soothes it to sleep with corrobboree.
And the drama is much improved by the omission
of all details as to how the girl came to have that
infant; it just transpires in conversation that she
was betrayed before the story opened, in parts un-
known and under circumstances not worth men-
tioning, by a captain of no account with an eye-
glass. The arrangements made in Act V. for
scragging that captain (who is presumably hung in
his eyeglass) are very comforting.
* X -* *
At Sydney Palace the business is as large
and the mystery of Dante’s show as murky as it
was a month ago. Possibly there is a colder smile
on the skull of the burnt woman, and a more bitter
wink in its empty eye ; but these are mere specu-
lations. The man who is publicly sealed up in a
bag and locked and corded in a box, which is then
put in another box and fastened with ropes and
chains, changes places with the man who was
outside in a second or two less time than
he did the first night; it took eight seconds
at first, and he can now get out and put
the other man in his place in about six.
The public has pretty well given up trying to find
out liow most of Dante’s tricks are done; what
the feminine part of it would like to know is
whether that nice young man’s beautiful hair curls
naturally, or whether he puts it in papers at
night, or whether it is done with the tongs. If
Dante will call and explain this point, the writer
will be only too miserable to see him, and if he
knows when the visit may be expected will make
it a point to be out. Experience has taught him
that the curly artist whose hair has been aspersed
is often difficult to reason with.
* * * *
“ Bob,” a comedy drama new to Sydney,
is to be the second production of Miss Maggie
Moore’s season at Sydney Royal. The “Prodigal
Father” has done profitable business in its two
weeks’ season, and so far Miss Moore appears to
have struck a much better grassed patch of luck
than she did in her last visit to Sydney. For a
favorite of her calibre the bareness of that last
patch was something unusual.
< * * * *
The usual long list of names, mostly those
of nice girls called Flossie and Cora and Celia,
decorates the Sydney Tivoli advt., but there is no
overwhelming star at Rickards’ show just at pre-
sent. The Alhambra appears to anchor itself
principally on Ring wood, the banjo soloist. The
Bulletin has a vague idea that a small show has
dropped out of the list of amusements somewhere
this week, or last week, or recently. It can’t exactly
locate the missing entertainment, but it has the
ghostly impression of something being lost that
one feels when he walks along the principal street
in a dream, just before he discovers, in his dream,
that he has forgotten to put his clothes on. But
the impression is too hazy to be worth investiga-
tion.
* * * *
Melb. Royal was packed again on Satur-
day night, and the sale of Walter Baker’s goods
and chattels at the “ dear old home ” will be con-
tinued for another week or two. The scene of
that, festive auction is a really splendid thing.
Every man, woman, quadruped, and vehicle con-
cerned in it gives valuable assistance. Of all the
dear old homes that Baker has gambled away
during, his long connection with the Bland Holt
co. this is the most picturesque property, and
when Ladylove wins it all back for him in a canter
the audience would like to see him make a trium-
phant return at the head of his faithful army of
retainers and other live stock.
* * * •
Talking of Ladylove, one regrets to notice
that “ Sporting Life ” is absurdly incorrect in turf
details,’ like many other racing dramas. The
“ weighing-out ” enclosure is an impossible ar-
rangement, and the victorious filly carries 9st. 51b.;
also, after the race, the horses line-up at the spot
where Walter has just been rescued from a gipsy
caravan, and all the traditions of the Epsom
course are ruthlessly outraged. Nevertheless, for
the sake of Woodstock Hall under the hammer,
not to mention lfi other scenes and the efforts of
the co., Bland Holt’s entertainment deserves all
the success it is getting.
* * * *
Likewise, the prosperity of “ The Liars,”
at the Bijou, has been fairly earned. The talk in
H. A. .lories's play is bright and crisp, the charac-
ters well contrasted, the mechanism of the story
ingenious. . It is a most worldly play, to be sure.
Brough, as the preacher of wise saws and modern
Jtakes hold' of the situation at the finish,
and puts matters in the way that Mother Grundy
would have them go, and the audience is
expected to rejoice over the disappointment,
of the one earnest sinner in the show. Every-
body is happy and comfortable except the victim
of a first-class passion. The woman who has
played upon the seriousness of the man from
Africa until he wishes he was dead is content to-
march off to supper with her husband on the
understanding that her affections will be re-pur-
chased with further jewellery and things. The
prosperous sassiety liars pursue their careers of
uselessness, as usual, and philosopher Brough is
about to marry a peaceful widow, but the en-
thusiast whose soul dared to soar above the Pro-
prieties is left to kick himself for his courage.
Matinees have been introduced at the Bijou, and
seem likely to grow in gilt-edged popularity.
“The Liars” will take its second matinee on
Thursday.
** * *
“La Poupde ” is doing a steady business
at the Princess ! s, and the date for ‘ 1 The Geisha ”
is not yet announced. The most admirable feature
of “La Poupee,” next to the choruses, dresses
and stage-fittings, is our old friend Lauri, who
seems to have suddenly recovered his long lost
buoyancy. Charles Kenningham, too, is a general
favorite (although his make-up and manner, in
the first scene, suggest female, impersonation),
and Miss Vane seems to be accepted as a success-
ful performer of the name-part. The doll, how-
ever, is even less convincing than ever, for she has
discarded one or two of her squeaks ; or perhaps
the squeak needed oiling, the other night, and was
unable to pop out when called for. Melbourne
parents are invited to bring their little ones to the
doll-show on Wednesday afternoons.
* * * «
Talking of matinees, the widow and family
of John Hennings are to take a benefit at the
Firm’s theatre next Thursday (24th), when all the
available theatrical forces will combine to make
the programme worthy of the occasion. Twenty
paintings, in oil and water colors, by the deceased
artist are to be added to the other attractions,
each ticket-purchaser getting a coupon chance in
the lottery.

Nothing calculated to eclipse Peggy Pryde
and the boneless gentleman has been happening at
the Opera House these last few weeks, but Peggy
is just now .at her liveliest, anyhow. Also one of
the recent arrivals, Fred Poplar, seems capable of
genuine funniness when his song gives him a show.
His only bright effort, thus far, is achieved with
the aid of a tailor’s dummy, which he takes to his
bosom after many whiskies and loves more dearly
than a brother.
At Poverty Point.
The suicide of Scot Inglis is partly- at least a commen-
tary on the dreary accursedness of the hash-emporium.
His wife was away ; he had just lost his billet; and then
he considered things for two days in his boarding-house—
and died. Any other man who sits down in a hash-
mosque when his spirits are at their lowest ebb and his
nerves are out of tune to think over his misfortunes
might do the same.
There have been three “ Kings of Crime ” in Sydney-.
Albert Marsh was the best of them ; Diver is a respectable
- second, and Charles Holloway-was the worst. The place
where everybody begins to know whether the King will
be a success or not is at the end of the first scene. Simon
Grandelu is joining the band on the understanding that
it will plunder his aged father and give him half the
proceeds, and he wants to know what will happen to
him if he should send in his resignation. Then a hand
comes down on his shoulder from behind and a voice say-s
“ Death.” Albert Marsh said it loudly and convincingly
with the limelight on him. Holloway nearly blew- down
the scenery with a shrill, petulant monosyllable. Diver
says it in a whisper, which is only natural, for when a
criminal is posing as a highly respectable citizen, and the
local policeman is drinking- in the public-house just oppo-
site, he doesn’t w-ant to yell out compromising remarks
like that at the top of his voice.
“E.J.8.”: Isn’t your London correspondent wrong in
stating that Marie Elster is married to a brother of the
Larnach who w-on the Derby? “ Jeddah ” Larnach, I’m

“The Liars,” the H. A. Jones comedy, w-hich the
Broughs are now- playing, is really the same author’s
“Case of Rebellious Susan ” over again. Compare the
two plots. “Liars”: A young man is in love with
married flirt; wise man of the w-orld (soldier) disentangles
matters; young man goes off to Africa ; woman goes
back to her husband ; man of the w-orld marries an in-
teresting widow. “ Susan ”: A young man is in love w-ith
another fellow’s wife : wise man of the world (barrister)
disentangles matters ; young man goes off to Maoriland ;
woman returns to husband ; man of the world marries an
interesting widow. The plots of the two plays are as much
alike as two houses in the same suburban terrace. Every-
thing happens in precisely the same way in each. “ The
Liars,” though, introduces more character than we get in
“ Susan.”

“ N.’’: Anent actor H. R. Jew-ett turning religious.
Nearly all actors are of a religious turn. Few sceptics.
Actresses are more sceptical. Jew-ett, the most unlikely
of men, former husband of a popular actress, Miss Maggie
Knight, follows the example of Montague Stanley-,The
Edinburgh tragedian, and Sheridan Knowles, dramatist.
“Haresfoot”: Mrs. Hennings, w-idow of John Hen-
nings, will be remembered as a graceful dancer on the
Melb. stage 30 years ago.
“ Shifter” : Actors don’t suicide through poverty, but
are murdered by it. Sam VVooife died through exposure
at nights, sleeping in Sydney Domain. Humoured ne "as
a Balaclava survivor. Belike.
“ Haresfoot ” : “The Liars” is precisely the kind of
thing that Williamson and Musgrove will have nothing
to do with. Their gauge of Australian taste is that
dialogue goes for little ; the pinch of experience has tola
them that this nation is of the Ducrow kind, wanting to
“ cut the dialect and git to the hosses.” In other words,
it is simply the recipe of Demosthenes —“ Action, action,
action.”
Wily Harry Rickards intends to sing around Maoriland
with Biondi and a select co., for the benefit of his health
and pocket. Not that he is off-color, but because he
might be in need of the trip—if he didn't take it. So
with one eye on the pool and the other on his constitu-
tion, ’Any stars for safety.

The “ cyclists’ theatre-night” is indeed a humorous
gag. A few weeks ago a cyclists’ night was advertised to
happen in all its glory at Melb. Royal, and after much
preliminary “boosting” with ads. and paragraphs only a
dozen of the L.V.W. (half complimentary tickets) and
one member of the V.A.C.U. attended. Total amount,
14s. !
All the scenery, accessories and smart dresses used in
the Melbourne Royal productions “ Round the World in
80 Days ” and “Sons of the Sea,” now belong to the
Theatre Royal proprietary, which had a lien on the lot for
one week’s rent—£7o. The failure of a season is some-
times better than a success—for the landlord.
The theatre usher seldom attempts to remove a rowdy
sailor. He knows better. In the gallery of a Sydney
theatre t’other eveninga party of tars were interrupting
and making themselves generally objectionable, when a
man who wanted to hear the play .yelled out “Order!
order !” “’Ere, wot yer interruptin’ thedrammer for?”
cried an attendant, evidently sent up to keep order and
feeling called on to do something, and, grabbing the
unfortunate person, he bundled him out with violence,
amid the cheers of the jolly- British sailors.
When Doughboy, a non-professional speculator, opened
his music-hall great was his glory. The first week was
“good hiz,” so, after treasury on the Monday, he invited
the conductor (Johnson) and the pianist (Foozlebersr)
around to have a drink. The publican attended to their
wants, which Doughboy mentioned thus: “I’ll have a
small bottle of fiz, and so will Johnson.” Then a pause,
and Foozleberg allowed his imagination to run away
with him (this he confessed afterwards), but Doughboy
brought him back to earth with the words —“And
Foozleberg! Oh, give kirn a long beer !” Fact.
There is little joy these times for the Australian scenic
artist. Some of the most familiar names were attached
to tenders for a job in a big country town a few weeks
back. A set of nine scenes was required for a stage of
fair dimensions, and the work was worth £7O at a cut-
throat estimate. Yet competent brushers asked as little
as £35 for a job occupying six weeks, and out of that
would have to find materials and pay a mechanical assis-
tant at least £2 10s. a week.
“T.D.”: Safe for a mere lightweight to write of the
extraordinary development of tissue among the men of
Bland Holt’s co. now that they have left Sydney? Baker,
the seceded Cosgrove, Charley Brown, Montgomery, and
nearly- everybody except Bland himself, are of constantly
increasing proportions—which seems to prove that “laugh
and grow fat” is a trumpery adage, and that the best
fattening process is indulgence in a round of villainies,
or endurance of the world’s persecutions for twenty y-ears
a night.
Mrs. Tommy- Hudson (Miss May Habgood, Antoinette
Sterling’s pianiste), has died suddenly in Adelaide, sad
to say, during her husband’s absence in India.
Dear Bulletin'. —Those who thirst to see their names in
a newspaper, so long as they- do not appear in the police
news, have a fine field open among the “ energetic and
influential committees” who mismanage benefit enter-
tainments—bodies which seldom have even a rudimentary
knowledge of the business they- undertake. Not long ago
a young Australian lady vocalist of much promise had a
farewell concert tendered to her—that is how the ener-
getics put it—prior to her departure for Europe. As date
of concert approached her maestro, anxious for his pupil’s
success, enquired about progress and found that, beyond
holding a few useless meetings, the energetics had done
nothing, whereupon he took the whole thing in charge
himself. After the concert, nevertheless, the papers said
that “the large attendance testified alike to the popularity
of the singer and the energetic endeavors of the commit-
tee to whom the tusk of organisation had been entrusted.”
The “ energetic endeavors ” had for a net result about as
much coin as the young lady had taken at a concert,
assisted only by a few local amateurs, in a country
town of 2000 inhabitants.—J.A.C.
The' late Helen Faucit is said to have been a great
actress (as Pauline, in “ Lady of Lyons,” and more tragic
heroines), according to the taste of the times when she
flourished. They were the times of high falutin and
awful stage unnaturalism. This writer has a recollection
of Helen Faucit as a Shakespearean recitress. Her stiff
gestures and slow, inhuman spouting of the lines were
ridiculously- old-fashioned even as compared with, say,
the style of Miss Heath (Wilson Barrett’s wife), who was
not more than five years younger. Acting of the “ great”
kind which appears to have been identified with Charles
Kemble, Sarah Siddons, and Helen Faucit, was absolutely
destitute of all sense of humor.
“ S.S.” : Very entertaining is this claim set up by the
poor player to be his own and only efficient critic and
the accusations of ignorance of histrionic hugger-mugger
that are hurled at the journalistic reviewer of mummers
and their mummery. After all, who cares a tinsmith’s
swear about an actor’s methods? All that concerns the
critic and the people is his effects. He attempts to re-
produce Wilfred Denver, “ Father Christmas,” or Ham-
let, and is judged by results as they appeal to intelligent
minds ; hang his methods! Let actors bicker over them if
they will—it is their business. If actors acted only to
actors and for actors the matter of merit might be
left entirely to the perfesh to worry out. The pretence
of a my-sterious faculty in the trained pro. is 90 p>r cent,
tommyrot. The most perfect piece of comedy I have ever
seen was performed by a little nine-year-old comedienne
play-ing school-teacher to a class of half-a-dozen baby
mummers in a suburban back-yard.

» * * *
One of the brighter spots in the Westralian
Govt.’s administration of the goldfields has been the pro-
vision of public batteries ; though as to them there has
been, generally speaking, much delay and a good deal of
pettifogging. Norseman battery is the only one yet at
actual work, though by the time this is in print the plant
bought at Belong should be rattling away. Others at
Yalgoo and Mulline should talk this month ; and still
others at Tuekanarra and Lennonville (Murchison) and
Mt. Ida and Mt. Leonora (Menzies) in Dec. Yerilla will
also have a battery, but it will not be ready until
January.

• • • •
Westralia puts up another record in gold-pro-
duction, that for Oct. amounting to 116,8240 z. This is
so far above any previous month, especially last month,
as to create a feeling that it will be found much greater
than the next. Nearly one-half (55,1480 z.) came from the
mines at Hannan’s, though the outputs from several other
camps are substantial. Murchison, 14,3160 z. Where did
it all come, from ? Has the camp become too poor to keep
one small newspaper-hummer on the spot ? He would
not eat—or drink—much. Coolgardie, 10,780 oz.; N.E.
Coolgardie, 10,1390 z. ; N. Coolgardie, 72740 z. ; E. Mur-
chison, 36960 z. ; Dundas, 32550 z.; Peak Hill, 31550 z. ;
Broad Arrow, 27910 z.; Mt. Margaret, 29290 z. ; Yilgarn,
17170 z. ; Pilbarra, 1388 oz. And yet no sign of a return-
ing boom. Not a few Westralians came over to Melb. to
see the Cup, and also to see if it were possible to float
mines in the east, and have returned knowing that there
was not the remotest show. All the Australias have had
enough. Developed mines are not for sale, and as soon as
one approaches a reasonable stage of development it is
snapped up by Bull’s representatives. Boomsters have
given up the game. Some have gone back into the stopes,
where they find beating hard holes a little more irksome
than before; others cannot dig, and to beg are not
ashamed. A few have gone to gaol, where many
should be. One, who made a pot of money, is night-
watchman on one of the mines he sold, and, there being
so little to watch, has an easy billet —though the screw is
not fattening. Newspapers generally have given up shriek-
ing for a boom, and have mostly gone into liquidation
themselves. Writer saw a list last week showing the fate
of the W.A. goldfields-press, and a horrible one it was.
Magnificent mining-plants are scattered over the fields—
silent monuments of Bull’s folly and the rascality of the
boom. Here and there are pubs., in which hundreds of
pounds have been taken weekly for drink, now absolutely
deserted. And all this in the province which is now Aus-
tralia’s chief gold-producer!
* • * *
The new battery of 40 stamps lately started ab
the Galleymont gold-mines is one of the finest gold mill-
plants at work in N.S.W., and is well worth inspection by
parties contemplating erection of similar machinery. The
ore is fed automatically through rockbreakers and feedert
without shovelling, and the whole is most substantially
built. The engines are a fine pair of horizontal compound
type, driving the battery with ropes. Mr. M. Eissler,
the managing engineer, speaks in the highest terms of
the construction and design of the plant, which was built
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South
Melbourne.*
* • « •
Another great Kalgoorlie mine has fallen into
line as a regular producer and div. payer—the Golden
Horseshoe. First clean-up from 20 heavy stamps over
70000 z., and if a complete clean-up of the cyanide solu-
tions and slimes had been made the return would have
been about 10,000 oz. Mine looking well enough to antici-
pate last month’s return for a long time. W. G. Brook-
man has declared in London that had the surveyors
marked out the Boulder as he intended the G.H. ground
would have been in it. The G.H. was pegged by a young
N.S.W. man—Henderson, whose family is well known in
the Forbes district.
• • * *
All hope of shareholders ever obtaining restitu-
tion of the enormous profits made by Col. North in the
flotation of the Plunderderry Mine has gone. As part
vendor, North got in cash £78,740 16s. 4d. ; and it was
evidently his intention to refund the lot, but his executors
declined to be bound by his distinct (verbal) public
promise. The matter went into Court, where Justice
Romer felt a strong disposition to give effect to the
settlement that was contemplated, but though he had
spared no pains to find means of doing so, was unable.
The circumstances, he said, showed a promised gift not
completed at the death of the man who promised, and
which could not be enforced against his executors. He
showed his sympathy with the shareholders by not giving
costs. Then their solicitors wrote to the executors, in-
cluding Mrs. North, and Capt. Harry North, asking if they
had any proposal to make. Mrs. North regretted she
could do nothing owing to the expenditure on
the estate being in excess of receipts. Capt. North
was not prepared to make any proposal. Both acted
strictly within the law—so did the latter’s dad in
floating the Plunderderry!—but dad; when he realised
that the “ golden hole ” had vanished, and what a hopeless
run the shareholders would have for their money, did the
right thing. Had he lived he would, as Justice Romer
was assured, have carried out his promise. But Mrs.
North, his wife,'and Capt. North, his son, decline to be
judged by any other than the strict letter of the law.

COLUMN
The Westralian Government proposes to spend
during the financial year ending 30th June, 1898,
£1,379,876 of loan money. The principal items are :
Railways and tramways .. .. £539,145
Harbors .. .. .. .. 259,609
Coolgardie water supply .. .. 250,000
Water supply and sewage .. .. 29,000
Development of goldfields.. .. 119,246
Roads and bridges .. .. .. 8000
Immigration .. .. .. .. 2000
Agriculture .. .. .. .. 5000
Salaries .. 67,815
Expenses of raising loans .. .. 100,000
“ Expenses of raising loans ” is a very beautiful item.
Westralia wants 1T,279,000 for works, and its credit is
about good enough to get the amount net at 3b or at
most 3 Vi, per cent. But it insists on borrowing at 3 per
cent, for the beastly dignity of ranking with M.L., Vic-
toria and N.S.W., so its loan has to be sold at a discount,
and it will probably require to give £1,379,000 of stock for
11,279,000 in cash.' Therefore, it leaves posterity to lose
£lOO,OOO on the transaction, and the present generation
saves a little or next to nothing in interest. £1,379,000 at 3
percent, means 641,370 per annum interest; £1,279,000 at
3j per cent, means £41,567 interest; so if the discount
could be saved by raising the interest J per cent, it would
only cost £2OO a year to save. £lOO,OOO. If the loan was
floated net at 3| per cent, the interest would be £44,765,
in which case it would cost £3lOO a year interest to save
that £lOO,OOO principal, and even at that rate a 3i per
cent, loan at par would be cheaper in the end than a 3
per cent, one sold at a big loss. If a province’s credit
is only good enough for 3J per cent., and it insists
on floating 3 per cent, loans at a heavy dis-
count, it simply means that it is paying the other \ per
cent, interest with borrowed money. It might just as
reasonably make the interest 2 per cent, and sell each
£lOO debenture at £6O ; or 1 per cent, and sell the £lOO
debenture at £40.; or pay no interest at all, but sell each
£lOO debenture at £2O and let the buyer make his profit
when the loan fell due for repayment. The principle of
shunting the load on to posterity is the same either way.
* * « *
Consideration of the N.S.W. Estimates, issued
last week, make Premier Reid’s falling away (under the
pretence that revenue is wanted so badly that even the
cherished principle of Freetrade must go overboard to get
it) absolutely inexplicable. Last year’s revenue was
£9,489,718, and expenditure £9,507,202 ; deficit £17,484.
Next year’s expenditure is estimated at £9,681,421, or
£174,219 more than last year, and as last year was £17,484
short, that means that £191,703 more revenue is needed
to keep the accounts straight. Well, four months of the
12 are gone, and already Premier Reid has received
£163,028 more revenue than he had received by the end
of 0ct.,’97. In other words, with only one-third of the
year gone, he has already collected seven-eighths of the
extra money wanted. There is no attempt this year to
pay more of the public works expenditure out of
revenue than was paid last year. There is no
attempt to do with less loan money than las year—in fact,
the Reid Govt, is “blewing” more loan money this year
than it ever did before. The amount payable in reduction
of old debts is decreased by £42,000. The harvest promises
to be distinctly better than last. The count ry (we have
Premier Reid’s word for it) is prospering under the Free-
trade regime. But the one overwhelming fact is that the
extra revenue which Premier Reid wants is already
coming in so fast that on present appearances he will
have mo.re than twice as much as he says he requires
without extra taxation. Is the present move intended to
show that Reid isn’t a bigoted Freetrader after all, so that
if the Federal Government wants a Protectionist Premier
the choice won’t lie absolutely between Turner and Kings-
ton and he may have a fair chance of the billet ? Or does
he contemplate more expendilure than appears in his
estimates ? Or does he want to produce a big real surplus
with a view to silencing his critics? Or is a reduction of
the land-tax looming in the far distance? The only thing
which seems certain is that the Reid Government doesn’t
contemplate giving up the habit of charging unproductive
works to loans. Such a burst of honesty as that is beyond
any N.S.W. Government nowadays.

* * * *
Vic. Leg. Council a little while ago almost
unanimously threw out the Bill abolishing the attach-
ment of wages under £2 a week. The other day it
changed its mind, reinstated the Bill, and read it a second
time in a friendly and almost cordial spirit. The Interests
of Property, as usual, were the cause of the change. In
the interval a number of people represented to the 48
Rich Landlords that very often a small creditor attached
a man’s wages, and larger creditors with Property had to
wait. Sometimes the man lost his billet through the
small creditor’s action, and then Highly Respectable Per-
sons with Property got nothing at all. Moreover, he
sometimes put up a friend, as a bogus creditor, to attach
his wages, and thereby Solid People with a Stake in the
Country were done out of their money. Also, the attach-
ment of wages could be abolished by the attached person
going insolvent, which he generally did, and in that case
highly respectable creditors seldom got anything at all.
In fact, it dawned on the 48 Rich Landlords that the
attachment of wages was sometimes actually injurious to
Property, and the news affected them almost to tears.
* * * *
The Mayor of Sydney proposes to borrow an-
other £100,900, to pay off the bank overdraft—in other
words, to help out the revenue by charging the accumu-
lated deficits to loans. A move much more to the point
would be to increase the rates and make the revenue
cover expenditure and a little over, and pay off the
shortage that way. One simple reason why most muni-
cipalities in N.S.W. are dead-broke is that the rates are
not high enough to cover expenditure, and the rest is
deficit. Sydney, for instance, levies Is. 4d. in the £.
London tackles its difficulties manfully by paying ss. Id.
in the £, and the average all over England and Wales is
3s. lOd. in the £. It is the old story how this is, in pro-
portion to population, the richest country in the world
and too dashed poor to pay its way.

“ T.W.G.” : If you want a reasonably safe in-
vestment. with moderate interest just now and better in-
terest later on, no call-liability, and a fair chance of get-
ting your capital largely increased in course of time, the
preference shares of the Commercial Bank of Australia
might suit you. Anyhow, if it were the writer’s gamble
he would as soon go into that as anything else just now.
* * • *
The strongest point made in defence of the
recently tried and acquitted Q.N. Bank directors was
that, even while their bank was going with a mighty
slump to its doom, they didn’t sell their own shares —
that one of them even bought more shares. Of course, if
any one of them had sold his shares, he would have lost
his qualification as a director. He would, therefore, have
lost £3OO a year. If he sold after matters became
really bad, he would have laid up great trouble for the
future ; the man who cleared out at such a time could
hardly have persuaded the public that he didn’t know
the bank was cronk and the balance-sheets unreliable,
and that might have meant ruin and even gaol.
He would probably have got only a small amount for his
shares ; Q.N.B. shares were as high as £ll or £l2 even in
’9l, but it was only because there were comparatively few
in the market; if anyone had tried to dispose of thou-
sands of them he might soon have run the price down to
£5 or less. If the board had made a unanimous attempt
to clear out, it might, while getting very little for its
stock, have caused a slump which would have wrecked
the shaking institution, and led to investigation and
heavy calls. It was even advisable to buy shares ; the
best hope for the Q.N. Bank lay in a new boom, which
might send up the value of the securities, and the best
hope that the bank might last till it came lay in keeping
up public confidence. The holding of the shares proved
nothing either for or against the Q.N.B. Board. An
honest foolish director who didn’t know—through his
neglect and want of business capacity—how things stood,
would have held on or sold, just as it suited him. A dis-
honest one who did know would have held on, partly be-
cause it was difficult to get out and partly because
getting out would have been strong evidence against him.
Supposing, however, as is quite possible, the
Q.N.B. directors stayed in because they honestly believed
in their bank, it merely shows that they knew little or
nothing about their own institution. Few directors do ;
and this is one point where the law wants reforming. A
director is paid to know about his bank and if he doesn’t
know he holds his billet and draws his salary by false pre-
tences. He tells the shareholders at the half-yearly meeting
that the bank is prospering, and they think that he knows
it is prospering—they pay him to know, and they can’t
imagine that the honorable gentleman would tell them
that a thing was true without knowing'it. While it is a
sufficient excuse for a director that he was a lazy, credu-
lous dunderhead who neglected his work, and while an
auditor can dodge the unpleasant duty of knowing that
the bank is rotten by simply certifying that the balance-
sheet agrees with the books (which is often just the same
as saying that the perjurer in the witness-box agrees with
the one in the dock) nobody is responsible except the
manager. And the law doesn’t make him commit him-
self by stating in the balance-sheet that nil the bad and
doubtful debts are provided for. It doesn’t make him
state that all the alleged paid-up capital has actually been
received from the public. It doesn’t compel him to stop
reckoning as profit the interest, on the unsecured or badly
secured overdraft against which nothing has been paid
for seven years—he can simply plead that he was
naturally a sanguine individual, and quite capable of
honestly' regarding an unsecured o.d. as good, though
nothing had,been received on account of it for 20 years.
And the law can’t prove he wasn’t such an individual. As
the banking law stands in most of the Australias nobody
in a bank is responsible for anything barring embezzle-
ment or forgery, and when people can grant overdrafts to
each other from the purest motives they don’t forge or
embezzle. The law of Queensland having decided that the
Q.N.B directors were so unspeakably incapable that they
didn’t know about the s ate of an institution that was
falling to pieces before their eyes, what does the public
propose to do with them ? Will any one of them, after
that public certificate of his practical imbecility, be
allowed to keep on directing public companies ? And will
anybody put a penny of his money into any company
which any one of them directs?
* * * *
What is the matter with the B.H. Junction?
Bulletin correspondents ask. There doesn’t seem to be
anything particularly wrong with it novv, except that it
is a mine which yields, and will (so far as can be seen) go
on yielding, sulphides of no higher than milling grade. A
couple of years ago the co. was on a big pocket of re-
markably rich sulphides, and the new-chum Adelaide
directors tore them out and paid dividends gaily. And
then, when the pocket had been pretty well emptied, the
overweight crushed in the workings—they had been too
busy rooting out ore to attend properly to the filling-
in of the depleted parts. Then a few hundred
shareholders who had put down this paper’s repeated
warnings to its liver, or something equally irre-
levant, fell out of a paradise of dividends into a
purgatory consisting of a stove-in mine of medium-
grade ore, without any reasonable plant for dealing with
the refractory stuff, and without any cash reserve to buy
the necessary plant with. Now the plant is running all
right, and the mine is opening up all right; debts are
being wiped off, and divs. will start early in the new year.
But they will not be on the champagne scale ; they may
run to bottled English, though the mine’s two neighbors,
the British and the North, can’t even make “colonial”
out of their graft.
» * * *
N.S.W. Govt, metallurgist Taylor, reporting on
the Broken Hill zinc problem (which is, after all, the
whole sulphide problem, so long as concentration is
adopted) is only able to say that a lot of things that might
answer elsewhere will not do on the Barrier, where fuel is
scarce and labor dear, and that the processes which are
most likely to succeed are those which aim at getting the
zinc in the “tailings” into a more compact body by
means of chemical changes. Which is the opinion several
times expressed here—sometimes in connection with the
Carmichael process, which Mr. Taylor evidently believes
is one of the two most likely to succeed. The other is
one which T. J. Greenwav, of Block 14, has in hand ; but
it has not yet been tried outside the laboratory. A third
possibly effective system is the magnetic concentration of
the zinc tailings ; this, it is understood, is to be tried by
the German co. which has secured sites for the erection of
machinery near the mines. Nothing is generally known
here as to what that can do, though.
• * « *
S.A. Railway Dept., which proposed to raise
the freight on sulphides from Broken Hill to the sea-
board by Is. 6d. per ton, has undertaken to hold off until
the beginning of 1900 at anyrate. By then processes of
extraction ought to be advanced a bit; for the present
the increase would make hundreds of thousands of tons of
stuff now handled at a fraction of profit quite unpayable.
Meanwhile, if the private railway from Cockburn to
Broken Hill would reduce its rates to the level of the S.A.
Govt, lines, much stuff now unpayable would begin to
yield a profit.

In the first six months of this year Broken Hill
exported £395,092 worth of sulphide concentrates. In the
third quarter it sent out £256,800 worth ; and in October
over £lOO,OOO worth. But against that there has been an
entire cessation of bullion export.

"Finding the air-oompressor altogether too small
for the work ahead, the Oobar-Chesney directors, after
seeing several big compressors at work on Bendigo, have
placed with Thompson and Co., of Castlemaine, an order
for one as big as that lately turned out by that firm for
the New Chum Railway and Mt. Lyell mines—capable of
running about 20 drills. Also, ordered from the same
people two boilers of the size and type supplied to the
New Moon mine, where one has taken the place of three
old Cornish boilers in driving, alone, one of the biggest
batteries in Bendigo. Erected, this part of the new plant
will cost about £4OOO.
* * * *
Cobar notes : Refreshing to note that most co’s
at Cobar now propose systematic mining. Hitherto,
little holes slapped right down through lode matter were
called “ shafts,” and pig-rooting after rich spots “ open-
ing-up.” Cobar Gold-Mines (“ Fort Bourke ”), Great Peak
Freehold, and Chesney are setting examples likely to be
followed. The first-named has not yet had the ghost of a
show to display its qualities. The old co. stuck up a 100-
head mill without making room in the mine for men to
work. Soon found that it could not keep even 40-head
going to advantage from the enormous lode ; but, not
to be beaten, the co. slammed through everything it
could lay hands on, and yet the result, ad considered, was
not so bad. It is the only mine from Treat Cobar to Peak
in which copper has not been found, and that is only
because it has not yet gone deep enough.
At the Great Peak, development-work is being
pushed well ahead. The mine shows copper at the lower
levels. The Conqueror (the Peak’s next-door neighbor) is
phenomenally rich in silver-gold ore. It also possesses a
big lode rich in gold alone. Last crushing from it was
130 tons for 2400 z. of good-quality gold. The Young Aus-
tralian is still cyaniding, and paying 3d. div. The tail-
ings-heap will soon be finished', and the supply of free-
milling ore is very low and will soon run out. What it is
going to do for divs. and money to develop the mine’s
rich copper lode is unstated.
♦ * * *
The Lucknow (five men’s ground), formerly
known as Bolte’s, at Wyalong, N.S.W., has in five months
yielded from its small, but strong and enduring vein, now
being worked at a depth of 340 ft., just under £14,000 in
gold. There appear to be years of profitable work ahead.
* • * *
Ed. Bulletin. — Re those <l Cobar notes.” True
that Randolph Adams and Barnett both took some over-
an-ounce samples from the bottom of the Occidental, but
the important fact that they came from a streak in the
main ore-body has been omitted. The mine was cheap at
the price, and probably the reason it was not bought was
because the crowd contemplating the deal saw no chance
of selling it at a much bigger price. Nine cases in ten, if
not 990 in 1000, it is not out of the mine that money is
made, but on the turn-over. At present it would better
pay the Cobar-Chesney to concentrate than to buy flux to
blast-furnace without concentration. The object of going
down another 200 ft. before opening out is to see if the ore
changes in character. This important question will be
settled in two months.—Peak.
* * * •
Another start made with the Homeward Bound,
Yalwal, N.S.W. ; this time with cyanide. Shut down for
nearly four years while the co. fought no end of law-suits
to retain its lease —all on account of its agent not paying
£5 rent at the right time. As if the business-like thing
to do was not to fine more or less heavily instead of for-
feiting. And that asinine law still exists, and as long as
mines are worked by companies like mistakes will arise.

The plans for the zinc-works, which the Aus-
tralian Metal Co. proposes to erect at Broken Hill, are
being made in Germany, and are now weekly expected.
The Metal Co. is mostly German also, and collaborates
with Hamburg operators. Present high price of spelter—
£22 10s. a ton —spurs the co. on.
* * * *
Though the Tas. Copper Co. is making big pre-
parations for the erection of a special pyritic smelting
plant at Rosebery on the west-coast, there is an appalling
decline in the value of the locally-held shares of the co.
Several mines on the line have immense bodies of low-
grade ore in sight, but the refractory agents are in alarm-
ing evidence, zinc being particularly plentiful. During
the last six months Rosebery stocks have come down
heavily, and many shows have closed “ awaiting the ad-
vent of the Emu Bay railway,” the daily papers say, but
there seems to be another fly in the jam somewhere. Tas.
Copper shares are selling at 7s. 6d.—six months ago they
were 40s. If Rosebery sulphides can be treated profitably
it is a big field ; meanwhile, the public seems to have made
up its mind to wait and watch.
* * * *
North Mt. Lyell: “ A number of hands are em-
ployed in napping and bagging the rich ore, which will be
sent out at the rate of 70 tons a week until further
notice. It is intended to keep the bulk value of the ore
sent away up to 20 per cent. Copper-contents of the N.
Lyell ore carrying this per centage of copper run from 8
to 9oz. of silver and from 1 dwt. to lhlwt. gold per ton.”
Obviously it has never struck the Argus correspondent
that unless the management can make better arrange-
ments for treatment than any other copper-mine owners
in Australasia have yet been able to make, the mentioned
silver and gold contents are almost valueless in a 20 per
cent. ore. The total value would be about l's per ton of
copper, and the chances are they will find that the bulk, if
not the whole, will be eaten up in costs of extraction.
“ Napping and bagging ” and “ keeping the value up to
20 per cent.” indicate hand-picking, whereas if the
account published about the richness of the ore-bodies
in this mine were half true, it should be an easy
matter to send away 70 tons a week without it.

Ed. Bulletin.—The special correspondent at
Gormanston of Zeehan and Dundas Herald has spread
himself over a half-col. to deny the truth of a “ Wild Cat”
par dealing with the importance of No. 4 Tunnel ore as a
factor in the maintainance of Mt. Lyell divs., and with
characteristic enthusiasm fails to apply himself to the
point at issue. The par in question sought to prove, on
the evidence of average value, that No. 4 Tunnel was
showing signs of rapid exhaustion, and demonstrated the
necessity of that part of the mine keeping up its end of
the stick. That theco. regards No. 4as an incident is in-
dicated by the policy of drawing sparingly on this part of
the mine and keeping the material distinct from that of
the “ open cut.” Writer attaches no special importance
to the fact that the co. now describes the rich ore as com-
ing from “ Underground ” instead of from “ No. 4 Tun-
nel”; it may, however, have some meaning. No. 4
Tunnel” or “ Underground” to have any special value to
the mine must keep up its high grade ; if it does not it
simply becomes part of the whole, and Lyell must work
out its salvation as a low-grade proposition. The “ S.C.”
indulges in one independent calculation to show that the
Sept, out-turn from No. 4 Tunnel was worth (assay value),
according to him, £4 7s. 6d. per ton. After Lake Dora
booming he is trying a method of inverse calculation and
we should feel joyous that he is within 12s. of a correct
solution in a calculation involving nearly £5. He makes
special point of the improved silver value of the Sept,
run. Certainly the metal did show a big increase on pre-
vious months, but the par treated with “ average ” and
not “particular” value, and as the “S.C.” has not
grasped the rudimentary fact in mining finance that one
yield does not make a mine, the following table of values
may interest him :
Aug. 1897, No. 4 Tunnel (Assay value) .. £9 14 6
28 weeks ending 9th Feb.,’9B .. .. 612 8

4 „ „ 21st Sept.,’93 .. .. 419 5
4 „ „ 19th 0ct.,’98 .. .. 4 19
A mine or particular part of a mine that is capable of de-
clining so persistently and awfully in so short a time may
be equal to any enormity. Writer would like some better
reason than the past performances of Mt. Lyell to justify
him in regarding it as a “ gilt-edged mining security.”
According to its general manager, practically no pay ore
has been developed below No. 4 Tunnel, and since the
said G.M. penned his memorable report on the reserves
extensive exploration at No. 5 Tunnel has only
resulted in the development of a small streak of
silver ore. So, on present developments, life of
mine is limited to 7 or 8 years with full
complement of smelters in operation. How seriously the
position of Mt. Lyell as a “ safe investment ” has been
affected is shown by following':—Average value for six
months ending 31st March was—copper, 4 - 25 per cent.;
silver, 3'69oz. ; gold, '177. Average recovered value open
cut and “ underground " for Oct., 1898 : Copper, 2'59 per
cent. ; silver, 4-29oz. ; gold, '132oz. One day when all the
furnaces are in blast, a cyclone may strike Mt. Lyell and
its average value may fall only half per cent, copper, half
oz. ei'oer, hi If dwt. gold —not much, seeing it has fallen
double this in the past six months. At same time, copper
man easily come back to the £47 10s. it was bringing 12
months ago, and silver may any time be worth 2s., seeing
it has been lower than this within pa 118 months. Share-
holders would then find themselves confronted with this
little sum

Lock washday
At the trial of Q.N.B. ex-directors the
accused were considerately and gracefully referred
to, not as “prisoners,” but as “defendants.”
Moreover, the ex-directors were allowed to absent
themselves—“ with permission ’’—from court dur-
ing their trial, and one of them—on account of
ill-health—was permitted to drop in and view the
proceedings whenever he happened to be passing.
None of the accused entered the dock—where the
first-offending, or perhaps innocent, young woman
is sometimes exhibited for hours. The gentle-
manly defendants were also accommodated with
seats at the solicitor s table—just as in an ordinary
civil action ; and the case was removed from the
Criminal Court to the civil section—“ Criminal
Court ’’ not sounding nice. There can be no ob-
jection to this considerate and humane treatment
of the Q.N.B. ex-directors, but why should not
similar delicacy be shown with regard to all per-
sons undergoing trial ? It is surely time enough
to treat a man as a criminal when he is proved to
be one.
• * * *
At the termination of the trial of the
Q.N.B. ex-directors the absurdity of the whole
proceedings became manifest. The verdict of
“Not guilty’’ was hardly declared when most of
the jurymen stampeded in the direction of the
discharged magnates, and began to ostentatiously
pump-handle them. Add to this the Chief Jus-
tice’s smilingly-expressed pleasure at the decision,
and it will be plain that only a little music was
needed to complete the “effect.”
* * * *
One mystery at least has been partly
cleared up by the Q.N. Bank cases. About a
dozen years ago the safe at a, suburban branch was
incomprehensibly looted of its contents (some
=64!)00). Put into the safe (?) on Saturday, on
Monday morning money had vanished. Evidence
given last week showed that the- notes, some
<*l2oo, had been recovered. How, whence, or
wiere, deponent sayeth not.

The anniversary of the Dunbar wreck has
just passed, and every old Sydney inhabitant baa
been reminiscing. Related by one that some of
the people who flocked to the spot professed to
recognise certain of the corpses tossing about in
the surf, and one Hibernian person went so far as
to exclaim: “Oh, be jabers ! —if that isn’t Mrs.
Mulcafferty’s leg ! ” (mentioning the wife of a well-
known Sydneyite, who was among the passengers).
Whereupon, a low, deep, hollow voice from the
more or less awe-stricken crowd : “ And how do
you know it’s Mrs. Mulcafferty’s leg ? ”

The Curfew Bell regulation is now law in
Hobart. The Bulletin considers it only right to
warn juvenile visitors to the Derwent that it will
be necessary to have a birth-certificate or parents
handy to avoid being run in as “ under sixteen 99
one of these summer evenings.
* * * *
In fining the master of the Sydney har-
bor ferry steamer Manly, <£3G in all, for carrying
123 passengers over his licensed number (550),
Mr. Delohery, P.M., did a most salutary thing.
\v hen the inevitable Sydney harbor Princess Alice
affair at last does occur, may it not be further in-
tensified by the illegal overcrowding of the vessel
or vessels concerned—for lots of Sydney harbor
steamers, even when laden with only their
ticket ” number of passengers, are nevertheless,
actually much over-loaded, the Marine board sys-
tem of calculating passenger-allowance being
radically wrong.
Manly won’t be a place for reasonable people to
live in, or even visit, until one has the alternative
of getting there overland. Thousands of people
absolutely shrink at the idea of travelling on occa-
sion in a steam-boat crammed with 1000 or 1300
passengers, strongly leavened with hilarious
fools, and packed together like sardines;
and it does not need a strong imagination
to conceive what hideous things would come
to pass if a boat so loaded got into collision, or
anything happened in a heavy beam sea to a
crowded steamer whose engines broke down off
Dobroyde bumbora, or Middle Head. As well go
ashore at the Gap 1

A SAVING CLAUSE.
THE SHEPHERD: “I’m afraid it’s apt to lead a
young girl astray.”
GIRL: “Oh, no! No girl ever came a ‘cropper’
yet who put the brake on in time.”
In Melbourne, the other day, Miss Amelia
King obtained £lOO damages for breach of
promise of marriage by Emanuel Ring.
The sooner it is realised that the N.S.W.
“ society ” idea of running a Queen Victoria Con-
sumptive Home is a fizzle, the sooner will some-
thing really humane be done for the sufferers con-
cerned. It is the duty of Govt, to take the care
of indigent consumptives out of the hands of
private philanthropy, especially when that philan-
thropy shows itself unequal to the responsibility.
There are over 3000 consumptives in N.S.W.,
every one of whom is to some, and many to a
great extent, a disseminator of disease as well as a
personal sufferer, and no portion of the State
revenue can be better spent than in housing and
medically caring for those unable to pay for their
own needs; always provided that the foreign con-
sumptive is kept out to begin with.
* * * *
The catching of the sharks “shepherd-
ing” the “ salmon’’-shoals off Bondi (Sydney)
beach pays curious di vs. One sea-lawyer's stomach
yielded two gold rings and a gold scarf-pin, and
another’s a silver watch, “ black and slime-
covered, but (join/j ! ” which panned out £2. The
lucky captor asserts that he had once a pair of
shoes which had been tanned in a shark’s innards.
There is a beautiful and unusual spectacle to be
seen at Bondi, just now, when the sun strikes low
from the west. Every now and then, as a big
roller towers to its highest just before breaking,
within its face are to be seen thousands of quiver-
ing, glittering objects—the “salmon ” which have
been forced inshore by the sharks, and which, by
the passage of the sun-rays through the watery-
medium, have their silver sheen turned to a splen-
did golden green. By the way, The Bulletin
has in type a charming sketch by Roderie Quinn
dealing with the shepherding of the salmon by the
sharks—the drovers of the sea.

Is there aught after death ? Yes, Probate.
The baleful spread of education was de-
monstrated at Redfern, Sydney, the other day,
when a laborer excused himself for not working
on the plea that someone was having
an occult influence on him. Some
day we will see poor fellows wan-
dering up and down George-street,
unable to drink beer because they
have been hypnotised. About which
time a volcano will break out in
Surry Hills, and the sea-serpent
will come ashore and get tangled
up in the Oeean-st. cable-tram.

On a Southern goldfield, once,
“ the man under the bed ” actu-
ally did turn up. Loud screeches
were heard one Sunday night
from an elderly spinster, living
alone, and sure enough there was
a man under her bed. On being
yanked out, he proved to be an
eccentric local character whose
“soft” spot was prospecting
and the belief that ultimately
he was to stir up the country
with the discovery of a
fabulously rich reef. Un-
der the bed he had been
assiduously attacking
theskirti rig-board with
a broken chair-leg,
and pathetically
told his captors that
“It was all right
this time ; there was a hard bar
in ihe face, but the lode was
ju»t behind it.’ 1 The spinster
seemed to think that she should
have her prize by right of dis-
covery, but the unsympathetic
authorities removed him to the
lunatic asylum.
• * *
A Japanese states that the
Japanese intestinal canal is half as
long again as the European, and thus
accounts for the Jap's enormous rice
consumption. Which recalls a Victo-
rian-Hibernian medico’s humorous asser-
tion that his over ...upie binjie was the
result of “ another injustice to Ire-
land,” because, as he put it, the brutal and
bloated Saxon, by devouring the meat and thus
restricting his (the doc’s) forbears to a distending
vegetable diet, had produced in them and him an
intestinal elongation which removed him person-
ally from the regions of romance and deprived him
of a coveted interest in the eyes of the fair sex.
* * * *
The finding of an unsuspected inch-long
hob-nail in the skull of a deceased Victorian female
lunatic is more than paralleled by the case of an
inmate of N.S.W. Gladesville asylum, who was
found to have driven a French nail up to the
head in his cranium. Even more wonderful was
the case of a man who, after shooting himself
through the brain in Sydney Domain, walked over
and presented himself in the surgery of a Mac-
quarie-street medico. [He died within'three days.]
It will be remembered, also, that N.S. Wales P.M.
Norton lived over a year after being shot throii‘"h
the brain by a woman ; but stranger than all, per-
haps, was the ease of the Melb. youth who, after
shooting a ramrod clean through his head, was
able to walk to the hospital, and was eventually
turned out cured. . Yet another man dies of a
little spot of clotted blood in his brain, or a tap
from an umbrella !
* * * *
It would seem that Mr. David Mitchell,
who lately announced his intention of bequeathing
his unique library of 30,000 vols. to the N.S.W.
public, is stirring up Government to at once
provide suitable housing therefor. But, wouldn’t
it be rather unbusinesslike for Govt, to start
building a receptacle for what has not been
definitively handed over to it? And be this said
without the shadow of an imputation on Mr.
Mitchell, whose word in public estimation stands
equal to his bond.

A Melbourne wife, giving evidence in her
suit for divorce from her vanished Charley, stated
that in the latter days of their connubial life he
was “jolly to everybody else but her.” And
she ?
Two Rings Amelia had in view
When first her “ Yes ” was spoken—
The man Ring, seeming good and true,
And ring, the marriage token.
But both are gone ; Emanuel
Accounts for her dejection.
When called upon to ring the belle
He wrung her young affection.
Which Ring did ’Melia dearer hold—
The man, with ardent kisses,
Or e'en the promised hoop of gold
That makes a maid a missis ?
No matter, compensating Fate
Sweet solace soon arranges,
And ’Melia may find a mate
On whom to ring love’s changes.
Sing a ditty melancholy
Of a hubby gay and jolly
To all except the woman who attracted him of yore.
When, with jealous eyes and tearful,
She beholds him looking cheerful,
She fancies he’s forgotten that he’s not a bachelor.
He is ever bright and pleasant
If another girl be present,
Or a pal looks in to take him for an after-dinner walk;
But he hasn’t heart for joking
When he sits at home a-smoking,
Saying “ Yes ’’ or “ No ” or “ Fancy ! ” whilst the missus
does the talk.
So, her eyelids growing redder,
Mary fain would be a “ deader,”
Which were better far than living with the “partner of
her joys.’’
Still she feigns a wish to please him,
Though she’s cold enough to freeze him,
And can offer no distractions like the barmaids and the
“ boys.”
Ah ! the man is merely human,
And the wife is—just a woman,
And they took their chance together in the ordinary
course :
Having played at “ Ducky-deary”
Till their spirits both grew weary,
The only courtship left them is in seeking a divorce.

A steamer leaving Melbourne on Cup-day
advertised for a doctor, but had to postpone sail-
ing till after Steeplechase day, as no medico would
start till he had had his gamble.
“ Medicus ” : It is well-known amongst
doctors that the most honorable and straight-
forward patients are - Chinamen ! Unknown
amongst them all the little tricks of calling in a
professional rival to say whether the treatment is
ri<rht (to do the profession justice, he 11 seldom go),
of surreptitiously visiting quacks, thereby up-
setting results of proper treatment, and then
blamin'* the doctor ; of returning an abusive reply
to a civil request for settlement. But your aver-
age Australian is “ fly ” my oath !

An old man hanged himself at Spudville,
Vic. the other day, and whilst he was still pulsat-
ing (’n the-rope several people gathered around and
discussed the situation in awed whispers, and let
him go hang. One of their number went for
police assistance and the benefit of clergy, and
tlin others watched to see that the suicide did notJ
escape. When the police arrived and cut the man
down he was just dead. At the inquest the on-
lookers explained that they did not cut the tope
because they understood that it was illegal to do
S: )—probably they were afraid of being sued for
the value of the rope by the would-be-suicide.
\ ou never know your luck.
* * « •
Where is the logic or fairness of com-
muting Bisson’s death-sentence to one of penal
* Tvitude for life? One can imagine he hears the
N.S.W. Cabinet arguing the point, with Sunday
Brunker moo-ing across the table at intervals.
“ Was he a murderer ? ” “ Dunno.” “ Was he
insane?” “Dunno.” “Shall we hang him?
“Dunno. Better not, perhaps.” “ Send him to
a lunatic asylum ? ” “ Dunno. Too much
trouble.” “ Beave him in gaol ? ’ Ye-e-e-es .
Moo-oo ! ” Yet, if Bisson wasn’t insane, no man
in Australia ever deserved banging more than he
did, and if the Reid Cabinet believed that lie
wasn’t insane, he is the last man in Australia it
would have reprieved. And if Bisson was insane
—think of the brutal horror of sentencing a man
to life-long punishment for a mental disease.

me he was now learning to be a barber, under the
tuition of a man who advertised that he would

took a hand you should just have seen the blood-
and-hair fly.”

A Sydney D.T. correspondent throws a'
pleasing light upon the ingredients that mostly
compose the hog that supplies the lard that some-
times makes the festive margarine : —“ Swine that
never taste aught but the semi-putrid offal or half-
boiled carrion. Think of the dog’s-flesh character
and origin of the lard and the kindred nostrums
made up from this unsavory sustenance ; and
of the ‘ mountebank lying,’ in season and out, of
those who sell this carrion-fed bacon or pork as
dairy produce ! ”

* * * *
In May last two burglaries in jewellers’-
shops happened at Perth, and particulars of the
stolen goods were sent East. In July theN.S.W.
police notified W.A. that two Frenchmen had
been arrested in Sydney, and a large quantity of
the missing goods recovered. The W. A. police
(to put it very mildly) had by this time decided
that they had made a mistake about the burglary.
The W.A. Govt., therefore, declined to send over
for the Frenchmen. N.S.W. Govt, replied, “If
you don’t, we shall send them over and land them
on the wharf at Fremantle, as we consider such
characters a menace to society while at large.”
Westralia prosecuted perforce, and the Gauls got
seven years apiece. And you ought now to see
the faces of some of the lofty police officials ! The
matter will probably be brought up in W.A. .Par-
liament.
*** * .
Downer, Q.C., in melancholy opposition
to Kingston’s Law Reform Bill, which shuts the
University out of the training of barristers and
solicitors, asserts that it will soon be unnecessary
for a Crow-eater to be able to read and write.
The eminent Q.C. named told S.A. Assembly,
during the discussion of Kingston’s clause to do
away with the necessity for youths matriculating
before getting their law-articles, that the only
question asked him by Judge Gwynne when he
applied for his articles was : “ Are you the young
fellow that went to school with my son Ted?”
Yet Downer thinks the University examination
should be retained.
« * * *
The other night, on the Mosman, N.Q.,
a hideous one-eyed plantation kanaka tried to
break into the bedroom of a Mrs. O’Donoghue,
whose husband (a miner) was absent. The plucky
woman fired three revolver shots at the intruder,
who was afterwards found, wounded in the breast,
some 40yds. away. The woman was charged,
before Mr, Donnelly, P.M. (Polynesian inspector),
with “ shooting with intent,” and, though the
wounded kanaka freely confessed his evil inten-
tions, she was remanded for a week—and kept
nearly 48 hours in the look-up before being allowed
bail, although she has four small children—one
under 12 months. The residents held an indigna-
tion meeting, at which funds were collected for
Mrs. O’Donoghue’s defence. The local paper
further reports that a warrant for the arrest of
the nigger was refused. If the local autho-
rities don’t show a lot more tact in dealing with
white women, especially miners’wives, under such
circumstances, there will probably be niggers
dangling at the end of string in the North. What
sort of fuss would there have been if the attempt
had been made upon, say, the wife of the central-
mill manager. Would she have been refused bail
had she been plucky enough to defend her honor
with a six-shooter ?

A man up Maryborough (Q.) way was lately
fined .£4O for selling “ wine ” in a prohibited area.
The liquor was found in a galvanised-iron tank,
between ceiling and roof of dwelling, and was
tested by Gabriel (of Mt. Morgan fame). He put
less than half an ounce of the “ wine ” into GOoz.
water, and dropped in a frog, which survived just
20min. The decoction was being sold to kanakas
on surrounding plantations.

* • * * *
That venerable bush inhabitant who never
saw a railway or a bike or a wood-blocked road or
a hansom cab, cropped up again last week. His
name was “ Scotch Bill,” and he had fossicked for
40 years on Scotch Bill’s Creek near Bright (Vic.).
In all that time he had never been even to Bright,
and Melbourne was nothing but a rumor to the old
man. A few days ago the police came and re-
moved Scotch Bill on a charge of old age, rheu-
matism and bad luck, and humped him
laboriously down the mountains on a lit-
ter. When he saw his first locomotive he
nearly had a fit, but when a lady rode past
on a bicycle Scotch Bill sat up with difficulty
and explained that his life had been wasted ; he
had been asleep and a mere vegetable all his days
on Scotch Bill’s Creek, whereas he might have
gone into Bright once a month and seen things,
and mixed with the dizzy whirl of society. Bill
was then removed to the benevolent asylum at
Beechworth, which seems a pity. His country
might surely get him into a Melbourne institu-
tion, so that he could spend his declining years
watching the cable-trams go by, and could totter
out now and then to see the shops and the big
policemen. Poor old Bill l had barely time to
know that he was alive before they buried him
again.

Buller (M.L.) Miner, when asked by the
local corps of the Salvation Army for a subscrip-
tion, responded with this soul-cheering hymn :
SALVATION MINING HYMN.
(Respectfully Dedicated to the Salvation Army.)
Are you pegging out your claim in the New Jerusalem ?
Are you taking out your miner’s right'on high?
Are you going to get your portion in the wealth that
waits for them
Who shall seek the Eldorado of the sky ?
Will you join the rush to Zion ? There is room, they say,
for all,
And it goes ten thousand ounces to the ton,
In a pure and perfect country where no evil can befall,
With a climate bathed for ever in the sun.
Through its reefs of rarest crystal runs the gold in solid
veins,
And its gleaming sands are marvellous to see,
And the nuggets lie like boulders all along the shining
plains;'
Will you hump your swag to Gloryland with me?
For there no salted wild-cats wake the slumbers, of the
blest,
Not: duffer claims deface the happy shore ;
Where the jumpers cease from troubling, and the rouse-
abouts shall rest,
And the dead-beat draw his divs. for evermore.
Will you pad the hoof to Zion where the bright bonanzas
J lie,
With a rich and rare and never-ending yield ;
And a sure eternal title waits for all who will apply
To Immanuel, the Warden of the Field ?

P®X r f, f l ‘? VlC f tona Monday afternoon,
under the direction of Lady Hampden, with the
object of getting forward a few stages the
burning question of compulsory cooking in state
scnoois. l here are now two camps of reformers
anxious to shed a juicy tenderness over the chop
and steak of the working man of the future. One
party, the I\ational Council of Women, recently
deputationised the Minister of Education, with
the object of taking steps to minimise our national
dyspepsia, and at Monday’s gathering another
scheme for turning third-standard girls into
juvenile Soyers was put forward by Miss M.
Manning. Result of . the meeting was that
another deputation will shortly file into the
Minister’s room ready to advocate its own
special brand of cook-stove and gridiron. One
speaker mourned that a sizeful obstacle to
girls taking lessons at the schools under existing
? , J rai \B'®™ en t s w as that mothers preferred their
Maud v lolets and Irene Phyllises learning French
and music to dabbling in dish-water and chopping
carrots in school-hours. Another sensible woman
rose to remark that the school-kitchens should
be furnished cl la “ the old bush home,” otherwise
the girls, after a course of culinary high-art with
a steamer and a fish-kettle, would break down
badly if asked to prepare dad’s dinner with no
better utensils than a rusty frying-pan and a
lid less saucepan. Also, it’s a useless to compli-
cate things with elaborate stoves and ranges,
when the humble “ colonial oven ” is what fills
i m °st ratepayers. One resolution of
the scheme ambitiously referred to the hoped-for
establishment of a chair of cookery at the Uni-
versity, but a wise amendment wiped out this
notion of putting mutton and mathematics on the
same level.
. 0n great cookery topic I should like to put
in my humble little say, that the first great princi-
ple of cooking is having anything to cook. Com-
pulsory enthusiasts would treat all school-girls
alike to a course of plain and fancy tucker-prepara-
tion, shutting their eyes to the fact that while
things are made smooth for the town girl by the
baker and the ham-shop and fish-shop, so that
she can pull through fairly if she can boil a potato
and make tea, the country child has to learn the
mysteries of bread and butter and cheese and jam-
making, while its back-blocks family is truly
grateful if it can ring the changes on tin-dog and
damper. However, it is a great subject entirely,
and the present agitators will win a thankful
country s blessing if they can succeed in shifting
the nightmare of indigestion an inch or two off the
nation s chest.
Government House is putting its shoulder to the
wheel energetically to push along the Consump-
tive Hospital movement. A gratified section of
local sassiety has received missives in Her Ex. ’s
own hand-of-write inviting them to a meeting on
I hursday m the Govt, House best parlor to con-
sider a scheme which Lady Hampden wishes to
make a big thing of. It is to be a monster fair on
wind-raising lines next August. So there will be
plenty time to work it up.
Sydney pleasure-seekers are fast returning home
from the joys of Flemington, looking rather jaded
from trying so many assorted samples of Mel-
bourne climate, but full of chat about the festivi-
ties of Cup-time. They do say that some guber-
natonal frisks were very giddy indeed, and much
talk is flying round concerning one particularly
gorgeous ball to which a visiting potentate de-
spatched her ladyship, but from which he ab-
sented himself, having other fish to fry else-
where, At the whirl a Potts Point matron “got
into the papers ” on account of her neck-and-
shoulder display. They cover up more in Mel-
bourne, it seems. But the most meaningful and
the selectest revels didn’t get into the papers—
they never do. Another Sydney matron set pre-
cedence at naught and distracted officially-minded
aides by prancing with her partner into supper
ahead of Govs, and their wives.
