Mr. J. A. Pollock, who graduated at Sydney
University m science in 1889, is spoken of as a
certainty for the vacant Professorship of Physics.
Mr. Pollock is a slight, boyish-looking man of 35
or so, and is remembered as having held countless
numbers enthralled at the last meeting of the Aus-
tralian Association for _ the Advancement of
Science (A.A.A.S.) by his demonstration of the
system of wireless telegraphy. A few protests
are heard against mere lecturers becoming full-
blown Professors. It has been done in Sydney at
least twice before—in the cases of Prof. Anderson
and Prof. Butler, each of whom is held to be an
emphatic success in his own department.
Prof. Wood’s tour in Europe next year will not
be entirely devoted to historical research—the
romantic element will enter largely into the trip.
Before the end of the year, the Prof, mil be
married to Miss Madeline Whitfeld, an exceed-
ingly popular girl-graduate, who carried all before
her in the way of scholarships when at the Uni-
versity. Miss Whitfeld will be the third ex-
college student to marry this year, Miss Anderson
and Miss De Lissa having lately become Mrs.
Ashton and Mrs. Bensusan respectively.
There is a story current that a legal dignitary
who has just got sick-leave doesn’t expect to die.
He expects to recover quickly and get married to
a very charming woman just about half his own
age, who was at least once engaged to a dashing
member of a certain gubernatorial staff.
What constitutes a “ society sham ? ” A young
man advertised for a wife the other day. The
main points of the advt. were that he hated society
shams, that he was a student, and that his annual
income was £7OO. Not being able to find a wife
who was not a “sham” amongst his extensive
circle of acquaintances he advertised for a suitable
article—and found himself married to one within
a week. The irony of this true story is found in
the fact that the acquired wife was engaged to be
married to a worthy young man in business with an
income very much under £7OO a year. It is now
the jilted one’s turn to be cynical.
. One of our big benevolent societies lately got
into a curious hole. Its honorary solicitor and
best committee-man died, and the committee, in
recognition of his services, offered the position to
his eldest son, a highly reputable solicitor. The
secretary addressed the nomination to Mr. Alfred
, imagining that the eldest son was called
after his father. The committee was horribly
startled when an impressive young swell arrived.
And just now the committee is jumping on its secre-
tary, the senior partner jumping on the committee,
and the secretary jumping on his hat. For these
prim old gentlemen of 70 find it impossible to dis-
cuss matters connected with their female patients
before a bright young man in checks. Should this
meet the eye of the young gentleman aforesaid,
perhaps he will see a way of relieving the worry
of the estimable old blushers.
On the secession of “ Joseph of Canaan ”
Walters from Hyde Park Unitarian sermon-shop,
Rev. Zillman comes in as shepherd. He has had
a varied career, having worked round to his pre-
sent Unitarian belief via Anglican and other sects.
Mrs. Z. (No. 4 of the title) is a daughter of a
well-known Melb. identity, deceased Capt. Ful-
larton, late Port Melbourne harbor-master.
Her Majesty’s d.c. on Saturday evening pre-
sented a gay and festive appearance for an off-
season show night. Dotted about were many
familiar faces of confirmed playgoers. A black-
and-blue lady looked about the smartest in a big
theatre-party; that year-old color harmony is still
in force. A Sydney girl, just returned from a
trip ’Ome, also appeared in the two tints—two
long, sky-blue chiffon tails floated to the edge of
her skirt, and her hair was dabbed about with
azure bows. Also, her hair-knob was “right.” I
am told that, in Paris, Victor Hugo (the barber,
not the poet of that ilk) now refuses to dress hair
on the n i,pe of the neck. The correct way is for
the hairpins to stab it just into the hollow of the
head. On Saturday night most women had their
knobs either above or below Plimsoll-mark, there-
by putting on ten years to their age. In a box
ever-green Kilmorey was playing moth to a youth-
ful matron trimmed with skeeter-net frills in
bright green. Concerning the stage clothes, it is
a sad fact that while the imported players have
shaken down into their cavalier raiment, “love-
locks and all,” Australian members of the caste
continue to look less at home in ancient fashions
than a new-chum does on the back of a bucking
brumby. The women all wear the garb as to the
manner born, but the native men, with all their
tine physique, look as awkward in the togs as if
they had been dressed up by Dunning for a skat-
ing-rink carnival.
A new member of the Dampier co. is Miss Roxy
Barton, one of the Russell-Barton family, of Five
Dock., She is one of the many society girls whose
ambition it is to make a name and fame for them-
selves in the profesh. In “The Duchess of Cool-
gardie” she looks handsome and not too stage-
frightened, draws herself up, and inflects her voice
in the Maud Jeffreys manner.
A correspondent, under the cloak of “Chorus,”
writes me some interesting gossip about Julius
Knight and Ada Ferrar. It seems that the
Kmght-Ferrar feud” is a misnomer, as Mr.
Knight is on as good terms with Miss Ferrar as
with the rest of the company. The lady is parti-
cularly popular with her colleagues, is a refined
and peaceable being, and it doesn’t seem fair to
let her suffer from the stigma of a feud -with Julius,
the idol of the world—when in front of the foot-
lights.
, Miss. Maud Fitz-Stubbs gave a concert on Friday
night in the Y.M.C.A. hall, which attracted a
crowded and enthusiastic invited audience. More
men than are generally lured into a musical shivoo
tempered the womanishness of the crowd, and
most of the items were bright and shining suc-
cesses, which brought a regular procession of
flowers up the centre aisle. A baby violinist,
Keith Kennedy, aged about one, toddled on the
platform and wrestled like an infant Hercules
with a difficult piece, double-stopping and all,
though, he was hardly old enough to carry his
little fiddle unaided. But the chief focus of all
the opera-glasses was on Premier Reid, who, with
Mrs. R., sat throughout the programme like a
little man, and listened to a tuneful and rhyth-
mical waltz composed in his honor by Miss Fitz-
Stubbs. His verdict was printed on the bill-of-
fare “ The more I hear the ‘ Premier Waltz ’ the
better I like it.” The above is recorded as the
one thing, on earth that G.H.R. really has a
definite opinion about.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
The quality of the entertainment Gov. Brassey
provided for his guests, at the recent Cup festivi-
ties, was far superior to anything he did before he
went to England. There was no stint, and every-
thing was of the best. Of course, the uninvited
are hard to please in such matters, and the hard
remarks one hears usually come from those who
were not present. Apparently, His Ex. is going
to make a bid for popularity. He has not (up to
date) threatened any address on British trade, or
the navy, and he has been most genial and
gracious all round.
“Oneway of expressing joy, as well as anger,
is to smash things, and hang the expense. I have
heard that, in days of old, the British aristocracy
made a point of sitting down rapturously on its
belltopper when it scored a big win at the races.
A certain tall and conspicuous young married lady,
having pulled off £lOOO (it is said) over the Cup,
proceeded to vent her feelings on her long gold
chain, which she tore from her neck, broke into
little bits, and flung to the winds. The tem-
pestuous creature had been pining for a trip to
London, and this was, perhaps, her little way of
indicating that she was now off the chain “ on her
own,’’unless hubby would join the expedition to
foreign parts. Hubby, however, has definitely
decided to bear his better-half company, and the
broken valuable has been replaced.
“Bijou dress-circle looked ‘oh so bright!’ as
the hymn says, on Vice-Regal command night,
Tuesday (Nov. 8). Four Governors (each with his
order on its red ribbon tied up tight round his
neck like a ‘ good boy badge ’) brought along their
women-kind —a scant-enough allowance of skirt to
leaven that lump of suite. Only three girls to
nine or ten bachelors ! Petticoat-pet Hampden
openly shirked the over manned seance—and small
blame to him, considering that three out of eight
vice-regal ladies as his own lawful assets are
habitually available, minus the trouble of contest-
ing their society with 15 naval competitors. So
there sat the cute Viscount enthroned snugly in
Mrs. Baronet Clarke’s box, cheek-by-jowl with his
handsome hostess, another lady in vivid pink, and
a couple of beardless boys. 1 write ‘enthroned’
advisedly, because that night the mistress of
Rupertswood seemed to pose as Queen of the
occasion. A diamond tiara sat like a crown
on her dark head, and barring crimson or
orange, she couldn’t well have chosen a
more conspicuous color for her deeollete
satin gown tha/i its intense emerald-green or rich
royal blue (impossible to decide which, by arti-
ficial light). Anyway, the hue was not particu-
larly becoming to its brunette wearer. S’Rupert’s
liege lady habitually affects greens, blues, or blends
thereof, with signal success, but she grows over-
audacious in her color-experiments ; and whether
owing to a mistaken shade or the cold white glitter
of diamonds, ‘ Amy, Lady Clarke, ’ as she may or
may not be called some day, looked as if she might
have done worse than follow the example of Miss
Murphy, of Killaloe, ‘ who put the powther on, in
case the bhoys should say that she was schwartliy. ’
“Bijou vice-regal box is a cave of winds, and
Lady Brassey was muffled up so as to seclude all
toilette landmarks, except an upstanding aigrette
on jewelled stem and extra-fiery diamond necklet,
rendered less trying by black velvet neckband.
Her grey-haired Victorian Ex. boasts neither com-
plexion nor roundness nowadays, though at 20,
’tis said, a more winsome aristocrat never breathed.
Up to the present, however, her manners are rich
in ‘ that repose which stamps the caste of Vere de
Vere.’ She is so uniformly elegant of deportment
that buxom belles of the moment seem lolloping
and unschooled by comparison.
“ Unlike his sometime substitute, the vice-regal
Ancient Mariner doesn’t pin his admiration to
plump charms. Grace and style characterised his
matrimonial choice both times, and Mrs. Freeman-
Thomas, the only finished sample of his up-bring-
ing available for local reference, is undeniably
smart. Naturally, therefore, his brusqueness set
no store t’other night by Vara Renny, with that
sidling stage gait of hers, and head see-sawing
archly all the time from one bare shrugged
shoulder to t’other. The irritable patriarch
growled audibly till the offender had finished her
turn and wriggled off. Vara is not one of
‘The Liars,’ by the way. In fact, I fancy she
represents the one pure pearl of womanhood in
that setting of society Sapphiras. Probably her
stage hubby-elect, Baronet Brough, would catch
it hotter from his sugary gusher in the long run
than did the matrimonial victims of all those
other smart-tongued fibbers put together. To go
back to the Broughs’ glittersome audience : Mrs.
Geo. Chirnside, in her box, looked like a picture—
pale-blue gown, soft white empire-fichu folded
round her bare shoulders, and black plumes in her
fair hair. Miss Dorothy Brand wore the simplest
of unrelieved black frocks. Mrs. Packenham,
wife of one of the new Brassey attaches, is a slight,
fair, youngish matron, with a kind of clear-cut
nose to set off pince-nez by-and-bye. She had a
black lace zouave over her white silk bodice, while
Lady Lamington looked gentle and dowdy,
ensconced in cream silk pelisse with a sprinkling
of diamonds glimmering in a glow-wormish way. °
“ One of the Governors seemed rather serious re
the wretchedness of that stage Romeo strung on
by a married Juliet. Maybe he could have entered
still more thoroughly into the tragedy had Romeo
worn the hobbles. And when Mrs. Brough is
asked ‘ls there any flirting in Central Africa?’ a
senior vice-regal cynic tolled forth such a hollow,
ironical laugh. Insensate hearers stared, giggled,
and buzzed delightedly. ‘ He-he ! Lord Z ’s
laughing 1 ’ To many ears, however, that mirth-
less ha-ha seemed to say ‘ No flirtin’ anywhere, for
wretches in our position ! ’ Paddy-whack Haigh,
with scarlet revers to his aide-de-camp coat,
beamed like a red sunset, and very likely would
have made a bush-fire look pale if there’d been
enough petticoat to go round.
“Gov. Jerry Smith knows how to mix his suite-
salad all right. As an off-set to Erin-go-bragh
aforesaid, Private-Sec. Ord is a gentle creature
with a touching silvery streak showing above each
ear. Hon. Spencer Lyttleton, not so young as he
once was, sports a profusion of dishevelled whiskers
and top-thatch, a tiny bit in the manner of Speaker
Mason. The latter, by the way, it seems, spread
his plumes in great style, t’other day, and in his
capacity of Mighty Potentate led a party of Par-
liamentary naicest on board a war ship in the
bay, by invitation, he thought, bio ceremonial
pomp of welcome, no hosts, no fizz, no tea, no
nothink. The Admiral never turned up at the
Mayor’s dinner, either —order of toasts presum-
ably unsatisfactory.
“ The Lamingtons spent the tag-end of their
Melb. visit at Healesville, riding and driving across
country with Miss Alice Brand and Lord Richard
Nevill. Their Victorian Excellencies are so fond
of ‘Lord Richard.’ And, indeed, he seems quite
admirable enough ! One can hardly picture him
without a heap of Brassey wraps hanging over his
slender arm. And when the Old Man of the Sea,
in or out of season, complains to his surrounding
juniors of a draught, it’s always left to the good
boy of the batch to burrow for that venerable
rain-cape and help its owner into it. Captain
Frewen, the new aide, has his share of good
humor, too, judging by looks. Poor soul ! He
always seems to arrive in a lone, lorn vehicle long
after the rest of the party, and hovers distractedly
on their outskirts until the lordling aforesaid
points out a seat to be gained by climbing over two
female and four male guests.
“ American journals announce that the Duke of
Manchester, now or lately in Australia, is to
marry Miss May Goelet, of New York. The lady
will possess about four million dollars when she
marries, and as hard-up dukes always want to
marry cash, the story is credible. Moreover, the
future Mrs. Montagu is said to be a pretty girl—a
small detail for a dook as compared with the
dollars, but still worthy of mention. Manchester
has been popular in Melbourne as a genial youth.
Australian girls are warned off, for he is not likely
to let-goe of Miss Goelet and her four millions.
“ Premier Turner errs somewhat on the side of
economical ’umbleness. When Gov. Brassey was
returning to Melbourne, the Premier, wife
and daughter footed it along Bourke-street to
Spencer-street rail way-station, Miss Turner armed
with a huge bouquet, which was, presumably,
intended for Lady Brassey. The family party
would have been less conspicuous in a carriage.
The grovel that is worth doing at all is worth doing
in sassiety style.
“At the Salon, recently, a lady professor of
fencing showed her professional paces in conjunc-
tion with two small girl pupils. The trio wore
scant black velvet knickers, white blouses, zouaves
and red sashes. The juveniles with the long curls
looked rather like little princes in the Tower, but
their teacher would have been none the worse for
a basque to her blouse or tails to her coat or some-
thing. She began by extolling foil-drill as a
deportment-gracer and figure-developer, and it’s
all that, I should say, judging by a casual survey
of its exponent. But if you’re one of the ‘develop-
ing ’ sort there are spectacular objections to tight
velvet pants for cold-blooded afternoon disulav.
“At a conversazione at Prahran Town Hall
the other night, farewell was bidden to Mrs. Bear-
Crawford, who is off to England for a trip. Mrs.
8.C., both as Miss Bear and since, has found much
of the money and energy for the Vic. Women’s
franchise movement. The lady’s revenue comes
from the Chateau Tahbilk vineyard, although she
is something of a shining light in temperance
circles. She always dresses in grey, and figured
before Parliament recently, when a M.P. opposed
to the women’s vote accused her of writing anony-
mous letters to country papers, in which she
affected to write as an indignant constituent of
members who were opposed to the Bill. A. very
energetic lady is Mrs Bear-Crawford.
‘ ‘ A shining member of the racks of Melbourne
Johnniedom, who always flatters himself that he
is appropriately dressed on every occasion, is
still noticeable by the crestfallen air which
he brought back from England. Ere start-
ing to London by the ill-fated China, this
Adonis laid in a stock of suitable attire for the
voyage—packing 40 suits in all. The poor fellow
almost tore his hair when the vessel ran ashore at
Perim to find that after all Fate had got the best
of him—he had forgotten to pack a ‘ wreck-suit ’ i
“Dr. Amy Castilla, the Melbourne lady-
medico, whose illness was alluded to in The Bul-
letin a few weeks ago, is dead. Her case was
hopeless from the first. The death of the red-
haired little woman is a bit of hard luck, after her
tremendous battle for success. She worked at
teaching all day while studying for her degree.
“ Two songs and a waltz from Glen and Co.
(Melb.) are not startling efforts in local composi-
tion, although the waltz (‘The Cliveden,’ if you
please) may be expected to ‘ catch on. ’ This is by
Arthur Smith, who was responsible for the Hope-
toun Polka of yore, and it is scored with a
pleasing lilt. Oyston’s Band will play ‘ The
Cliveden ’at all the forthcoming whirls. ‘ The
Dear Old Home ’ ballad has a melody of rather
better quality than the words, whilst ‘ She is far
from the Land, ’ arranged to Tom Moore’s verse,
and dedicated to the People of Ireland, is stronger
in words than in music. If the distressful
counthry accepts the dedication it will be mainly
on account of dear old Tom.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
An ancient in harness is Dr. Hansard, of
Ashfield (Sydney), aged about 90 years, who has
been a qualified medico for 63 years.
On Melb. Stock Exchange Lord Kilmorey
(he pronounces his name Kil-murray) is known as
Lord Kill-money, from the number of non-divi-
dend paying cos. with which he has had the bad
luck to be connected.
Julian Salomons, again getting ready for
a run abroad, is the oldest practitioner at N.b.W.
j; ar —oldest from date of call. G. B. Barton was
called a year earlier, and H. C. Dangar seven
years earlier, but neither of these practise.
Rev. Nobbs, of Norfolk Island, was for-
merly in the British navy. Afterwards in the
Chilian service under Cochrane—Lord Dundonald.
A son of the excellent old man, also a parson, and
a bit of a sport, is now at Narandera, N.S. W.
Yic. postal department lost a good client
when old Baron Von Mueller died. People used
to laugh at the Baron when he would tighten
his comforter and complain about being
hardly able to live on the “ miserable
piddance” which Govt, allowed him,
said pittance being .£l2OO a year when
he started growling. But, in reality,
the poor old man did find it hard to
keep things going. Every day he
would pen between 10 and 30 letters to
various botanists of the world, and
nearly every letter would accompany
per same post several pounds of root,
bark, leaves, or wood taken from some aber-
ration of the vegetable world. In this way
the greater part of the Baron’s =£l2oo was
absorbed, and when Govt, docked his
screw he could scarcely make ends meet.
Fancy ! There’s another L. L.
Smith And he’s a sporting man, too —secre-
tary of Hobart Hunt Club, When Mel-
bourne’s L.L. was in Tasmania last year,
and got into the hands of the law touching
his registration as a medical-practitioner, he
insisted on seeing the Tasmanian L.L. When
they came face to face, the man from “the
Governor’s residence ” was silent a moment,
and then burst out : “Don’t you think your
existence is a damned impertinence ! ”
The street “ attack ” on N.S. Wales legis-
lator Carroll, about which such a flourish was at
first made, turned out to be nothing more serious
than an after-dark jostle by a couple of beery
sailors. The fact that the men were Swedes was
in itself strong prima-facie evidence in disproof of
robbery.
“ Melb.” . One of our cleverest young
lady doctors is this week committed to the grave.
For the curative person to be one with the vital
forces distilled out is an anomaly, but frequent.
I have yet to meet a good, hearty, juicy lady
medico, similar to the most familiar type of suc-
cessful male doctor.
Justice Cooper, retires from the
Queensland Bench on sick leave, after 15
years service, vaulted on to his seat out of a
brief and not glorious Attorney-Generalship, thus
continuing a bad old practice. During his career
as a Judge he dressed better and hung more than
any of his colleagues.
Chief Justice Way, S.A.’s ornate and
gushing Acting-Governor, is believed to be a cer-
tainty for a new-year knighthood. The C.J.
has frequently declined the bauble, his enemies
alleging that he appraises himself at nothing below
a baronetcy, but if there is no sign of the
baronetcy it is better to take what’s going.
There is living on an East-coast (Tas.)
mining-field a man of phenomenally-mixed blood.
He is son of a Chinese, and his mother’s father was
son of a Hindoo father and an English mother;
her mother was a Frenchman’s daughter from
a Creole wife. He is mentioned as clever and
well-read, and has twice represented his district in
an important public body.
Dr. Campbell, M.L.C., who died suddenly
in Adelaide, the other day, was a curious blend of
Democrat and Tory. He will be best remembered
in S.A. for his advocacy of the homestead-block
system which was adopted after much agitation,
and has been a wonderful success. The doctor
was one of the numerous medicos who were mixed
up in the far-reaching hospital row,
Ednie Brown, whom Parkes brought over
from South Australia to conserve N.S.W. forests
at a big screw, and who was presently dropped,
has his troubles in Westralia, too. Provision has
been made in the Estimates for only six months’
salary ; but the Minister explained that though
the official would retain the position, “ it is a good
thing for all of us at times to get a scare.”
The whirligig of time has spun to some
purpose in the North. A few years since and
Morehead, the acknowledged wit and satirist of
Q. Assembly, scared quondam friend and foe with
sudden quip and merciless mimicry. Dickson and
one other were constant butts. Now, Dickson,
and the other sit in the high places, whilst More-
head is a broken and prematurely-aged man.
A much-criticised Judge of N. S. Wales
D.C. Bench expresses himself heartily ready to
quit office on pension, being of opinion that he can
command a good place at the Bar, with a possi-
bility of reaching the Supreme Court Bench later.
He defies Govt, to shift him except on pension, and
therefore welcomes strictures which may hasten
the Cabinet to open the pension door for him.
Throssell, W.A. Lands Minister, is very
deaf. Uses a “recorder,” an instrument which
he holds between his teeth, and is so enabled to
hear the faintest whisper. He had this in position
one day when a M.P. introduced a candidate for
a ™ et ' “Now speak up,” said the M.P., “the
old fraud is as deaf as an adder.” “ Deafer than
forty adders, young man,” said Throssell. “But
n A/r^ OU rol, ble to holler.” And he smiled at
the M.P.- “Go ahead —the old fraud is all atten-
tion. ’ The boy got his billet, and the story was
sent around to the credit of Throssell and the
confusion of the M.P.
Governor J. B. Thurston, of Fiji, died of
throat-cancer, and those natives, who at a mighty
pow-wow offered up prayers that Thurston might
die of something horrible, are now acknowledging
that “after all there is a Go“d! ” A God that
answers prayer !
Canon Potter, of St. Kilda (Vic.), says
that the Anglican clergy of Sydney have little or
no humor. All the better for them. A keen
sense of humor would only make them miserable.
The augurs should not smile, even at each other,
in these days of doubt and suspicion.
Percy Spence, on his arrival in London,
at once fell into heaps of work. As a black-and-
white man, Spence’s quickness and phenomenally
accurate artistic memory, which enables him to
draw correctly out of his head, where other men
need models, gives him a great advantage.
PREDESTINATION.
(Sketch at a City Poultry-shop).
The Earl of Eustob, who will some day be
Duke of Grafton, was once associate to Chief
Justice Way in Adelaide. He was then known as
Colonel Fitzroy and he was in Australia for
“colonial experience.”
The fate of the children of Mrs. Walsh,
commonly called “Granny,’'who died atGlenlnnes
(N.S.W.), the other day, in her
91st year, excited thrilling specu-
lations half a century ago.
Then, and since, many would-be
authors endeavored to invest the
story with permanent literary
qualities, but in no case with
success. The simple facts are
the drama. Walsh, shepherd at
Moonbi station, New England,
out with his men on the run ;
Mrs. Walsh, at home in her hut,
washing ; two children, Willie
and Alfred, aged 4 and 5, play-
ing, a stonethrow from the hut.
Mrs. Walsh saw blacks—it was
the year 1843, and there was
bad blood between blacks and
whites. The mother came to the
door, missed the children, searched ; when tho
men came home at night, they also searched ;
searched fordays, weeks, years. It is 55
years since then, but no word of the boys
or oftheir fate has ever come to hand.
The late N.S.W. Judge Win-
deyer, while visiting London, was once
approached in the street by a gentleman
unknown to him who held out his hand
and said he was glad to see the Judge
again. “You have the advantage of
me,” remarked his Honor—“l am
sorry to say I do not remember you.”
“Don’t you recollect my appearing
before you at Sydney as a petitioner in
divorce ? ” enquired the stranger. “My
name is “Oh, yes,” remarked the
Judge, drily, “I trust that you are satisiied
with my decision, whatever it was.” “Well,”
replied the other, “ to tell you the truth, I’m not.
You divorced us, but we’ve got married again.”
Certain admirers of the late Q. Premier
are moving heaven and earth to get a statue
erected to him. If Q. Govt, is going to take any
steps in the matter of commemoration (as a recent
vote appears to indicate) it is only fit and proper
that some Democrat should rise and ask what is to
be done towards perpetuating the memory of the
late Charles Lilley—a man whose voice was always
lifted on behalf of the people—a man who was
deposed from his lofty position because he dared
to call a spade a spade and a thief a thief.
The death of G. W. Goyder, for over 40
years S. A. Surveyor-General, recalls that through
ignoring his advice hundreds of farmers are now
face to face with absolute ruin. Goyder mapped
out an imaginary line of rainfall 20 years ago in-
tended to mark the limit of country fitted for
agriculture. His advice was ignored, but his rain-
fall-line proved remarkably correct. His forecast
has been wonderfully verified this present season.
In the Upper North country there is nothing to
reap, whereas in the lower country there will be
an average of five bushels.
At the first ball given by Gov. Hotham
of early Victoria, the supper was of the sixpenny-
restaurant sort—plentiful but primitive.
Beer, port and sherry were the drinks.
A notable of the time asked a
flunkey for champagne. Hotham
overheard. “There is no
champagne, Mr. ,” said
he. “These people, sir, are
not accustomed to drink
champagne. Do you think
that, as Governor of this
young colony, I should be
justified m setting such an ex-
ample of extravagance ? ”
“ Oh, as to that, your Excel-
lency,” sa rd the other, “they
are too far gone in the vice.
You, sir, should know that,
for don’t they pay you =£7ooo
a-year ! Surely, sir, that is
going in for champagne, and
a poor brand at that.” And
Hotham looked round for a cor-
poral’s guard of marines.
Five years’ practice at N.S.W.
Bar qualifies a man for appointment to the
Supreme Court Bench. A fair average, as ap-
pointments go, is, however, about 20 years’ prac-
tice. Judge Dickinson, in the early days, was
appointed a N.S.W. puisne Judge four years
after being called to the Bar, but that was before
the new rule.
Nov. 4th was the 12th anniversary of the
death of N.S.W. Chief Justice Martin. His pre-
decessor in office, Alfred Stephen, only died about
four years ago. Both are interred in the church-
yard of St. Jude’s, Rand wick. Last week a hand-
some memorial was erected over the grave of
Stephen ; but Martin’s tomb is still unmarked.

his staff remembered the stipendiary magis-

Brolly— ■“ My God, I am proud of you ! ” But,
when they presented a similar message, the tele-
graph-office wasn’t taking any ; and “My colo-
nial ” had to be substituted.

would be likely to lead him at present.

COBBY DRAWS THE COLOR-LINE.
COBBY (returning hat given him by Mrs. Cockie):
“No fear me wear it that hat. Lucy say
me like-it a Chinaman.”
Dr. Allan Campbell, the S. Australian
M.L.C., who lately died, was as good a representa-
tive as Australia had of the modern philanthropic
English Conservative ; and therefore, as Sidney
Webb found in the N.S.W. capital, was far
removed from the hard Tory who owns the Leg.
Council. Cockburn, fascinated by the doctor’s
tenderness of heart and active unselfishness, went
so far as to create a new Executive office for him ;
but there was a row, and Dr. Campbell went out
hurriedly. His last public action was to superin-
tend the opening of a Convalescent Home in the
hills, in connection with the Children’s Hospital.
“ I have often dreamt of this,” he said ; “ now it
is a reality.” Next day he suddenly died.
The sudden death of George M'Cullagh
Reid, cabled from Maoriland, relieves from the
strain of providing “copy” a journalist whose
career was exceedingly chequered. Originally a
Presbyterian parson, he drifted into Queensland
politics in the mid-Sixties, but failed to make an
abiding reputation, and 1870 found him at Auck-
land (M.L.) with no capital but a head full of
ideas. Blue-eyed, pushing Henry Brett was ship-
ping-reporter of Auckland Herald, and to him
Reid proposed that they should start an evening
paper. The Auckland Star was consequently
born, Reid contributing a pair of scissors and a
gum-pot, and leaders that burnt into the manifold
local abuses. Brett's contribution was .£BO in
cold cash, a thorough knowledge of the printing
business, and indomitable energy. There was
already a well-established evening paper in the
town —a nice, mamby-pamby organ that just let
things slide. The Star introduced carrier-pigeons
as an accessory to its news-getting department,
made a specialty of mining-news, and ran the other
paper to a stand-still in a couple of years. 11 should
have been mentioned that old “Willie " Wilson
of the Herald helped Reid and Brett by
machining their bantling for the first few months.
The incident, however, which gave the Star its
first strong grip on public favor was a brawl. The
streets of Auckland after night-fall, in 1870, w ere
unsafe through the chartered hoodlumism of
scions of the “ very nicest ” families, who under
the nom de guerre of the “ Rollicking Rams ”
took charge of the city, smashed in windows,
pulled down shutters, and remorselessly “ plugged ’
the more sober-minded citizens who remonstrated.
The social influence of the “Rams” rendered
them immune from police interference. Then
Reid took the matter in hand, ridiculing and
abusing the ring-leaders by name.- Vhereupon
the “ Rams ” swore to wreck the Star
office. A great crowd turned out to see
the fun, and one afternoon just after
publication, a champion of the “ Rams ” strolled
airily into the Star office and asked Brett
with mock-civility the meaning of the article.
He then strolled round the counter to chastise
Brett but the latter, a long-armed man then of
great strength, met the gentleman “in the open-
ing ” with a smashing upper-cut which knocked
him out and cost him several teeth. The crowd
then chivvied the “Rams”upQueen-st. with much
jeering and cabbage-stalk while Reid improved
the occasion by hanging the routed one’s incisors
in the window accompanied by a placard “Teeth
extracted free of charge. Apply within.” The
Star’s circulation trebled that night and never
went back. After about three years with the
Star Reid’s restless disposition induced him to
sell his share to Brett for .£3OOO and he went off
to Dunedin hoping to repeat his Auckland
coup. He started a paper, but foolishly,
in the strongest trades-union town in the pro-
vince, he tried to introduce female labor into
his printing department; his capital vanished,
and his paper was dead in a few months. Revert-
ing to politics, he gained a seat in the House of
Representatives, but subsequently took an official
position in connection with immigration. The
fast 15 years of his life he devoted to journalism.
He was by turns leader-writer on Auckland
Herald (and whilst there incubated his world-
famous Noah’s Ark hoax), editor of a short-lived
evening paper in opposition to his . old partner
(Brett), editor for a brief space of Melbourne
Standard, then for a few years leader-writer on
Sydney Morning Herald— and now he has died
in harness on the staff of Auckland Herald.
Peace to his ashes !

I came some 20ft. to the surface perhaps a hundred

* * * *
Thompson was in the embryonic stage of
jackaroo. His first day in the saddle was a 20-
mile ride—horse a “liver-rouser ’’—thermometer 90
in the shade—tweed pants. That night he con-
fided to the cook that he was very sore, and the
cook kindly gave him a box of ointment, and told
him to rub it well in. Thompson did; but,
when he bunked, things began to hum. A gentle
glow became such a terrible red heat that Thomp-
son’s groans aroused all hands. Ointment
examined—Stevens’ blister, the only substitute for
firing horses !
‘ ‘ Maranoa ” : One evening 30 years ago I
pulled up at a Queensland district accommodation-
house, on the verandah of which sat an oldish
man, with a bottle of brandy in one hand and a
small billy in the other. We foregathered, and
his first words to the landlord were “ Bring
another bottle, Mike.” It came, and shortly
afterwards tea was announced. I went in ;he
didn t. After tea I went out to the verandah
again, and his next words were “Fetch another
Mike—it isn ta ‘ Coo ’ yet, is it ? ” Mike brought
it, saying “Oh, no, sir ; not quite a ‘Coo ’ yet.” I
had a bad head when I left next morning. Re-
turned to the place a few days afterwards. A
saddled horse stood at the door. Presently out
came my branched friend, who sprang into the
saddle, calling out, “ All right; come up next
week, Mike. ’ I went in, but hadn’t been there
ten minutes when, at a hand-gallop, back came the
horseman, who yelled, “Here, Mike, you know
you re a scoundrel, and will cheat me if you can,
so put it down on a piece of paper, and be quick.”
Mike put something on a piece of paper, handed
it to him, and off he went. Later, Mike got quite
confidential with me. Rubbing his hands, he
said, “ Good deal, that—five cows in six days—all
for drink. He never drinks more than five cows
at a time.” The squatter died wretchedly poor.
Mike is now himself a rich squatter. He never
“touched it.”

“ Tracker ” on healthy appetites :
Jack, a young Sydneyite ; Fred, a Queenslander, and
the writer were camped in the shadow of a mighty rock,
and, just before sundown, two native girls came up with
a fish of about 121 b., which they gave us in return for two
out of three magpie-geese we had shot. They roasted
and ate one goose, and we grilled our fish. We were al
pretty hungry. 1 ate about 21b. of fish, Fred about 3,
and Jack (still in his teens) all the remainder, certainly
51b. ; damper and tea as well. So no wonder the Queens-
lander said he was “as full as a tick.” But the Sydney
lad wanted to know if I wasn’t going to have the remain-
ing goose for supper ! “ Hang it all, man,” I said, “ take
what you want, and be satisfied for once.” Whereupon
he roughly plucked and cleaned the goose (at least 71b.
net), cut it in two, grilled one half and, to my astonish-
ment, polished it off, along with proportionate damper.
The blacks looked on in amazement at a whitefellow who
could thus beat them on their own ground, and at last
one of the gins crossed over to the performer, and, put-
ting her hand upon his stomach, observed, “My word,
you fat like ’em that young pellar buppalo when him
come alonga water when him big one tirsty.”

“T. Bend ” : Shot
head off whip-snake, once,
near Brisbane water-works,
and, coming to some blacks
camped round fire, offered
one of them the snake.
He asked first how it had
been killed, and, when told,
accepted it and laid it on
the tire till all the wriggle
was roasted out of it. Then
he curled it up and gave it
another two or three turns.
When it was done he took
it up and pulled it to pieces,
taking the skin off by sec-
tions and eating as he
skinned. Never offered his
mates a morsel, though he
asked me to have a bit.
Happening on a string of
eggs inside the snake he
remarked that they were
the best part of it; then
taking one end of the string
he held his head back and
let the whole slide down
his throat in an instant—
smacking his lips after-
wards.
“ Long-’Un ” on brush-turkey matters :
In answer to “ S.H.”. (Bulletin, 15/10/’9B), young brush
turkey (lowan, malle-hen, or native pheasant) as soon as
hatched works passage tail-first through about 2ft. of
mullock, flaps its apologies-for-wings until dry, and goes
out into the cold world on its little own. Robbing a nest
once came on a young ’un half-way down. Afterwards
great pals with poultry at home. Walking through scrub
once heard great row t’otherside brow of sandhill; crept
up and peeped ; 20yds. distant two lowans heaping up
nest; started 10 to 20yds. away, striking leaves and twigs
backwards with feet and wings. These birds build
mound in winter, after first heavy rain. Dig small hole
in ground, fill with wet sand, rotten leaves, &c., and
cover with sand about 3ft.-high, base dia. 10 to 15ft.
Start laying in Sept., and average about three eggs fort-
nightly ; opening and closing nest each time. Eggs laid
small end upwards (to give chick a start on upward jour-
ney), sometimes in layers of half-a-dozen. Once found 17
in one nest. Can tell good eggs by fresh pink color, but
when scratched and dull, beware ! Seldom more than
three eatable from one nest. Collected about 50 one
Sunday, and yanked ’em carefully miles to camp. Onlv
one in five good. Can catch this bird easily with aid c

“T.L.”: Re “S.H.’a ” query (B. 15/10/’9B).
Nine-tenths of the young brush turkeys (or would-
be young turkeys) come out in the stomachs of the
goannas that burrow into the mound and “ scoff ”
the eggs. The other tenth, if if has luck, just
crawls out through the tunnels made by the goan-
nas. If eggs weren’t eaten as aforesaid, you
wouldn t be able to see the grass for turkeys.
Reminds me : Have seen young turtle in the S.S.
islands oome out of sand. They scratch their way
to the surface, and, after a few moments’ hesita-
tion, make straight off for the ocean. If nine-
tenths of them didn’t get gobbled by fish the water
would be stiff with turtle. Often 200 eggs in a
nest. Have kept these little chaps from time of
hatching (size of a 2s. piece) till 18 months old,
and then they weren’t quite as big as a saucer—a
slowness of growth which points to a very long
life. 6
* * * *
The best bit of cattle-lifting on record is
credited to a bullock-puncher in a far-out Vic.
mining centre. A young fellow, son of aforesaid
puncher, was arrested for embezzling two steers.
The animals were secured, and put into the police-
paddock with a mob of 50 or 60 head at sun-
down. At daylight next day the steers were
gone, and they have never since been seen. To
get away with the cattle the lifter had to pick
them from a mob in the dark, and get them out
of a well-fenced paddock, the gate of which was
securely locked. The police-station adjoined the
paddock, and the attempt was expected. The
case against the prodigal son was dismissed, and
on returning home he is understood to have dined
off the fatted steer.

Dear Bulletin.— Re the “Sign of the
Cross.” I have seen here in California a half-
Chinee-half-American-negro. Had Ah Sin’s pro-
minent cheek-bones and slant eyes, combined with
Missa Johnsiner’s broad nose and thick lips ; color,
yellowish-brown. Told me he considered himself
far superior to either of his parents, and a notch or
two above the white man. Australia hasn’t yet
much to complain of in the way of crosses. In
New Orleans, not long ago, I noted the following :
Mulatto, quadroon, octoroon, nigger-creole, white-
creole, American-Chinee, and Spanish-Chinee. In
Texas and Arizona, you often come across the
American - Indian crosses and Mexican - Indian
crosses ; and the American-Mexican cross is com-
mon in border-towns. The most noticeable charac-
teristic of the crossbred in this country is the
female’s immorality, which becomes more" marked
as the cross deepens. Thus, the pure-blood negress
is strictly religious, mostly a hard-shell Baptist-
church three times a Sunday. The half-caste is a
lot less strait-laced; while, as for quadroon and
octoroon, they are seldom seen by day, as they
pursue their avocations at night. All due, in my
opinion, to the Southerners’ ineradicable abhor-
rence of the negro. The octoroon’s one-eighth
nigger makes the average white pass her by,
whereas she, being the other seven-eighths white,
shuns the nigger and nigger-crosses, and puts up
with the non-average white, who doesn’t mind
her nigger dash. Either he ruins (for want of a
better word) her, or more often she, being a big-
bosomed, voluptuous, splendid - eyed creature,
ruins him. From this stage it is a short step to
the Chinese quarter. —Veldt (’Frisco).
* * * *
“S.S.” : A Sunday-afternoon amusement
that I have seen practised by young men out in
the Lonesome is more entertaining than setting a
severed bull-ant to fight to a finish with his own
hind-quarters. Prospectors about Swift’s Creek,
Gippsland, catch jays and magpies in springs,
attach scraps of newspaper to their tails, and then
turn them loose. The jollification begins when
the decorated jay attempts to rejoin the jay com-
munity. The jays, apparently, are fiercely demo-
cratic, and object to tags of all sorts ; anyhow,
they pick on to the bird with the tag, and give
him a bad time, fairly hunt him bald if his docket
is not worried off meanwhile. By turning loose a
dozen or so magpies and jays so decorated it is
possible to fill a square mile of bush with racket
and fury and feathers and bird blasphemy.
Wanted, a Society for the Prevention of Cruelty
to Dicky Birds.

Popular Australian superstitions : .Snakes don’t die til

3“ Timi : ” Was at the tangi, or obsequies,
of Te Moananui, one of the principal chiefs of the
Ngatimaru tribes of Maoris, who died at his
settlement near Thames, M.L., in the late seven-
ties. The rangatira had been dead about a fort-
night (ugh !) at the time of my visit, and the
august corpse lay in an open coffin full of rum, one
wife at the head and another at the foot armed
with feather dusters, keeping the flies off. There
were 700 or 800 Maoris present who every hour
or so gathered round the coffin and wailed per-
functorily for the dead. The interest of most of
those present, however, was obviously centred in
the commissariat. The staple was an immense
wall of dried eels which I roughly measured at
50ft. long, 6ft. wide and 4ft. high. And they had
been steadily eating their way into it for a f6rt-
night, mind you! Its original dimensions were
probably half as much again. Then there were
two or three carcases of beef, dressed with the
blood in them, and most unappetising in appear-
ance and smell, and immense pieces of dried shark
so uniquely pungent as to easily supply the pre-
dominant note in the concatenation of aromas.
For the refreshment of pakeha visitors there was
baker’s bread and rum in buckets, pannikins being
tied to the handles with withes of flax.

Related that a snake, which lived under a
Hawkesbury farmer’s house, struck up a friend-
ship with an infant of the family, and when the
child was alone would leave its cover ancl let the
youngster fondle it. The terrified parents had
frequently watched proceedings from afar, but the
snake would make off on their approach. Even-
tually it was decapitated with a spade by the
farmer.

“ W.A.T.” : Having seen a tiger-snake
frequently about our hut we decided to try music
to entice him into the open, so we got an acquaint-
ance to place his auto-harp on a stump about six
yards from the hut while self and two others kept
handy with sticks. The harpist played “Home,
Sweet Home,” through twice; but no snake.
Then the musician tackled “See Saw,” and
had strummed half-way through the second time
when our lodger made his appearance and glode
straight tovvards the musician, whereupon we
made a strategic rear-movement and cut off
snake’s retreat. It measured 4ft. 3in.
STRONG-MAN SAMPSON
Dear Bulletin: — Be “Nell Cook” and
her two little pigs (Bulletin, 8/10/’9B). About
midway between Bulumwaal and Sheepstation
lives a miner’s wife, who trained two little pigs
to fetch wood for her. Often asked by sceptical
travellers to give an exhibition of the intelligence
of her pigs. She would say “Go and fetch me
some wood, quick! ” at the same time stamping
her foot, and the pigs would instantly run into
the scrub, each grab the first dry stick it came
across and drag it to the house. Talk about pigs
being pig-headed after this I Can any Bulletin
reader quote another instance of a pig being made
useful when alive ?— Fred. Bradshaw. [Pigs
have been trained to act as pointers ; are still in
use as truffle-finders. —Ed. B.]

Pluck ? Another back - country sample.
Some time ago a traveller threw dqwn his port-
folio-latest Darling-River-ese for swag—at the
door of the homestead of a man known to have a
little surgical skill. “Boss in?” “Yes, but
having tea.” “Right, I’ll wait. When he’s
through, tell him, willyer, I’d like a word with
him.” The traveller sat on his swag, and waited.
Half-an-hour, and he was still waiting. “ Night.
Ha’ you got a knife— I got a hook in my thumb ? ”
A great cod-hook, deeply buried. “Painful?”
“Bit.” “ It’ll have to be cut right out.” “Yes;
had one in my palm before.” The “surgeon”
gashed the thumb open, and bound it up.
“Thanks, you did it neat.” The missus came to
the door of the bath-room where the operation was
performed, and the traveller swiftly put his hand
and arm behind his back so that she shouldn’t see
the blood. Boss, after washing his hands, went
inside to get the man a drop of whisky, looked
down the track—there was the patient, swag on
shoulder, off to the river-side to camp.

Among the ruins of Hobart’s most ancient
necropolis the local aldermen boast of having
lately discovered some Tasmanian blue-gum burial
crosses bearing date 1808. There is proof positive
that these “ wooden tombstones ” could not have
been erected less than three-quarters of a century
ago: and they are still fairly sound.
* * * *
“ May ” : That fish, after escaping from
the angler, often warn their mates is quite true.
I knew an eel that brooded over his injuries, and
even sought revenge. One morning I had jabbed
him with a three-pronged fork—one prong bent —
but he got away. In the afternoon I was stand-
ing waist-deep in water painting the bottom of
my boat that was hanging from davits attached to
wharf. I leaned against a pile, and trying soon
to move along, found it was no go—my legs were
tied to the pile. Had no means of cutting the
binding, so the rising tide crept slowly to my
chin. One inch more—but my mate came and,
stooping, cut away the very eel I had injured in
the morning (we knew it by comparing the fork
with his wounds). We were quite unable to untie
the knot that bound his head to his tail.

At no f"ue in the world’s history was so
much thought given as at present to the question
o Drink. In America, England, and other
great centres, the best intellects of the community
try to solve the great question. That mankind
must drink something better than water, tea,
eottee, and cocoa is universally acknowledged. In
America the expression of public opinion is given
m the prodigious consumption of Lager Bier.
Hager is the national drink ; once Lager becomes
the established drink of a nation, diseases of
b ® oome unknown. Here in New
nouth Wales the Sydney Lager Bier Brewing Co.
Ltd. has established itself in the favor of the
community. And while Lager is known to be the
best beverage for men and women, it is highly
satisfactory to know that Sydney Lager is proved
by analysis to be the best on the N.S.W. market.*

Mrs. Harrison Lee.
Mrs. Harrison Lee, the Australian prohibitionist,
is a woman who believes in milking the cows
regularly at five o’clock each evening. She was
brought up in the mountains of Victoria, “in the
great Dividing Range where we were all so
friendly,” and she advances this as an excuse for
her social w r armth. She is a good and simple
woman with a beautifully-arranged set of Sunday-
school principles. When you call upon
her, she greets you with a lovely smile
—and tea ; warm, tea. If you give
the password “Alcohol shall Die!”
she calls you “Brother” if you’re
a man, “Dear,” if you’re a woman;
and she becomes your sister Bessie,
purring, and purring, and purring.
She was married, eighteen years ago ;
spent eight years of married life
as an invalid: was then healed by faith ;
and has now spent 10 years in “the
happy service.” As an invalid, she
could not walk ; but, after being cured
by faith, she walked, and talked, and
fought the “Demon Drink.” She
rode 50 miles a day over mountainous
country and dined on damper in a
navvies’ camp, in order to preach that
evening in the cause of water and tea.
She believes “ Drink ” to be the cause
of all other social evils, and she be-
lieves that every man’s pocket has a
hole in it, and that hole is at the top.
She admits that the deduction from
history and from every-day observation
is that the desire for alcoholic liquors
is part of human nature, but she smiles
that difficulty away, and says in a voice
of high sweet key—“We can reform
human nature. ” She is so simple and
so optimistically imaginative that she
reminds you of the child smiling and
saying, “We can reach the moon if
we have a ladder long enough.” She
recounts her great record in Ballarat
in ’9l, when a poll of the people was
taken and 48 hotels were closed in East
Ballarat in one day, and 27 in West
Ballarat. And she dwells upon this
fact as if it were an indication ; when
all the time it merely signifies that
the sea-shore sands are fewer tempor-
arily by some grains. She points to
Edison, the genius, because he said
that he “kept his brains for a better pur-
pose than drinking ”; she would not point to
Edison, the “ chain ” cigar-smoker, or to Bis-
marck, the brandy-drinking and chain-cigar man;
she “loves those who bind themselves that others
may be free;” and her definition of, liberty, is,
that “it ends where another’s danger begins.”
Her own sex is her sorest trouble. “Itis so much
harder to cure women than to cure men of
drunkenness ; when women lose their self-respect
it is so seldom that they ever get it back again.
Signorina Cesca Vollugi (photo, page 14) is
the very promising young Melb. girl who lately
took the name-part in a Melb. amateur-produc-
tion of “ The Bohemian Girl.” Cesca Vollugi,
like Lalla Miranda, is the daughter of a clever
music-and-singing teacher, and, after her father’s
death, she had the advantage of further careful
tuition from Melba’s first tutor, Cecehi. That the
damsel is an artistic singer goes without saying.
She has a nice voice, pretty stage-appearance, and
a decided aptitude—probably a real talent—for
acting.
Labor-member W. A. Holman’s recent
maiden speech in N.S. Wales Assembly was
a welcome relief from the dreary drip-drop
that does duty for oratory in the Parlia-
ments of Little Pedlington. The subject was
the amendment of the I27th clause in the
Draft Bill—a theme which, of course, readily lent
itself to effective use at the hands of a man who
since childhood has fed fat his mind with the
literature of ultra-democracy ; whose god of
gods is the vox populi; and who for seven
or eight years—since he was in the last of
Lis teens —has been recognised as marvellously
endowed with the gift of speech. Starting in the
presence of a House half-empty, Holman quickly
filled the benches ; and when at the end of about
an hour he concluded, he received a round of
cheers from all quarters of the Chamber—a
tribute seldom paid to any member, and the equal
of which in enthusiasm has not been heard in the
House for many years. Premier Reid told
Holman the speech was one of the best
he had heard ; and Wise, to whose speech
Holman’s was a reply, was no less generous of
appreciation. No one in the House has claims
equal to Holman’s to be considered an orator;
but if the member for Grenfell talks often, one of
these days Speaker Abbott will be hit in the eye
by a Hansard man’s hand—thrown off by the
extraordinary centrifugal force which the record-
ing angel is called upon to develop in his attempts
to keep within cooee of “the last word.” The
cataractof Lodore is deadinanimacycompared with
Holman’s rate of speaking when he’s “at his top.”
The Bulletin, writingof Ashton’s maiden speech,
said that the speaker was so deliberate, and took
so long to get from the end of one word to the
commencement of the next, that the audience had
ample time to go out and have a drink, and return
without losing the thread of his discourse. But if
you cough while Holman is speaking you lose half-
a-dozen sentences.

Will Ogilvie’s charming volume of verse,
“ Fair Girls and Gray Horses,” is now ready. In
case of any local difficulty or delay in supply, the
best plan is to order direct from The Bulletin,
enclosing 4s. Cd. in stamps or postal notes ; and
get the book by return post.
“Anon ” : He the Wangaratta death under
chloroform (B 29/10/’f)8), it doesn’t follow by any
means that because the patient was healthy, im-
pure drugs passed him out. Fact is, few surgeons
know how to administer chloroform safely. An
eminent British specialist declares that all such
deaths are due to carelessness or ignorance on the
part of the administrators. Another specialist ad-
ministered the anaesthetic in 5000 cases without
one fatality. It’s the haphazard method of ad-
ministration that usually kills, apparently.
Writes Miss Hilda
Spong to Bulletin
from London :
Just two years ago (Sep.
’96) I made my first ap-
pearance before an Eng-
lish audience, at Drury
Lane, in the “ Duchess
of Coolgardie ’’ long
enough, I think, to justify
me in giving my im-
pressions of England,
especially as visitors from
this side don’t scruple to
sum up “down-under”
characteristics after being
but a couple of weeks in
Australia. Had I been so
hasty of judgment next
boat back would have seen
me on board, so wretched
did everything seem.
Chill, gloomy weather ;
chill, gloomy people ;
luxurious wealth joslling-
squalid poverty; an atmos-
phere more fit to chew
than to breathe, and an
apparent hopelessness of
ever getting a professional
opening with such an
over-crowded actor-mar-
ket. Such would have
been my verdict at the
time, and a very unfair
one it would have been,
for London isn’t England,
and the sun does oc-
casionally shine to some
purpose (89deg. in the
shade last week).
I got my chance through
an opportune introduc-
tion to Coleman, of Drury
Lane, when he was set-
tling the cast of the
“ Duchess of Coolgardie.”
Seemed favorably im-
pressed, and a reference
to Mr. Boucicault got me
the leading part. How I
succeeded, and how good
public and press were to
me, you know. And here
let me warn my Austra-
lian sisters of the difficulty
of getting an opening in
London. There is a hard
“ring," which it takes
strong interest to breal.
through; a letter of introduction will seldom do
it, and even when an opening is found, the engage-
ment is only for the run of the piece. Should it only
play for a week or two there is the expense of your dresses,
and another weary wait. Myownluckin being" out ” but
four rnonths in two years is very exceptional. As to
salaries also, only very few command big pay ; otherwise,
pay is lower than in Australia, especially in the provinces,
where £5 a week is average for leading man or woman.
The climate here is about one part sunshine to ten of
rain, snow, and fog, but there are compensations in the
chances of meeting such men as Pinero, Grundy,
Buchanan, Herman Merivale, or Anthony Hope. You
never know whom you may strike in a stroll down the
Strand ; perhaps the genial Louis Becke or the melodious
Ada Crossley. Then there’s a great old talk about down-
under. lam soon to visit America, and, as that’s on the
way home, I may perhaps be able to give you my impres-
sions thereof in my own proper person.
A PERTINENT QUESTION.
Why drink unknown brands of whisky of doubt-
ful quality when the “D.C.L.” Scotch (Black
Bottle) can be relied on as a real Highland
Whisky, thorough matured, and of guaranteed
uniformity.
A Modern Sampson.
Sampson in his clothes is nothing to set the crowd
agape. There are any number of men in Australia
who are more arrestive in a crowd than he; thou-
sands who are more expressive of strength, of will,
of determination. As for symmetry, you could
almost throw a stone at random, and hit a better
model man than Sampson. There are thousands
upon thousands of men in Australia who are taller
than he. As for big bones, stop the first police-
man you meet. He is bigger-boned than Samp-
son. But if you look for strength, wrestle with a
bull sooner than with Sampson ; take a kick from
a horse sooner than a blow from this human em-
bodiment of force. Lift his dumb-bell and he
pays you £ 100. That brick wall over there is a
good single-brick wall. Sampson stands ready,
his hand protected by a glove. He gathers
himself together; one punch !—out fly two
bricks. On the stage in his exhibition he
punches a 30001 b. blow. He gets down on
his hands and feet and supports a table carrying
twelve women. That is nothing. He has lifted
40001 b.—over a ton and three-quarters. He has
pulled against 14 men and beaten them. He
breaks shillings in his fingers ; iron rings upon
his biceps. At the age of 12 he was struck by
lightning; but, like the majority of those so
struck, he did not die. But he remained para-
lysed for 17 days. At the end of that time his
father, a doctor, suspended two iron rings from
the ceiling, and told his son to grip these and try
to raise himself. Then began the secret of Samp-
son’s evolution ; the summoning of the will. He
placed the iron rings upon his biceps, and strained
and strained his muscles against them. He arose
from his bed of paralysis, and kept on with his
iron rings for 26 years—till long after the world
had called him Sampson. That is the story of
Sampson, according to himself. Alow for his
regimen of health.
He takes a bath in the morning and he wouldn’t
mind a swim—“a swim’s an appetiser; it’s a
natural thing to do before breakfast. As for work
before breakfast—let fools do it. A man is weak-
est when he gets up, and the proper time for
exercise is in the afternoon or evening. When a
man exercises he should work—work hard with
101 b. dumb bells if he wishes to develop strength ;
light dumb bells are a mere farce.” Sampson’s
breakfast at 8 or 8.30 consists of a couple of eggs
or an omelette with brown bread and coffee. For
lunch at 1, he has two or three plates of soup,
beef, or mutton, with brown bread. He declines
potatoes and vegetables because they fatten:
eggs, meat and brown bread are muscle-
makers.” His drink with lunch is good wine—
Burgundy preferred ; if not this, then claret or
champagne. Dinner at 6.30 is a repetition of lunch
in quantity and substance. Between meals or
with lunch or dinner he will take any fruit that
is going. He takes wine or whisky at times.
But water ! oh never 1 Sampson has never drunk
pure water in his life. “Pure water it is bad *or
the boots ; it must be bad for the stomach.”
And tea ! Sampson is a Frenchman, and more-
over he earns “ ,£250 a week.” He dare not drink
tea ! This block of strength—built only sft. 7in.
high so that he shall have no weak spot—this
living force whose muscles burst rings of iron,
finds his weak spot in a cup of tea. “ Now, at 4(1
Miss HILDA SPONG, in “Trelawny of the Wells."
Photo., Alfred Ellis, London.
years old, tea makes me nervous as it has always
done; makes me feel that something dreadful is
going to happen—fills me with the feeling that an
accident or break-down will occur in my exhibi-
tion.” This from a man who claims to have worn
no support when he lifted close on two tons !
He does not smoke. Most of his work is done
by inflating his lungs—he can expand from 3‘Jin.
to 58in. And he considers that smoking injures
both lungs and heart.
S. A. MILLS.

MELBOURNE, Friday.
A few days ago the Board of Health reoeived
an anonymous communication to tho effect that
l)i\ Heyden, residing at Macarthor, near Wci-r-
-nambool, who arrived from India about six months
ago, had in his possession a packet of bacilli of
the dreaded bubonic plague The board took
immediate action by despatching its health
officer. Dr. Gray, to Macarthor for the purpose
of ascertaining if the report were correct, and : if
so, to endeavour to get Dr. Heyden to hand thd
microbes over for destruction. Dr. Gray
ascertained that tho report was quite correct,
but Dr. Heyden refused 'td part with
the bacilli, which he had secured for ex-
perimental purposes at great risk to himself.
He receives £3OO as compensation. When this
was reported to the health authorities they
considered the situation, and found that they
had no legal power Jo interfere with
the importation. Dr. Gray accordingly left
Macarthur to-day on his letutn to Melbourne. In
the meantime the Minister for Health, Mr. WiL
liarns, was appealed to by the board, and it was
represented to him that the patter was st
very serious one and called for decisive action
in the public interest. The board argued that Dr.
/ Heyden must have smuggled the bacilli into the
L colony, as inquiries at the Custom-house
/showed that no such importation had
/, been reported. Microbes are not dutiable,
7and there was no law to prevent
/their importation, but the Secretary for Trade
a and Customs states that everyone coming to thq
A colony is technically bound to repdrt everything
/in his possession. Mr. Williams, at an interview
! with Dr. Wollaston, suggested that an officer of
i the Customs Department should be sent
to’ seize the germs, and that Dr.
Heyden he prosecuted for secretly importing
the germs of a horribly dangorous disease. Mr.
Williams regarded Dr. Heyden’s conduot as
objectionable and favoured desperate measures,
but upon Dr. Wollaston learning that tho
microbes had been imported in 'gelatine.-
he saw his opportunity to proceed in
a legal manner, for gelatine is subject to a duty
of 3d per lb., and as Dr. Heyden had not paid
duty the department was entitled to seize tho
gelatine. Detective-inspector Christie, was, there-
fore instructed to proceed to Macarthur by this
afternoon’s train and make the seizure, and for
this purpose he was accompanied by a bacterio-
logical expert.
Society op Ahtists. —Hitherto the Art Society
has enjoyed a Government subsidy of £5OO a
year, but nnder the new arrangements made by
the Minister for Public Instruction the Sum of
£BOO has been placed upon the Estimates for
equal division between the Art Society and the
Society of Artists. The members of the latter
society will in this way be enabled to retain per-
manently the handsome gallery occupied by - them
during the past few weeds, and they also intend
to found a travelling scholarship with the money
Similar to those which have proved so beneficial
to Victorian students.

| On the line between Narrandara and
Jerildcrie “ a plague of grasshoppers ” was met
■with. The wheels of the train inflicted instant
execution ; bat they became so greasy that they
would not “ bite.” They slipped round and
round with great rapidity, but the train scarcely
moved an inch. A little sand on' the rails set
matters right, and the driver on making known
the cause of the delay was instantly exonerated
by the department. ‘

CARTOONLETS.


Do you ever have a headache ? Would
you not have a cure—simple, harmless, effective*
of trifling cost, and occupying no more space than
a thimble? Then, write or call on Newman and
Snelson (chemists to Lord Hampden), King-
street, Sydney. They have discovered a specific
which instantly relieves a headache of any origin,
and which never produces any of the injurious
after-effects so common with other headache cures.
The new cure is put up in packets of ten tablets—
ten doses—for Is. Id. by post. *
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
Brooks’s Australian Christmas Annual
has been produced, under the superintendence of
D. H. Souter, with excellent illustrations and
bright typography. There is a cover in three
colours by D. H. Souter, and two coloured illus-
trations—one by Souter, the other by J ulian
Ashton. Other artists contributing are Mahony,
Lambert, Fullwood, and Alice Muskett; and the
light-and-landscape glimpses 'ey the two latter are
particularly full of sympathy and skill. The
literary contributors include Lawson, Daley, F. J.
Broomfield, “The Banjo,” J. A. Philp, W. B.
Beattie, David Grant, Chris. Brennan, and
others; and the price of the magazine (30 pages)
is Is.
“Timothy The Japs at the C.S.R. Co.’s
Victoria Mill on the Herbert River (Q.) being dis-
satisfied determined on strike. Moment bell rang
at six o’clock following morning all the night-shift
Japs—about 30 - ceased work and made tracks for
the barracks, and, the day-shift boys not showing
up, crushing had to cease. Took three hours
talking and concession of their demands to make
them resume work. Strong contrast to conduct of
white men at same mill, who have for sometime
been greatly dissatisfied with the cooking. Trying
to get them to act au Jap. is futile, as not half-a-
dozen will hang together, and the bosses know it.
The enormous new White Star liner,
Oceanic, is 702 ft. long—l 4 yards over |mi le-
as against the Great Eastern’s 675 ft. When
launched (early next year) she will displace 17,000
tons of water, and her coal capacity will be enough
to let her girdle the earth at 12 knots an hour.
The N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Bier. This bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S.W. Analysts praise it most
highly ; doctors recommend it to convalescents,
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
Show gave it first prize against all competitors.
What has long troubled the rational people of
N.S.W. is “what to drink.” There is no fun
in summer weather in drinking fiery alcoholic
liquors that break up the nerves, affect the diges-
tion, heart, liver and kidneys; and on the other
hand there is an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should com-
municate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Go., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though the new lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
market.*
“Strychnos” re impure drugs and want
of an official drug-tester :
Imported articles, even if tested and found on arrival
in Australia to be pure, are singularly prone to become
either inert or chemically decomposed by time and
climate. What guarantee, therefore, would an indivi-
dual have, especially in a country town, that the chemist
has not had the particular drug or drugs used in com-
pounding a mixture on hand for, say, anything up to five
years? Recently saw a rural apothecary sell a consign-
ment of Blaud’s pills - stocked article-to a customer,
one of which, on being fired from a pistol, pierced a half-
inch deal board and finally lodged in a pumpkin. The
pill, on examination, was found to be only slightly
bruised, and apparently quite prepared to "undergo a
similar ordeal if called upon to do so. I fancy that an
official drug-tester will not meet requirements.

BILE BEANS
THEY STILL REMAIN THE
People’s Popular ■ Priced Preparation-
A Terrible State of Affairs.
SEVENTEEN LONG YEARS OF AGONY.
PARALYSIS, SPINAL TROUBLE, PILES AND INTERNAL COMPLAINTS.
VICTORY FOR BILE BEANS AFTER DOCTORS AND OTHER MEDICINES HAD
FAILED.
It having been reported that a marvellous cure had been effected in the case of Mrs. H. Ford, the
wife of Mr. Henry Ford, the well-known stevedore of Newcastle,
a representative of the Newcastle Herald called at the lady’s
residence, No. 3 Mona Terrace, Reid-street, recently, and found
that in this case Bile Beans for Biliousness had established pretty
well a record as a wonderful curative preparation. Mrs. Ford
had been a martyr to ailments which weie found to be beyond the
power of medical skill. She was seized with an attack of para-
lysis some seventeen years ago. This left her with occasional
pa,ins in the abdomen, the result of stricture. Her spine weak, her
mind nervous, and her speech affected to such an extent that it
was difficult for her to pronounce certain words. She suffered
the greatest agony during the past four or five years, and in the
meantime she was attacked with that wasting and painful
disease, Piles. Then, to make her sufferings more acute, she had
the misfortune to be fish poisoned. One baby very shortly after-
wards died from the effects of this in her system ; another child
was born dead, and the agony of the mother can better be
imagined than described. The last baby, which its mother was
unable to nurse, now shows signs of waste. That the Bile Beans
should have cured a lady in this terrible and complicated con-
dition is certainly a very great achievement, and yet we have
Mrs. Ford’s positive and emphatic statement, that nothing
else afforded her any permanent relief. Her husband,
. . she says, had spent a power of money in doctor’s
medicine during the course of her long and continued illness. She never experienced more than tem-
porary relief from their treatment, but Bile Beans have wrought a wonderful change in her constitu-
tmn. “ I have been taking them now,” she went on to say, “since baby was born, five months ago,
and I tell you honestly I would never have believed they could do what they have done for me. The
effects of the fish-poisoning seems to have entirely left my system, and I am free from that state of
nervousness which made my life miserable. Ido not suffer from the spinal affection that I mentioned,
and I have now become regular in my system, with the absence of those agonising pains, which every-
one knows are associated with complaints such as I had. I attribute my cure solely to Bile Beans for
Biliousness. I might mention that I gave them a good chance to do their work by dieting myself
according to circumstances and directions. Needless to add, I cannot speak too highly of this remedy,
and I shall always keep a box near me for emergencies so long as I have a shilling in the house. I
have recommended them to many friends, and excellent results have followed their use.” Mrs. Ford
expressed her willingness to allow these facts to be published in the hope that other poor suf-
ferers might read them. The newspaper-man congratulated her on her wonderful recovery, and agreed
with her that, after what she had said, Bile Beans were in reality a poor woman’s, as well as a poor
man’s, friend.

Woman’s Weakness-
The woman of to-day is usually ailing; there is always something the matter with her. The
world’s doctor bills for women is over double what they are for men and children. Of course, the
organism of a woman is much more complex and delicate, and requires a great deal of attention, but
to suppose that it was originally intended that women generally should suffer for the greater part of
i F r *^^ cu^ous 0n the face of it. The great trouble in this case lies with the woman herself ;
she disobeys the natural laws, fails to observe regular habits, and seldom takes proper care of herself,
or gives enough attention to her own bodily welfare. Tight lacing is one of the many causes of the
alarming predominance of weak women to-day. The abdomen is compressed into half its original
size, and the digestive organs not given sufficient room in which to perform their various duties ; they,
in their turn, crowd the heart and female organs, and the result is palpitation of the heart, irregular
function, and a general weakness of the whole system. It is absolutely necessary, furthermore, that
the waste matter of the body should be carried out of the system regularly and systematically, and this
can only be done by keeping the whole digestive organism in perfect condition. A bath occasionally
is an absolute necessity to good health; so is a thorough cleansing of the interior portions of our
anatomy.
A woman in an unhealthy condition is entirely unable to perform her duties as a wife and mother,
and instead of being a help and blessing to her husband, she will prove a fretful, grumbling burden,
whose very existence is a curse to herself, her family, and her friends.
Cleanse the system, then, and in future give more attention to the mechanism which forms your
womanhood. Take a few moments from the perusal of fashion papers and bargain advertisements,
and spend the time in finding what rules of nature you are disobeying, and what you can do to im-
P r ' )V , e your health and make those around you happy ; and remember that Bile Beans will cleanse the
whole body of waste matter, tone up the system, and give you renewed energy and vigor, thus enabling
you to make a fresh start, with a clear road to a healthy and happy existence.

Piles.
Here is an ailment which is a great deal more common than is generally supposed. Women suffer
from it to a much greater extent than men, and there is a delicacy about mentioning it which causes
much needless suffering. Bleeding Piles and Blind Piles are the two branches of this ailment, the
latter being by far the most painful and common. Blind Piles consist in the formation of hard,
knotty particles, singly and in bunches, extending upward into the bowels. Bleeding Piles consist of
a swelling of the hermorrohicle vein, which break their walls, and often cause great loss of blood.
I hey are an exceedingly disagreeable complaint, being accompanied by great irritation and inflamma-
tion. In cases of Piles the sufferer becomes irritable and restless, sour of disposition, unable to sleep,
and generally discontented.
Piles are generally accompanied by more or less constipation, and can be treated in two ways—by
injections, which is an exceedingly painful operation, or by the application of a purgative, which,
acting internally, will flush the bowels and thus soften and heal the afflicted parts. For this latter
no better remedy can be found than Bile Beans, as a trial will convince anyone. They act in a gentle
manner, doing their work thoroughly, without straining any of the organism.
Praise from Melbourne.
A WELL KNOWN THEATRICAL MAN CURED.
SEVERE CASE OF BILIOUSNESS.
A reporter having heard of a remarkable cure through the agency of Bile Beans, looked up Mr.
Frederick George Salter, a well-known figure in theatrical circles in Melbourne, residing with his
family at 15 Fawcett-street, Albert Park. After a few introductory remarks, the subject of our
notice freely entered into particulars regarding what to him and his friends is a matter of unalloyed
gratification and agreeable surprise. Mr. Salter tells his story as follows “For years I was a con-
stant sufferer from Biliousness and its dreaded effects. Needless to remark, various doctors and
patent medicines were tried with the hope of being cured, but little, if any, relief was experienced
from these sources. After an unsatisfactory night’s rest, I was wont to rise with a nasty taste in the
mouth and a feeling of intense weariness. The prospect of getting through the day’s business
was not, as may be easily imagined, a very bright one. The throat was parched
and dry, adding its quota to the evils already enumerated. Moreover, the whole system
was being gradually undermined, from loss of appetite, one of the
disastrous effects of Biliousness. Indeed, I turned away from meat,
especially in the morning, with disgust, and had to content myself
with a bare collation of a little bread and tea —a somewhat poor foun-
dation on which to stand till lunch-hour. In this age of keen com-
petition and restless energy in business circles, a man requires all
the help available to enable him to hold his own in the race for ex-
istence. Hence it is that people, when attacked with disease, are
prepared to spend their substance on physicians and medicines in the
hope of meeting with a speedy and effectual cure. Such was the
state of things when I happened to hear from a friend about the
efficacy of the newly-discovered cure for biliousness, Bile Beans. At
first I was a little incredulous, and no wonder, considering the many
alleged remedies for my distressing complaint which had all proved
ineffectual in affording relief. Nevertheless, following the motto,
‘ try again,’ I determined to procure a box of Bile Beans and put
their value to a test. Well, sir, my most sanguine anticipations as
to the curative properties of this medicine have been more than
realised. The fact is that, after one or two doses, I experienced a
change for the better in my system. I continued the course, and my
former vigor began to return, the symptoms above enumerated
gradually disappeared, and the whole man felt that he was in posses-
sion of a, new lease of life. That a change for the better had been
effected in the system was apparent. More palpable evidence of
restored health could not better be seen than the fact of the return
of my former healthy appetite, to which I had been for a long time a stranger. Thanks to the power
of Bile Beans, I can now take and enjoy a hearty breakfast, lunch, and dinner. My former glow of
health and lightness of spirits have again returned. In one word, lam a new man—and I owe it all
to Bile Beans.”
