Biliousness.
Biliousness consists of a poor appetite, severe headaches, sallow, yellow complexion, and a feeling of
general want of energy, and fatigue. Its cause is the misdirection of the bile, which becomes mixed
with the blood, and permeates the whole system.
The bile is a fluid secreted by the liver, which, when mixed with the pancreatic juice, completes
the process of digestion. The supply of bile is kept in the gall bladder, whence it flows when needed
for digestive purposes. If the pipe or duct which conveys the bile from the gall bladder becomes
stopped up through the existence of gall stones or other obstruction, the bile is forced back into the
gall bladder, which overflows, and the bile is then carried out by the only other outlet—the thoracic
duct-which conveys it to a large vein in the left shoulder. From thence it passes to the heart, and
becomes mixed with the blood. Its presence in the blood,is made manifest in various ways, such as
pimples, face sores, yellow complexion, dark rings under the eyes, &c. The digestion, too, is impaired
for want of sufficient bile to complete the process. The detrimental effect of misdirected bile is there-
fore twofold.
Bile Beans put the liver in a perfect condition, and prevent the ducts connecting the various
digestive organs from becoming stopped up. In this way they prevent biliousness. When a cure,
however, is required they cause the liver to secrete increased quantities of this fluid, and thus flush
the whole digestive system, and carry off all waste matter from the system, clearing off at the same
time all surplus bile.

A Valuable Discovery.
EXTRACT FROM A SYDNEY PAPER THAT ILLUSTRATES THE WORTH OF
BILE BEANS.
Scientists tell us that, in this nineteenth century of ours, that about the greatest strides made in
the discovery-world has been in medicine. We have to record a discovery, by an American medical
man, of a certain vegetable substance that has the effect in the digestive organs of nature’s own animal
bile. This discovery is revolutionising all the recent theories and remedies for digestive ailments.
The old theory of pepsin, bismuth, starving, purging, &c., &c., is to be done away with. This great
vegetable substance is compounded with other ingredients to facilitate the working, and to-day this
remedy can be purchased the world over, under the name of Bile Beans fob Biliousness. This great
remedy possesses the peculiar properties of acting on the bowels without purging or otherwise weaken-
ing the patient. This is because the substance that relaxes the bowels has about the same action as
the natural bile. Bile Beans are purely vegetable, and a course of them will probably successfully
oust the most chronic cases of piles, indigestion, chronic headache, kidney trouble, rheumatism, con-
stipation, and a host of other ailments that owe their origin to defective bile-flow, assimilation and
digestion. These Beans are placed on the market in a form that anyone can take them without medi-
cal supervision, and, as the price is so very low, there are few homes that cannot afford to always have
a box on the shelf for emergency. To be obtained from all chemists. Australian Depot, 39 Pitt-street
Sydney ; Victorian Branch, Flinders-street, Melbourne ; S. A. Branch, King William-street, Adelaide •
Queensland Branch, Charlotte-street, Brisbane ; W.A. Branch, Murray-street, Perth.

There will be no war. Paris has too long
been getting ready for its 1900 exhibition.
When Reid’s “ pure-Freeti'ade ” tariff first
saw the light, a big Sydney brewer sized it up :
“Now the taxes are to be paid by a few people
who own some land and a few people who drink
whisky.”
Australasian Premiers hold office—For-
rest, since 1890 ; Kingston and Seddon, since 1893 ;
lieid, Turner, and Braddon, since 1894; and
Dickson, since- Oct., 1898. And the last is likely
to go first.
“The man who can say ‘Yes’ or ‘No’
at the right time has the greatest command of
language.” —English paper. What a command of
tiie English language G. H. Reid must have, seeing
that he can say both of them at any time !
Barton entered N.S.W. Leg. Assembly in
1879; his first censure-motion against any Ad-
ministration was tallied last week. He adopted
Lyne’s motion and supported many others, but
that against Reid is the first solely “ off his own
fiat.”
Premier Reid, Cobden Club gold-medallist,
slowly but surely grabs the whole policy of
N.S.W. Protectionist party. At present, the
I’rotectionists are in the peculiar position of
objecting to a Protectionist tariff being imposed,
and demanding that the province remain Free-
trade.
Westralia spends £2OOO of borrowed
money this year to encourage immigration from
England. And, meanwhile, multitudes of the
people already in the country are doing their level
best to get out of it, Assisted immigration to
W.A. just now is very like assisted immigration
to Tophet.
Maoriland, although critics of its advanced
politics treat it as constitutionally a younger
country than N.S.W., is in reality older. The
M. Constitution Act was passed in 1852, whereas
that of N.S.W. was not passed till ’55. Even
Cape Colony obtained a Constitution Act before
N. Wales —in ’54.
The Kingston Govt, is safe to emerge
without trouble from the present Parliament, its
second since its formation in 1893. Premier
Kingston proudly claims that he "has a larger
majority at his back than he had when he formed
his team six years ago. Of his original colleagues
only Holder now remains.
Premier Kingston has ueclared that his
Govt, will be no longer involved in the great land-
gamble which has been going on since the Crown
first began to part with its estate. For the pre-
sent the proposal to substitute perpetual lease for
land sales has been rejected, but next year it will
probably have a big majority.
Q. Minister Barlow doesn’t believe in Leg.
Councillors addressing their House through the
President, but by the words “Members gener-
ally.” Why ? Because, Barlow says, the Presi-
dent is imposed on the House—the House doesn t
elect him. H’m ! Councillors are imposed on the
country—no one elects them.
Solomon, M.L.A., by way of his little
scoff at reform, wants S.A. Assembly to set apart
a day for considering whether rogues or fools do
more harm in this world. The fools are under-
stood to be people who don’t agree with Solomon,
M.L.A., and the rogues are the people who think
Solomon is a pompous and rather vociferous ass.

Queensland has had the same Ministry
ever since 1890, but, now that Byrnes is gone,
there isn’t a single man of the original team left
in it. If MTlwraith had come back to the bosom
of his old Cabinet to clear his character, as he
promised, he would have been in the position of
the prodigal who returned to the old home and
found a strange woman at the door. “Yes, your
mother died, and then your father married Jane
Brown, and she married Smith after your father
died, and when Jane broke her neck falling out of
the cart Smith married me. But you can sit on
the fence and rest a minute if you like.” Same
family all the time, only different.
Visiting-inspector Brodie’s offi-
cial report on the state of Norfolk
Island will be moved for one of these
nights in N.S.W. Assembly. The
report, which deals with matters of
importance both to the island and
to N.S.W., its constitutional guar-
dian, has been months in the hands
of Govt., and will be suppressed
unless pressure is applied. Mr.
Brodie is an experienced and re-
sponsible official, holding in N.S.W.
the position of Assistant Auditor-
General and Chief-Inspector of Pub-
lic Accounts.
It is, indeed, anomalous that
N.S.W. Premier-and-Treasurer Reid
should only receive a salary of =£1370,
while the useless Colonial Secretary
Brunker pockets .£lB2O ! But the
way to remove the anomaly is, not
to add £5OO to Reid’s present salary
and leave Brunker’s as it is, but to
reduce the absurd Brunker’s salary
to £1370, and allow, as in the case
of Jack Robertson and Farnell, both
of whom held the Lands portfolio
and the (Premiership, the difference
to temporarily attach to the portfolio
taken by the First Minister. The
assumption by Reid of “ £5OO for
the Premier ” is really an advance on
the total of N.S.W. Ministerial
salaries.
In Tasmania all who are not
property-holders must send in their
claims for an Assembly vote every
year. The Electoral Act treats every
mere salary or wages man as a luna-
tic, a criminal, or a pauper, and in-
structs the registrar to officially ob-
ject to their names appearing on the roll, such
objection standing as a complete bar to the fran-
chise unless the claim is sent in annually. Hence
one flourishing concern in the Isle of Bad Dreams
is the great Waste-Paper Industry.
Whether one is Federal or anti-Federal,
Protrader or Freetectionist, one must admit that
Premier Reid has upset the Aristotelian axiom
that a thing cannot both be and not-be at the
same time. His agile ropewalk from Yes to No,
and his incantation of the new Sugar Duties from
the vasty Absolutes of Freetrade, are as interesting
in their bearing on the history of philosophy as
they are in other respects a pieasant break in the
monotony of honesty.
N.S.W. Civil Service voted pretty strongly
for Premier Reid because that astute gentleman
divulged, just before the elections, that the Public
Service Board was fixing up a scheme of salary-
increases. The C.S. now feels too miserable to
say exactly what it thinks of itself in this matter.
The allowance set down for increases is just
.£15,000 which would run to about 18s. each per
annum all round. And the N.S.W. public ser-
vant, as a rule, hasn’t seen such a thing as a rise
since 1893.
The Public Service Code, now before S.A.
Parliament, if passed, will increase the number of
persons on the fixed list from 6000 to 8000, by ex-
tending to the “temporaries” all the privileges
enjoyed by the.present permanent men. In holi-

Curious how many S.A. constituencies, all
of which return two members a-piece, couple an
incessant chatterer and a silent man. But in more
than one case the silent man is the better speaker.
Grainger and Hooper, of Wallaroo, are thus
mated. Hooper, a working miner’s man, who is
heard rather less than once a session, simply
chains the Assembly with his native eloquence
when he does speak ; while Grainger, who hollers
and pounds the air almost nightly, rarely com-
mands a House.
Melb. Age digs up Graham Berry’s indict-
ment against Vic. Council, with some additions to
bring it up to date. The Council maintained
State aid to religion for 15 years after the people
emphatically declared against it. In 24 years it
rejected six Bills legalising mining on private
property, seven Bills legalising payment of mem-
bers, a Tariff Bill and four Appropriation Bills.
And, in the end, it gave way on all these points,
so all it did was to grumble and fret and waste
time after all. Now it has three times rejected
the One Man One Vote Bill, and, probably, it will
reject it three or four times more, and then give
in. The fretful old political invalid always gives
in at the end ; only the end is such a long way off.

The S.A. Govt, is anxious to see J. C.
Haslam elected as M.L.C. for the northern
district in place of the late Dr. Campbell, as
he has promised to support the Ministerial Bill
according a vote for the Upper House to all
householders and their wives. This would add
about 60,000 names to the Council rolls. The
Tories support Andrew Tennant, a gilded squatter
of some Parliamentary experience. He cannot
speak, and he knows little outside of pastoral
affairs, but he can vote most solidly.
“N.”: Brassey was the most efficient
talker amid the vice-regalities at Melb. Mayoral
banquet, through being so lately in touch with
Europe. He spoke of a conversation with Cham-
berlain on Australian Federation, but probably
the gush was mostly on his own side—he does not
seem to report the questions Chamberlain put.
How is it we can lay the finger on no remarkable
public utterance by the Secretary for the Colonies
on this important topic? Frankly, I don’t think
the Queen and the Salisbury Government favor
Australian Federation.
This year Yic. Government announces that
Victorian and N.S.W. coal must compete on level
terms for State coal contracts. And the Victorian
coal-owners kick—very reasonably, too. They
complain that, as they have to pay a legally-fixed
minimum wage, the outsider, if he gets the con-
tract, should have to pay the minimum wage also.
There is no coal-duty in Victoria as in Tasmania
and Queensland, and railway freight from Gipps-
land is quite as costly as sea-freight from New-
castle ; therefore, the minimum wage has a
tendency to become no wage at all.
The State’s right to deprive Dr. Heydon
of his packet of bubonic-plague bacilli is obvious,
and should have been promptly enforced, as
part of the supreme/, lex, without tinkering with
Victorian Custom House regulations. What if
the doctor’s effects had been burgled and the
packet appropriated by an unsuspecting thief, to be
thrown away on the discovery that it was unnego-
tiable? And a thousand other dangers, all outside
Dr. Heydon’s control, impended. The Supreme
Law of self-preservation moves, and should move,
by Executive minute, without waiting for police-
court or similar routine processes.

Sudden outbreak of Toryism from Sydney
Telegraph i
Maoriland has inaugurated another experiment in cor-
pore non vili. This time the pretended reform is the
levelling-down of the ratepayer, who henceforth is of no
more consequence in his own city than the crossing-
sweeper, who, unlike the crossing-sweeper of fiction, is
not a millionaire. The majority will govern, with the
aid of the property of the minority. It is hard enough
for the boroughs to keep clear of bankruptcy under the
ratepaying system. Even with the supposed safeguard
of a special rate, municipalities have managed to heap up
obligations which they cannot hope to meet. What will
happen now that the safeguard is still further weakened
by being put into the hands of people who don t pay any-
thing? Will the majority of ratepayers vote ‘‘yes for
every proposal that will scatter coin through place i
Will they elect a council of non-ratepayers? Will they
scout the idea of being economical with other peoples
money? Will they, in short, gradually confiscate the
property of the rich by taxing it from under them ? They
may, of course, do none of these thing’s. But the risk
that they may do some of them under a system august in
principle and without safeguard against injustice is too
great to make one wish to run it. We can watch Maori-
land from a safe distance.
Yet the Telegraph supports Reid’s policy (the
one he had till lately) of putting all taxes on
people with large incomes or a lot of land, and on
drinkers and smokers ; and letting the teetotaller
with less than .£2OO a year go absolutely free,
and the poor in general go practically free, they
having exactly the same voting power and the
same control over the revenue as the largest con-
tributor thereto. The cases are exactly parallel
—except that in the N.S.W. instance the prin-
ciple of giving an equal vote to the contributor
and the non-contributor happened to fit in with
the interests of the big Sydney importer.

Of butcher, or of baker, or grocer, don’t you see,
For they must wait for custom, but custom comes to me
“ Yang Anyut ” : The pre-natal-influence-
discussion suggests citation of cases of atavism or
reversion to ancestral types. Once knew a man
of good position, who had several children by a
Dusun woman. The third was quite black, and
showed other unmistakable signs of negro blood.
Titular father to all appearances was pure white.
Wondered he didn’t fire out the woman and hunt
up the only “ Seedi boy ” in the place, whom we all
put down as the true father ; but found out later
on that our friend was a Mauritius native and.
great-grandson of a negress.
From Tasmania. His reverence : “ Oh,
Mr. Doolan, do I see, sir, that Bulletin paper on
your counter?” “You do, father.” His rever-

man. Dear, dear, dear ! to see that paper here
after the manner in which it opposed His Eminence-

no longer. However, most priests read this wicked
paper, and even His Eminence’s secretary is broad-

less carefully wrapped up in the Unexceptionable-
S.M. Herald) to the palace at Manly.
Newspaper report of a Broken Hill Salva-
tion Army meeting :
In making an announcement, the Commandant referred,
to “ Adjutant ” Winter as “ Staff-captain ” Winter. Then
he turned to Adjutant Winter, and said—“ I beg your
pardon—not a staff-captain yet?” Adjutant Winter:
“No, sir.” The Commandant: “Never mind: it’s com-
ing.'’ Adjutant Winter: “When, sir?” (Laughter).
The Commandant : “ Well, if you want to know, it has
come now. It was one of the things I had up my sleeve ;
but I wanted to see how you behaved.” A tremendous
outburst of applause arid “ Hallelujahs ” at once arose, and
two of Staff-captain Winter's comrades seized him, and
after some struggling upended him and stood him on his
head on the platform. The performance plainly astounded
many of the audience, and the Commandant remarked—
“ You must excuse them ; it’s a way we have in the Army..
When an officer is promoted the promotion is not con-
sidered properly done till he has been stood on his head.”
By the way, there are female officers in the Army..
A Cautious Phrenologist.
“ Not being one who swallows every prescrip-
tion,” writes Mr. A. E. Wheeler, Phrenologist,.
High-street, Dunedin, in a letter, July 20, 1898,
“that is forced upon the public, it was only after
much persuasion that I decided to try Warner’s
Safe Cure. I had been a martyr for many years
to that terrible malady, indigestion. I was ulti-
mately induced to try a bottle of Warner’s Safe
Cure, which was said to give speedy relief. Al-
though not through one bottle, I have found to my
great delight that its virtues were not in any way
overestimated. I have been agreeably conscious
of its merits in relieving this terribly affecting
complaint, and can only in fairness add my testi-
mony to tlie many others who have secured relief
from its use.”

It would be a good idea to arrange for a
transfer of pictures when Vic. Artists’ Society and
its Sydney kindred associations hold exhibitions.
Many of the pictures in the new exhibition of the
Melb. Society are stale, from the show point of
view. They have been hung before, and are now
shown again, apparently to fill up. Meanwhile
there are many artists on Sydney side whose
work is only known by name in Melbourne.
What prevents such an annual exchange ?

The cure of piles by the new “ Canalin ”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to
any colony from the dep6t, 257 George-street,

G. 0. K. Waldron, Sydney City Soli-
citor, son-in-law of ex-Premier Dibbs, fell from
his bicycle on 9th inst. (it is supposed, owing to
an apoplectic seizure) and fracturing his neck,
died instantly. Mr. Waldron was a graduate of
Sydney University and a good all-round man,
popular in a very wide circle.
A good number of Sydney society women
have not yet stopped talking about the sale some
weeks back of furniture and household effects at
Cranbrook, Rose Bay ; and Kirkhain, Camden;
in the estate of Mrs. Scott, late widow of the
Hon. James White—-a sale that panned out to the
handsome total of £6600. Proceedings began with
a very able address by that boss auctioneer,
Walter Bradley. It needed an able address to
start the big ball rolling, for all the best people
attended the big sale—“ Mister James,” Mr. T.
A. Dibbs, Mrs. John See, Mr. Walter Hall, Mr.
Laidley, &c., &c. The billiard table was bought
by Mr. Eddie Playfair, for <£l9s ; a walnut and
satin wood bedroom suite was knocked down to
Mr. T. A. Dibbs for £lO6 ; and the cellar of
British cream sherry reached £7 10s. a dozen.
The Hotel Australia, The Australian Club, Mr.
Harry Rickards, and other celebrities figured
prominently among the buyers.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
the Grafter, the Melb. Cup winner, has been
sold for 2000 guineas to go to England, where he will race
In Mr. J. G." Clark’s colors.
* * * *
The biggest Sydney winners on The Grafter
were a certain publican who struck the double, llymettus
and Grafter ; a well-known pill-builder, who backs ’em
with much spirit; and a sporting tobacco-manufacturer.
• * * *
A Fremantle man who took a wager, £l2O to 2.\,
about Grafter and had no other bets, also went into four
sweeps and in each drew The Grafter. More peculiar
still, another sweep was being drawn incorrectly and was
stopped midway. In this one he had also drawn Grafter !

A N.S.W. cane-cutter, who had invested ill in
Tatr.’s biggest Cup sweep, got a wire announcing that a
certain number had drawn The Grafter, and, not having
received his ticket, supposed himself to be on that
animal’s chance. Accordingly, when news of the win
came to hand, he started out on a 16-storey jamboree-
cut ignominiously short by the discovery that the mes-
sage had been intended for another person of name differ-
ing by a single letter.

Symmetry’s performances at the late V.R.C.
meeting seem to call for a word of explanation. In the
mile Coburg Stakes, on Derby day, the handsome filly
carried 7st. 31b. and finished nowhere, unbacked, un-
honored, and unsung. On Cup-day she was third favorite
for the six furlongs Railway Handicap, which she won in
a canter, under exactly- the same weight as before. Up
to then it might have been said that VV. R. Wilson’s filly
made a poor show in the Coburg Slakes, because she
couldn’t stay a mile ; but, lo ! on the Thursday she started
a warm favorite for the Oaks, and took the lj-tnile race
comfortably. Then she went to Williainstown, and
finished last for the Cup, her stable companion, Aurifer-
ous, being at the extreme other end of the field.
* • * •
Seeing that Lee Metford’s interference with
Cocos (only- four starters!) the other Saturday was not
attributed to foul riding, it must be charged to the horse,
or rather, to the course. Lee Metford, being on the out-
side, naturally bored in towards the rails. Probably he
is a brainy animal, and as he didn’t know but what he
would have to make another journey round the course,
he objected to Cocos having the inside berth. An Aus-
tralian racer soon learns to hug the rails, same as a circus
hoise.
* * • *
One of the V. A.T.C. stewards lodged a rather
violent complaint with a daily pressman who made nasty
insinuations re the honesty of some of the racing at
Caulfield Cup meeting. “ You don’t mean to assert,”
said the scribe, “ that every horse was on the job ?” The
aggressive potentate thoughtlessly replied that he only
saw one animal which was very stiff, and he didn’t take
particular notice of its stiffness because he was too busily
engaged watching his own horse. “ Well, if you can’t
keep an eve on everything you ought not to be a steward,”
said the pressman. Whereupon the steward, abandoning
his haughty tone, begged the other fellow to come inside
and talk the matter over quietly, and the lion and the
lamb went inside and sat down together, no doubt. The
steward was the lamb at this stage of the proceedings,

bust and Hades full of oats won’ t hurt him. Which sug-

A picturesque psean of poesy rare
Concerning the merits of Forrester’s pair,
but was deterred by the dread thought that perhaps The
Bulletin had backed neither of ’em. ’Tis a cruel world
where the acceptability of poetry is reckoned its only
justification.”

Secretary Byron Moore waxed rather wroth at
an Argus statement that the V.R.C. rule re smoking ora
the Grand Stand Terrace is never enforced, and was justly
mad when the editor printed his letter of explanation
minus a pertinent par. : “ Because officials are not seen,
and heard all over the place annoying and irritating our
vast crowd of visitors it does not. necessarily follow thaH
regulations are so utterly neglected as to become a bye-
word.” The good management at Flemington is recog-
nised in the fact that the iron heel of authority- never
treads on anybody’s corns. As a matter of fact there is;
very little smoking on the Terrace, merely because the few
careless ones who do break the rule are not bullied into l
insubordination. A reproving look from an angel in a.
muslin dress will put out a cigar quicker than ten official
threats.

scribe’s advice to punters at next year’s racing carnival.

rately for the minor stake.

on “ latest form.” For the Final Handicap, at Fleming-

against the victorious Catspaw two days earlier, whereas
Elusive had performed very moderately at the meeting
up to then, it was surprising to find the mare the better
favorite of the two. She carried the stable-boys’ money
and won, the “ black ” colt being nowhere. Next week,
Wilson’s Symmetry and Auriferous ran in Williamstowm
Cup, and the mare, on the strength of two brilliant wins,
at Flemington, was preferred to Auriferous. This time,
the black colt won all the way, and Symmetry finished;
last.

In Melbourne, as in Sydney, it is left for the-
suburban courses to distinguish between “ reversal of
form ” and “ running a bye.” At Flemington and Rand-
wick during any big meeting, the most extraordinary
“ reversals of form,” from day to day, are passed over by
the stewards, so long as the owner is “influential,” bub-
woe betide the countryman who tries the game on.

The Lament of the Kensington (Sydney) Race-
course co. When the gee-gees thunder round Kensing- ,
ton course the voice of the paying crowd inside is a-
faint squeak compared with the awful roar of the non-
financial horde outside the fence. Without are dealers,
charging a “ brown ” for a good view from their carts;:
extremely dubious box-perched bookies shouting the
odds; box-stalls where highly-colored and fearfully '
mysterious drinks are sold for what they will fetch; and
a dense crowd of “ sports ” who must go to a race or
burst, and are too poor or too mean to pay for admission.
And now the K.R. co. wants Randwick Council to shift
the whole caboodle, but the Council doesn’t see it.
Writer once saw a weird example of the ruling passion
mighty amidst ph vsical decay. An aged crone with her
withered, trembling hands held up some parcels of
groceries to the howling bookie pleading piteously to hinii
to lay- her a bit on some horse—she knew he was a dead
cert. “If he aint, take me rations! Guv’ment pays.
2s. 6d. for ’em ! ”

At Hobart turf club’s meeting, run Nov. 5, a
mare called Guy Fawkes was a starter. The betting
public were, of course, expected to rush the coincidence,
and some knowing owners rubbed their hands as they
watched the tote-figures. Their dart was a mare whose
chances of paying a £lO div. seemed rosy. But Guy
Fawkes led from start to finish, the niare aforementioned,
after getting off badly, running second in a field of half-
a-dozen. The public pocketed a 2)4 to 1 div., and
enthusiastically dilated upon the “morality” of coinci-
dences.
• « * *
The successes of Merman, Georgic, Maluma,
Ebor, and one or two other Australian horses in Eng-
land, have been assisted, in most cases, by the heavy
state of the ground. Australian thoroughbreds, as a
rule, are bigger-boned and altogether rougher customers
than the ’Ome bred ones. Likewise the French and
American racers show more size and less “quality” than
their English cousins, and are generally underrated on
account of their less dandy appearance, until they have
fairly proved themselves good bits of stuff. Granting,
though, that the Australians will not be so kindly treated
by the handieapper in future events, there is likely to be
a growing demand for our best animals. They are to be
bought at low'er prices than the “ cracks” of France and
'Murka, for the reason that they haven’t the same chance
to win rich prizes in their native land, and the turf in
England being more often soft than hard their extra
strength should be a decided point in their favor. On
the same line of argument it would seem that Australian
speculators might as well try their luck with importing
some good class English racers to a country where they
would feel happier and get a better show to travel fast.
London Referee was remarking, the other day', that
English horses almost invariably improve when they' go
abroad to a more genial climate. When will VV. R. Wil-
son or somebody import a batch of uppah middle-class
animals from’Ome? Horses that have won certificates
of merit, and may be fairly expected to do something
considerable.

Melb. Argus, a journal that seems anxious to
shine as the friend and adviser of Ikey Mo, has been
making some mildly cynical comments on the “Tatt.”
sweep business. The odils-laying “ metallieian ” considers
himself cruelly robbed of the custom which flows to the
Hobart lottery-promoter, and on his behalf, no doubt, the
Argus says .-—“Certainly ‘Tattersall,’ or the person for
whom that name is a symbol, does not serve the public
for naught. He deducts 10 per cent, from the gross total
sent him, to pay expenses and reward him for his exer-
tions. . . Hence it is clear that the one thing which
would pay him better than to make an excursion to the
other side of the globe with the money entrusted to him
is to stay' here and promote other sweeps in subsequent
years. ... It might be thought that his annual levy of
10 per cent, would soon exhaust the fund from which he
draws. But it is annually renewed by the toil of the sub-
scribers, and, like the Israelite of old, his handful of meal
and his cruse of oil never fail.” Quite so. Yet the pro-
fits of “Tattersall” are small and reasonable in compari-
son with the percentage which the bookie derives from the
money entrusted to his care every year.
Leaving book-bets out of the question. Ikey
handles many tens of thousands of pounds per annum,
and the average amount that sticks to his jewelled fingers
is considerably more than two “ bob ”in the £. He con-
tinues to carry out his obligations to winners simply
because steady'bookmaking is a more attractive game
than welshing. In many cases he has tried both games
and learned that honesty is the fatter policy, therefore he
doesn’t suspend payment when a warm favorite happens
to win. The Argus has no ground lor surprise in the
fact that “Tatt.” doesn’t exhaust the fund from which
he draws. The one marvellous thing in this racing world
of Australia is the public’s readiness to go on accepting
short .prices from ready-money' bookmakers when they
have practically the tote remedy in their own hands.
The public-uneaning the pleasure-seeking crowd of
punters, as distinguished from horse-owners and
“ sharps’’—Heave fully 30 per cent, of their yearly outlay
in the hands of ilke.v Mo. Their investments are made,
as a rule, at considerably shorter prices than the starting
odds quoted by theipress. Ikey plunders them, whereas
Tatt. charges no moreithan the maximum com. allowed
to the tote. The Argus might as well express amaze-
ment that the tote stream doesn’t dry up. Meantime a
lot of “ constant readers ” are almost beginning to wonder
whether Ikey Mo has bought a share in that misguided
newspaper.

Ed. Bulletin.— Re diversity.of morning journal
starting-price quotations, let me .say that the Argus
quotations are accepted as official, and the. bookies pay on
the word of that journal even where it would be more in
consonance with their interests to accept the figures of
the Age. This practice is somewhat, awkward ifqr) those
who undertake commissions—especially fqr .those who
undertake commissions for ladies. Ladies are always.un-
reasonable and sometimes suspicious. I took out ss.|fqr
a more or less esteemed petticoated friend to back .Pirate
for the Steeple. I waited as long as I could without
losing a seat, and took the best price then ruling—3 to 1.
But in the next ten minutes he eased off and the starting-
price was fives. At time of writing I have not decided
whether to pay over 30s. and defer the theatre until next
month, or to part 20s. and endure cold glances of sus-
picion for the term of my natural life.—Miyaii.

The leading Adelaide sly-tote man admits to
having made a profit of £6OO, commissions and fractions,
out of Melb. Cup meeting. No wonder the tote-man
comes up and pays his fine of £5O with a smile !
* * * *
The victory of, say, ten warm public fancies in
succession at a Flemington meeting would reduce five-
sixths of the “ring” to a state of ridiculous insolvency.
Such as had not already lost more than their available
•capital would decline to bet against anything that
possessed a visible chance, whilst the dead-brokers would
simply stand, with closed bags, glaring at their hungry
creditors. The public are in a position to lose
an immense amount of money- to the “ books,” but
they are not in a position to win it. Certain newspapers
at this time of year entertain the public, as a body', with
gorgeous calculations of the amount which the public, as
a body, might win from Ikey Mo, and it’s all gammon and
spinach. The difference between the tote and the book-
maker is that the former admits to winning 8 or 10 per
•cent, of every- public investment without fail, whereas the
latter, who nets about 20 per cent, on an average, pre-
tends that the balance was won from him rather than
from his unsuccessful clients. ■
* * * •
“ Whirligig ” : “ Tattersall ” has sums of money
left over, unapplied for, from each consultation. His
trust-fund, so compiled, is probably now of cclossal pro-
portions. A drover, or sailor, or any- other worker, goes
away and, perchance, never sees a result-list. It may be
sent to him, if he is careful to ask for it and forwards the
stamp required. But, at the same time, it may never
reach his hand, but be getting daily unrecognisable with
fly-specks in some back-blocks kerosene-tin, while he is in
kingdom come or the Never-Never. Also, the most care-
ful of “ sports ” may fail to pick out the right figures from
the mass, or he may mistake the consult., and this is easy
•enough of late. These things naturally all make for
“ Tattersall,” who does not, I am sure, wish or want it.
Now, why does not our trusted friend establish a public
•charity-fund out of the above surplusage of unearned
increment—or send every one a notice of his wins, and, if
mecessary', hunt and harry, and look-up executors and
trustees until his duty is complete ? There's millions
in it for “ Tattersall ’’—he is well-trusted now, but Caesar’s
wife would then not be in it with him.

Ed. Bulletin. — You made a hundred and fifty
pounds’ book on Moonee Valley Cup, not one hundred.
Add the odds and the stake together, and the total is the
book You had 175)4 to pay the worst, and it looks very
pretty—on paper, flow accommodating your punters
must have been to allow you to lay exactly what you
wanted, and as you were 26)4 °- r - (over-round), you
should have laid that against the favorite at 2’s, because
that would give you another £l2 10s profit and you
couldn't lose anything. In future, dear Bulletin, always
make the favorite the worst. Never lay your full book
against outsiders, or else you will never throw up your
castor. You will find as you proceed that it is not
always possible to lay ’em all up so nice and even as
you did at Moonee Valley. One of these days you
will be caught “napping” by laying two “ponies”
on the field and 4 to 1 bar one, and the poor punters will
take 50 to 25 about something and 40 to 10 something
else, and then the number-board will have a shock, and
the first something’s number will “go out,” and you will
return his money, and the second something will be a hot
favorite at evens, and you will look into that bag
and find 10 to pay 50 with. Right here your “agility”
can have a rousing gallop and try and get 40
out of the “stume pots”—for that’s what they are
generally- when the above tactics are employed. I shall
look with a keen interest to your next “ book.” Try one
of the suburban ramping grounds. Don’t say you “ re-
served ” Lochaber; the punters reserved that for your
book. One effect your par. will have is to bring a lot of
new blood and boodle to the ringmen, nine-tenths of
whom are cadging for meals in Pitt-street every day.
Some are all right, but ask any ringman how many solvent
“books ” there are.— Sievier.

A man who had been at bicycle-sports came
down Queen-st., Auckland, to ascertain what had won
Caulfield Cup. Seeing a paper stuck outside a shop, he
thought it was a “ result ” notice. The letters seemed
to be sprinting around like quad-pacers, but he managed
to read —“(1) Schnapper ; (2) Rissoles ; (3) Beef Olives.”
He read no further, but said aloud to himself, in a severe
tone, “There y’ are; I tole y’ so. ’Nother (hie) out-
sider won : you would back the blanky Chief, an’ he’s
dead last.” So he was. The last item on the bill-of-
fare was, “ Antonio, Chef.”
■ » *
Dear Bulletin. —The artless “Victor” wrote
you re the Cup : “ A wary backer generally wished to
know whether owner, trainer, and jock are right." But
I would ask in the historic words of a bygone Ikey Mo,
what the blanky blank has the owner got to do with it ?
A respectable jockey tries his hardest to win, and a
decent trainer does his best to qualify the nag for victory
after he has got his instructions to prepare it for a cer-
tain race. An owner can t play any tricks with an actual
starter, not if his servants, the trainer and jock, are de-
termined to go straight. The worst he can do is to scratch
his bit of property. The late Fred Gretton (of Bass and
Co.’s Brewery) was an ill-conditioned beast with a par-
ticular fancy for scratching public favorites at the last
moment. When his trainer, Dick Porter, disgusted at
his tactics, asked him to state whether his intentions re
certain horses were strictly honorable, Gretton protested
that he had a right to do as he chose with his own. “ And
I,” said Porter, “ can give you a week’s notice to remove
your horses from my siable.” Gretton had to go away in
a hurry and find a trainer to help in his dirty work.—F.

Although the wickets about Sydney on Satur-
day were batsmen’s wickets, the scoring in the senior
matches, with few exceptions, was poor. Playing for
South Sydney, C. Gregory showed himself worthy of the
family name by running up 203 in brilliant style, the only
other century being made by Richardson, of E. Sydney,
who scored 127. Amongst the bowlers,‘"Pye, for Central
Cumberland, took nine Leichhardt wickets for 40 runs,
and scored 86 not out, which looks well for his chances
this season. Last year’s Paddington cracks, Mackenzie
and Trumper, are not yet in fettle, and were disposed of
for 30 runs between them.
* • * *
Adelaide cricketers had bad wickets until Nov.
5, when batsmen opened out. Clenv. Hill (78) and Lyons
(66—14 hits for four) were in great fettle. Green (dropped
from the S.A, team this season) made 142 not out, with-
out a chance, and there is now a unanimous outcry'
against his exclusion. S.A. has unearthed a promising
bowler in a young player named Marrett, a country
trundler, who has done remarkably well. Like Howell,
Marrett is sturdy' and muscular, and can bowl all day.
W.A. cricket, after long languishing under the
old club system, has this year been galvanised into such
vigor by the introduction of the electoral system, that
1899 is expected to witness the formation of an inter-
provincial eleven.

George Bull writes from London, under
date 11/10/’9B “ I travelled to Chatham to see the races
between the Irex and the Maid of Kent. One —the first
of the trio—was enough. Maid Of Kent rushed through
it like a torpedo-destroyer; the-Sydney boat lumbered
painfully astern, and staggered home under overwhelm-
ing canvas and a far too-numerous crew' nearly lOmin.
late on a 12-mile course. Wyllie, A.R.A., who, with Linton
Hope, designed the Maid of Kent, took his win as a man
does who all along expected it; Foy' accepted the hope-
less inferiority of Irex gamely and in admirable spirit.
Maid of Kent had the best brains in the English boat-
designing profession, and money was never stinted. Re-
sult—an absolutely unsinkable craft, light and thin
almost as a wager-boat, a regular bag-o’-tricks—roller-
,boom for reefing while under way, patent instantaneous
hoist for centreboard, and bamboo upper spars. Flying
light, she draws about 6in., and shows 4 or sin. of fore-
foot when well down. ’Thwartship section shows how
she is unsinkable, and how the seas escape through the
centreboard-box as they come aboard. Candidly, Irex
couldn’t look at her under any conceivable conditions,
and Foy and all the Sydney boys who tried to draff her
home know it. Maid of Kent is tougrh, and shallow
—lrex is broad, deep, and heavy —hinc illae lachtymae*
A 22-footer for Sydney on Maid of Kent lines would open
the eyes of the younff gentlemen who fancy they have
done fast thinffs over the Manly course, and for the sake
of education in this class of boat-buildinff in Sydney I
sincerely hope you may see such a wonder, for wonder is
the only name for Maid of Kent.”

The Australian maker of pugilistic champions,
Larry Foley, saw stronff-man Sampson at work during the
week, and this is how he sums the new-comer’s ability up
as compared with those who have been here before him :
“ They are the tallow-candles," says Larry, “ he is the
electric-liffht ! They are the strains of sand—Sampson is
the mountain ! Why, give him three-penn’orth of snuff
and he’d shift them all with a fairly healthy sneeze.”
* * » *
An index to Sampson’s marvellous strength may
be gathered from the easy off-hand manner in which he
has been breaking coins in halves round Sydney since his
arrival. Sixpences and shillings seem as pieces of lead in
his hands. At a favorite city resort, the other day, he
quite succeeded in convincing Hebe that she had given
him a cracked shilling, which broke in two as he swept
the change off the counter.

Lucifer has bitten off a big lump — much more
than he can chew, some people think —in matching
himself against Harry Dunn. The American will be
giving away at least 3st., and is meeting a man who,
during-five years’tour of the States and England, never
suffered defeat once though he wrestled a score or more
matches, perhaps. Among Dunn’s victims were Evan
Lewis (“the Strangler”), Farmer Burns, Schumacher,
and the German strong boy. Wonder if Lucifer, when
at home, ever dreamt of meeting any of these giants of
the wrestling arena V
* • • *
The St. Kilda Cycling Carnival made an
uneventful start on Saturday, in the presence of a
largish multitude, which expectorated freely and knew
all the riders by their Christian names. Wheel-racing
seems to have lost its charm for the outside public. The
grand-stand reserve at St. Kilda is filled with all sorts
and conditions of pedallers, mostly young arid blasphem-
ous, who take 6 “bob” to 4 about the favorite from seedy
bag-men, professing to be “ registered ” by some unknown
power. The attitude of the police towards these bogus
Ikeys is quite friendly. They are even permitted to
stand upon the benches around the rails and howl the
odds in a major key, and if one of them were to risk
a whole sovereign in a bet and lose it, he would probably
be able to give the welshed backer in charge for creat-
ing a disturbance. Bike-racing management at St. Kilda
is evidently in weak hands.
A poor afternoon’s sport— with 20 events on the
programme and only £64 of prize-money hanging to the
whole lot—produced no new idols. Walne is seemingly
the best man as before, W. C. Jackson, Don Walker and
“ Loo ” Barker coming a bit behind the ex-Queenslander,
and “Jack Parsons” —beloved of the small boys—vainly
struggling to get back into the front rank.

Edwards, the Yankee who undertook to bike
100 miles a-day for 12 months, put up a remarkable per-
formance before going down under to an attack of
typhoid. He kept up the weary wobble as per contract
for 250 days.

At last, by a simple Gazette notice, N.S.W.
Govt, absolutely protects mulga and all the family
of edible scrub from timber-cutters in the far north-
west of the province. Mulga is the only reliable
cattle, and sheep fodder in that droughty part; a
dozen huge squattages at least would have been
swept of every head of stock but for the growth.
Unfortunately, the wood, though scanty, is re-
markably fine fuel; and so a great tract around
Broken Hill, and for 30 miles out from the Tarra-
wingeeandSilvertonlines, and smallerareas around
other mining townships, have already been cleared
of the plant to the encouragement of dust-storms,
among other things.

The Falling Leaves
Give Warning of Winter
So the falling of the hair tells
of the approach of age and declin-
ing power. No matter how barren
the tree nor how leafless it may
seem, you confidently expect leaves
again. And why?
Because there is Life
at the Roots.
So you need not worry about the
falling of your hair, the threatened de-
parture of your youth and beauty. And why ?
Because if there is a spark of life re-
taining in the roots of the hair
Ayer’s 1
Hair Vigor
will arouse it into healthy activity. The
hair ceases to come out: it begins to grow :
and the glory of youth is restored to you.

ASSISTANTS Engaged for the Country.
Dear Bulletin. — Be pastor Yarrington’s
lecture on the Oxford movement. Curious how
the average British Protestant worships Luther
with generally only the haziest idea of that per-
son’s moral calibre. As a matter of fact, the
great reformer was roughly materialistic, with a
strong dash of the sensual, and did a good
many other things besides floor a material devil
with a candlestick. He held that a woman was
only to be respected in proportion to the number
of children she bore. Also, the common R.C.
belief that he died in a drunken orgie is possibly
not ill-founded. With all, however, he was a man.
What have the Sabbatarian Saint Dunstans of
to-day to say to this : “ If you ever find yourself
keeping the Sabbath for the sake of the day, work
on it, play on it, dance on it —do anything except
keep it ! ” —C.C.
Some items from the insolvency schedule
of Ah, Pat, storekeeper, Inglewood :
Business kept till last year 1897. Young often said he
retire and sick of it and advertised any where tried to sell
out all. But no body have it then he asked me carry on
myself and give him the money he put down and £3OO for
good will he intent go home to China, then i considered
this place is rising So i Bought him out at together £157
10s. in time payment and -i thought could do better as if
i get an european drive to the country. But whoever
can make no defferent that unobtain my wishes and i
seeing that can pay 30s. per week to keep extra man to
drive and got 5 or 6 pounds from country. So i gave up
runing Country and kept one assistant from January this
year and since January many long over dtie account
to met and connection all time and it make my brain
turned over and over and lost sleep for manage this diffi-
culty Business. And i had trouble in last June for owed
the rent ran up to £35 that Mrs. solicitor sent his
man came to the shop and take stock without a day
notice then i don’t know what i can do i had Ten pounds
in hand he said must want all I considered after a while
ran to Mr. G. Floyd to get £25 to be enough to meet this
unexpected trouble also paid him 14s. expense. But at
present the rent had run up £24 again and some other
Bills and many long overdue account coming to keen and
close impossible me to meet it. Sometime creditors
sent me threaten Languages seems to be going
to press and now if meet the rent cannot meet
other creditors if i meet creditors other account the rent
come to me at the same thing don’t last. I cann see my
way clear in this great difficulties and troubles position
So i put it to my assignees hand to save waste my Brain
to manage any further. I not intent to do this 1 cannot
help it myself i am alway do my best to any body what i
could, and what i don’t like myself i won’t unto others
and what i don’t like when done to myself i don’t do to
others this is the principle of my mind, here is the
Books i been kept But i never Balance the Business and
taking stock as me and Young confidential each other and
never thought be in this trouble.

Erskine and Bearcroft were rival counsel
in a suit where a draper sought to recover his bill
from a husband. The wife had been a lady of
pleasure. Erskine: “My client gave up all his
connections for this woman.” Bearcroft: “And
she gave up all mankind for him.”
Concluding broadside of wrangle between
two Sydney ’bus-drivers, one very fat: —“ Garn,
yer fat-bellied old ! it I had me way with
you, I’d scoop out yer bloomin’ yes-no and make
a blanky dorg-kennel of yer.”
Egyptian Sirdar Kitchener described —
Major-General Sir Horatio Herbert Kitchener is 48 years
old by the book, but that is irrelevant. He stands several
inches over Oft., straight as a lance, and looks out imperi-
ously above most men’s heads ; his motions are deliberate
and strong; slender, but firmly knit, he seems built for
tireless, steel-wire endurance, rather than for power or
agility; that also is irrelevant. Steady, passionate eyes
shaded by decisive brows, brick-red, rather full cheeks,
a long- moustache, beneath which you divine an immov-
able mouth ; his face is harsh, and neither appeals for
affection nor stirs dislike. All this is irrelevant, too ;
neither age, nor figure, nor face, nor any acci-
dent of person has any bearing on the essential Sirdar.
You could imagine the character just the same if
all the externals were different. He has no age but the
prime of life, no body but one to carry his mind, no
face but one to keep his brain behind. The brain and
the will are the essence and the whole of the man—a
brain and a will so perfect in their workings that
in the face of extremes! difficulty, they never seem to
know what struggle is. His precision is so inhumanly
unerring, he is more like a machine than a man. The
man Herbert Kitchener owned the affection of private
friends in England and of old comrades of 15 years’ stand-
ing ; for the rest of the world there is no man Herbert
Kitchener, but only the Sirdar, neither asking affection
nor giving’ it. His officers and men are wheels in the
machine ; he feeds them enough to make them efficient,
and works them as mercilessly as he works himself. And
if you suppose, therefore, that the Sirdar is unpopular,
he is not. No general is unpopular who always beats
the enemj'. Other generals have been better loved ;
none was ever better trusted. For of this one human
weakness the Sirdar is believed not to have purged
himself—ambition. He is on his promotion, a man
who cannot afford to make a mistake. Homilies
against ambition may be left to those who have failed in
their own ; the Sirdar’s, if apparently purely personal, is
legitimate and even lofty. If in this case he be not so
stiffly unbending to the high as he is to the low, who shall
blame him ? He has climbed too high not to take every
precaution against a fall. But he will not fall—just yet,
at any rate. So far as Egypt is concerned, he is the man
of destiny—the man who has been preparing himself six-
teen years for one great purpose. For Anglo-Egypt he
is the Mahdi—the expected -the man who has sifted
experience and corrected error ; who has worked at small
things and waited for great, marble to sit still and fire to
smite, steadfast, cold, and inflexible ; the man who has
cut out his human heart and made himself a machine to
retake Khartoum.

Perhaps yon are one of those people, and are not

The wise man will at once put himself in the
witness-box, and ask himself the following ques-
tions, as will the wise woman :
Do I sleep badly ; do I wake up in the morning,
even when I appear to have slept all night, fagged
and jaded, with lack-lustre eyes and an inclination
only to go to bed again. Do I eat badly, or, when
Ido eat “well,” does my food agree with and
nourish me ? Do Igo about my work dejectedly—
do I suffer from headaches, pains in either, the
limbs or body, and indulge in depressing retros-
pections ? Do I, in short, suffer from a loss of
mental and physical elasticity—that exultant
appreciation of life which all healthy people must
have ?
If to even one of these questions the answer be
“ Yes,” then that man realises that he wants a
tonic—-something to give him an appetite, to
steady his nerves, and to make him strong and
active in the daytime, and afford him calm and
refreshing sleep at night.
Such a tonic is Minerva Tonic, the world’s
greatest and only permanent restorative, the
praise of which is general and unstinted.

serviceable as a tonic remedy for strengthening
the system.”

A N.S. Wales P.M., whose name is no-
thing like Jones, was at one time also a Crowd
Lands Agent. lie took money from a squatter fot
lahd which he afterwards found was reserved.
Now, the rule is that all moneys received by an
officer must be paid into the Treasury, whence any
refunds are made. The official, not wishing to
advertise his error, refunded direct to the sheep-
man, who kicked up such a dust that the matter
reached head office, and the delinquent Was
ordered to Sydney under suspension. Old Jack
Robertson was then Minister, and interviewed
him thus : “Umph ! You’re Mr. Blanky Jortes,
Police Magistra te at Cow Flat, umph. Nice blanky
games you’ve been up to! I’ve a blanky good
mind to dismiss you, you darned scoundrel ” —and
jumping up and putting his extended palm behind
him, suggestively—“ I suppose you do a bit of
this blanky business, too ? ” Jones, recounting his
experiences to a crony afterwards, said, “ I didn’t
mind him swearing at me so much, doncherknow,
but when the old devil insinuated that I took tip,
I could have fainted.”

The N.S.W. National Art Gallery.
In providing a magnificent domicile for Art in the
Sydney Domain, the N.S.W. Government has
shown itself generously responsive to that higher
sentiment which forbids that a people not with-
out their hopes and aspirations, should be satis-
fied with bread alone. If I could catch the ear of
the new Minister for Education, who may be
credited with a laudable ambition to justify his
high office, and who is officially responsible in all
that relates to Art, I should like to say to him
that passing the contractor’s bill for the salons
recently completed cannot rightfully be taken as
marking the limit of his activity in this matter.
More properly, indeed, and on the assumption
that the administrative conscience in the educa-
tional section is in wholesome activity, I should
prefer to see it regarded as his starting-point.
•t * * *
The municipal governors of our city are, for the
most part, a poor enough set of creatures, but even
they have found the grace to acknowledge* that
they have much to repent of, much to learn, and
much to forget. Perish the thought that the
Trustees of the National Art Gallery of N.S. W.
should permit themselves to drift into a similar
Weakness. There are ancient acquisitions of the
“Dobson ” and other schools on the walls of the
Domain Gallery which not even a Gallery Trustee
would defend. Were we dealing with men
capable of being instructed by adversity, the exist-
ence of these exhibits could be tolerated if not
forgiven. But what is to be thought of men who,
with the accumulated abominations of nearly
three decades staring them in the face every time
they enter the building under their charge, are
content to move on indefinitely in the same dull
rut of helpless ineptitude?
* * • *
It is not necessary to say that East’s “ Sunlit
Haven,” Swan’s “ East African Leopards,”
Brangwyn’s “ Scoffers,” Riviere’s “Requiescat,”
are absolutely bad pictures. What I remark upon
them is that one and all they are innocent of any
distinctive quality or feature which should pro-
nounce them as suitably placed in a representative
national collection. They constitute the type of
picture with which one is haunted in the corridors
of the London dealer who, in most instances, has
a mortgage upon it to the extent of the cost of the
frame—the type which, at the end of an art-
season, was wont to find its way swiftly to its
doom, but which, in our own time, finds occasional
undeserved rescue at the hands of the Sydney-
London board of art-purchasers and the South
African millionaire.
* * » »
Look at the modelling of the right arm of the
girl in the immediate foreground of those truly
awful “ Scoffers.” Detached from its context,
who would ever pronounce it to be intended for a
human limb? In Riviere’s picture it is permis-
sible to say, “ Yes, the dog is a beauty, the armor
is faultlessly correct, the pattern of the embroidery
all that is to be desired.” But when these acces-
sories are brought in to reconcile us to nothing
better than the presentment of a cadaver
waiting for the undertaker’s last visit,
they arouse nothing better than a sense
of irritated disgust. There is one chance
for us. Should the Sydney morgue call for the
addition of an ornamental vestibule, it is to be
hoped that this precious “ Requiescat ” may be
promptly endorsed over to it. The extent to
which this wretched corpse was hawked about in
every possible world’s market, until an evil fate
brought it within the ken of the London selectors
for Sydney, is one of the open secrets of * the
picture-trade. Our men are now looked out for
in the centre of the picture-business, precisely in
the sense in which the yokel from behind our
mountain-ranges is looked for by the “ confidence ”
men of Hyde Park.
» * * *
I do not at all say that the London selections
are invariably bad. Mr. Yeend King’s “ Garden
by the River ” is one of the choicest productions
of an artist who, at his best, is far beyond the
average._ Mr. Margetson’s “The Sea hath its
Pearls ” is also a selection fairly calling for praise.
I’hat the right foot of the centre figure, with all
its purity of flesh tint, is as flat as a slab of jelly-
fish, without even a suggestion of contour or instep,"
is a deduction which does not seriously counter-
act the sense of satisfied artistic enjoyment yielded
by the picture as a whole.
* * * *
“ Why not then put the good against the bad ? ”
The question is of the essence of absurdity. It is for
the purpose of purchasing good pictures that the
Trustees of the N.S.W. Art Gallery are liberally
endowed with funds. When good pictures are not
offering can they not sit still and wait ? It is surely
a peculiar sense of compulsion under which they
operate when they fill these stately walls from
year to year with art exhibits in which the mean,
the mediocre and the commonplace constitute the
dominating note. A year or two ago the official
excuse of the Gallery Trustees for being con-
tinually struck with rubbish was that the growth
of a more exacting standard of taste was at all
times liable to overtake and stultify one’s estima-
tion of earlier purchases. When we are in front of
works like Peter Graham’s “ Glencoe,” recently
on loan from Melbourne—works like Leighton’s
“ Wedded ’’—like Cole’s “Arundel ’’—like Andrew
Gow’s “ Relief of Leyden,” it does not occur to us
to think of “standards of taste.” With a higher
standard of directing intelligence, the taste might
be trusted to look after itself.
* * m *
Every now and again it is thought necessary to
attach a working artist to the London board of
selectors. Naturally, the artist-selector must
have the compliment paid him of being requisi-
tioned for a picture of his own painting. Natu-
rally, under such circumstances, it would be quite
too unpolite to make any fuss about the price.
And that, I suppose, is how we come by Mr.
Alfred East’s “Sunlit Haven.” It was, at all
events, the method by which the Melbourne Art
Gallery acquired, for a trifle of IOOOgs., Professor
Herkomer’s unspeakably inartistic and hideous
picture of H.M. the Queen, which the Victorian
trustees daily pray may sooner or later be removed
from their custody, in the dead of the night, by
some public-spirited thief.
* * * *
The cost of a railway, a viaduct, or a new court-
house, is ascertainable by anyone having a know-
ledge of the literature of Parliamentary Blue
Books. The cost of any individual Domain
Gallery-picture is as completely blotted put from
human ken as if the transaction occurred in an-
other planet. Rumor has it that quite appalling
amounts 1m vebeen spent on some works—Riviere’s
“ Cadaver,” for example—which actually disfigure
and degrade the collection. If rumor is in error,
it can be easily overcome by a freer knowledge.
RAPHAEL SANZIO JONES.
Peter and the Voice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A desire to avoid raising harsh and revengeful
thoughts irt the minds of sundry creditors, by niy
presence among them, led me to take up niy
quartets in Rochester-] ane.
My little two-shillirig-a-week room overlooked
the lane, And whenever my work grew wearisome
I was able to aniuse myself by watching the Move-
ments of my neighbors. The staple industry df
the dozen inhabitants was bottles, which they col-
lected and disposed of to a marine dealer at the
end of the lane. My first impression was that
bottle-merchants could consume more beer and
make more noise about it than any other mundane
creatures, but when I became acquainted with
their wives I found I had done the bottle-ohs an
injustice. All day long, while their lords and
masters were out bottle-prospecting, these ladies
would stand at their doors, in various stages of
undress and sobriety, and bawl compromising and
highly-insulting remarks at each other, only stop-
ping to rush into the middle of the lane and bang
furiously their mud-bespattered offspring.
Whenever one of them possessed the where-
withal to indulge in the luxuries of the corner
pub. she became the centre of the envious abuse
of the other inhabitants, and carried herself with
that conscious importance which I formerly be-
lieved to be the sole prerogative of mayors and
town-councillors. It is a custom among savage
nations, who are not blessed with banks, to carry
their real estate about in the form of jewellery.
The Laneites followed this principle religiously m
regard to drink.
“Yah,” I heard an old dame - yeli to a younger
and more fortunate sister, “yer thinks yer ’igh an’
mighty becos ye’ve been drunk twice to-day—don’t
yer, yer ’ussy ? I can tell yer in boom time, when
champyne bottles was bumpin’ round, I’ve often
bin boozed fer two weeks at a stretch—garn, yer
bloomin’ hupstart! ” The young woman treated
this outburst of the old aristocrat’s with that ful-
ness of contempt which can only be attained by
the newly rich.
There was one little house that kept itself
strictly apart from Lane society.
It interested me, because I had never seen the
woman occupant, though I had heard her voice,
which might have once been an educated one,
though now shrill and acidulated by poverty.
The master of the house, whom I got to know
by the name of Peter, was a long, shambling fellow
with a damp, weak face, half Covered by a
straggling beard.
Every evening Peter would walk back from the
dealer’s, looking neither to left nor right, and never
by any chance going to the pub or speaking to his
mates.
After tea, lie would come out and sit smoking on
the step until summoned inside by the Voice ; even
on wet days I have seen him sitting there, holding
an umbrella over himself, and silently smoking.
One night, when returning home from a con-
vivial gathering, with a flask of spirits in my
pocket, I ran against Peter, lounging disconsolately
at the end of the lane. He looked so utterly
broken-up and weary that, in the fulness of my
heart, I invited him to come and share my bottle.
He gazed carefully at his house for a few seconds,
and then, desperately making up his mind, fol-
lowed me into my room.
I poured him out a stiff glass, which at oiioe
warmed him into revolt. He leant back in his
chair, and started recounting, in a brave voice, his
matrimonial troubles. “Ye ain’t seen my wife ? ”
he began. “ Well, I tell yer, sHe’si E fair terroi’i
and no mistake. She bosses me round, won’t let
me tyke a drop of beer, and won’t ’ehr pf itle
yarnin’ with the other blokes—says as she’s a
bloomin’ lydy, an’ must get respect”’
I filled Peter’s glass again, and he Went Bn with
added warmth.: “ I welit arid ’ad piiit tb-day;
a,nd she smelt it, and, before I. could lift hdy fjnger;
she up in i lirids irb orie oil tile ‘ poirit, ’ arid theri
turns me out without me tea ’’—Pe.t>r.helped, him-
self to another nip— “I tell ye, I ain’t goiri’ to
stand it. I’m a mari, airi’t I ? I’ll give ’er as good
as I gets —so there!”
I felt sorry for Peter, and I gave him my opinion
on the duty of a wife to a husband, and also on the
necessity of a man having a little alcoholic nourish-
ment occasionally. Finding himself supported,
Peter became very brave, and, marching to the
door, declared his intention “of goin’to be boss in
me own bloomin’ show.”
I watched Peter cross that road. His first steps
were very firm, but as he approached the door I
could see lie was trembling. He cautiously tip-
toed past it several times and then knocked ner-
vously.
» * * »
For about ten minutes I heard the shrill voice
that might once have been a lady’s, but I heard
nothing of Peter’s bass in answer.
For the next few days when passing my window
Peter kept his face turned away—l think there
must have been a mark on it.
In fact, I am certain there was, and I felt sorry
for Peter,
Also for the Voice,

A Songless Bird.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
C marked thy throat with music throb and thrill,
Thy tuneful voice in tender tumult swell ;
I marked thy changing cheek suffuse and fill,
Thy lips that laughed—a soft melodious bell;
A winged wonder—child of song wert thou,
And I— a songless bird upon the bough 1
Around thee, sweetly eager all, they prest—
The dainty warblers who were ling’ring near,
And one would sing the gentle billowy breast,
And one—the eyes that droop, so shyly dear ;
In dulcet phrase I heard them plead and vow,
And I —a songless bird upon the bough !
Yes —I —with seraph-harpings in my soul,
Could only gaze (O, drooping heart and wing!),
.While round I heard the puny raptures roll,
While ev’ry careless breast might homage bring,
Might breathe harmonious incense to thy brow—
And I —a songless bird upon the bough !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Backing a Dream.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I had a very peculiar dream a few years ago when
I was in New York. Gamblers, you know, are
mostly superstitious. I’d been “punting” in
partnership with an old pal named Fred Strong,
and we’d been doing so badly that our joint capi-
tal was down to fifty dollars. So, on one night of
the 3rd July, ’94, we had made up our minds to
“make a spoon or spoil a horn ” with the fifty
the next day at Brighton Beach. A good pro-
gramme had been prepared for the “Glorious
Fourth ;” entries were numerous, and a great holi-
day crowd sure to be there; so prices would be
liberal, and we determined to take a chance at
some long shots. It’s not often a favorite gets
home on a holiday at Brighton. Well, after a
rather hearty supper I got to bed late, and
ha l a restless night. Finally, however, I got to
sleep, and had a most vivid dream. I saw the
finish of a race just as distinctly as ever I saw any-
thing in my life. It was won by a black horse,
ridden by an English jockey named George
Taylor, who wore a black jacket with white
sleeves, and black and white quartered cap. The
number on the saddle-cloth was 8. Then, just as
clearly, I saw the numbers go up. with the jockey’s
names, on theboard—“Number 8 (Taylor) ” on top.
1 woke up as the clock was striking eight.
That’s a good omen, I thought. When I went to
the bar for my morning cocktail there were eight
men in the place. As I entered the restaurant
several men were going out. I looked round the
tables eight persons seated at breakfast. “By
Jove ! number B’s coming pretty strong to-day,” I
thought. “Guess I’ll have to back the dream.”
I breakfasted and called for my check. It was
numbered 88—and the amount was sixty cents. I
handed the cashier a five-dollar bill. He gave me
eight five cent", pieces, and eight half-dollars—
apologising for the small change. Well ! It was
coming pretty strong on the number 8. When I
met Fred I told him all the circumstances. He
was greatly impressed. As soon as we reached
the course we got a programme and scanned it
■with nervous impatience, for superstition was get-
ting a strong hold on both of us. In the fourth
race, a mile (8 furlongs), number 8 was Mr.
Lakeland’s black gelding, Stranger, 4 years old,
1081bs. (black jacket, white sleeves, black and
white cap). We stared at each other in silence.
At last I red gasped : “Why, it’s a dead moral,
old man ; a copper-lined cinch. We must plunge
on it. ” “Yes, ” I said; “ we’ll go the whole hog. ”
The only discrepancy, so far, was that Taylor
was not the Lakeland stable jockey.
Well, we looked on at the first three races —
never bet a cent. Stranger was good enough for
us, and we waited, wondering what price we’d get.
“Sure to be favorite,” said Fred. “Just our
luck,” I growled. You see, we’d become so
imbued with the idea of this dream that we
looked upon it as all over but the shouting.
When the numbers went up for the fourth race,
the jockey on number 8 was G. Taylor, and
presently out he came on Stranger, and number 8
on the saddle-cloth. That did settle it. We
rushed for the ring, which, in America, is a very
different style from ours. It is an immense shed,
and every bookmaker has a stand, on which is
a board with each horse’s name, and his price
chalked opposite. Stranger and another opened
at 4 to 1, but before we could get in the former
was rushed so that he was cut down to 2|. We
each took twenty-five dollars, and fought our way
through the crush in different directions. Lake-
land was going down the line, backing Stranger.
On my side the rush was so great that I saw the
books, one after another, rub out 2|. Down! down
went Stranger—2 to 1, 9 to 5, even touched 8 to 5.
I was in despair ; but I could not get through the
crowd. “Coine on; come on, boys; bet lively;
they’re at the post ! ” I gave one despairing
glance down the line, and at the last stand but
one saw Stranger. I tore through the mob,
but couldn't get within three or four rows of the
stand. Waving my money aloft, I yelled
“Stranger!” The bookmaker leaned across, and
stretching his arm over the heads of the people,
grabbed it. But he hesitated a second, looked at
the money, and spoke to his sheet writer. The
latter nodded. Just as my ticket was handed
over the bell rang. “They’re off,” yelled the
crowd, and there was a mad stampede for the
railings. I rammed the ticket into my pocket
without looking at it, and rushed out. I couldn’t
find Fred, but got a fair position for seeing. The
perspiration was falling off me and I was actually
trembling. I’m usually a cool gambler, but the
darned superstition had worked me into a nervous
state. I was really betting against my judgment,
for I didn’t like the horse.
Stranger was in a good position and going nicely
as they ran along the back stretch. Improving
along the top turn, he entered the straight a good
length in the lead and running easily. I felt a big
lump in my throat, “By Jove,” I said to myself,
“I’ll never laugh at dreams again. Let’s see!
Fred must Have got 2J. That’s a hundred
and twenty-five we win. ”
At the distance a red jacket and green cap shot
up to Stranger’s girths. Taylor sat down and
drew his whip. “Come on, Taylor; Good boy,
George ! Stranger ! Stranger ! The favorite ! The
favorite wins ! ” was shouted on all sides. I saw
Stranger’s tail switch, his ears went back, and my
heart sank into my boots. I knew it was all over.
Stranger had turned rogue, and was stopping like
a cur. In half-a-dozen strides the red jacket and
green cap was well clear of him, and I turned my
back on the race disgusted and cursing my folly
and superstition.
When I met Fred the first words he said, were
“You know, Bob, I’d never have thought of that
damned cur but for your dream. I couldn’t see
where he had a living chance —and what the deuce
they made him favorite on I don’t know.”
“ Where did he finish ? ” I asked.
“Eighth ! ” snarled Fred, with a scowl.
“It’s no use crying over spilt milk,” I said.
“By-the-bye, what did win?”
“Damfiknow. “Oh! number 13 —Banger.
One of old Bill Daly’s long shots again. I sup-
pose it was 50 to 1. ”
“Well, Fred, Banger’s a black horse; and
that’s as near as the dream has come to it.”
“ Curse your dreams, ” growled Fred, as he t re
up his ticket. “ What price did you get ? ”
“Five to two,” I said, producing the ticket;
and a nice time 1 had to get ”
“What’s the number?” asked Fred, as I
stopped, gazing open-mouthed at the ticket. He
took it out of my hand.
“ 1250 to 25 Banger,” he read. ' “ What infernal
fooling is this ? A nice mess and row there’d have
been over this ticket if we had won.”
“Holy Gee!” I shouted as a wild thought
flashed through my brain. I snatched the ticket
and rushed off to the stand I’d got it from. Not
a soul cashing. With a trembling hand I passed
the ticket up saying, “ What’s this ? ” “ What is
it!” said the cashier. “Why, it’s the only bet
we laid against Banger—so to I—and it’s spoilt
our book. How did you come to back it ? ” he
continued as he tore off the corner of the ticket
and stuck it on the file.
“Oh—a dream,” I stammered, for I’d hardly
got my breath.
“ A dream, eh ! Do your dreams often come
out ? ”
“ Well! —not often—at any rate, not like this
one 1 ”
“ I should think not ! Five—a thou—one, two,
three, four, five fifty’s—a twenty and a five.
Twelve hundred and seventy-five dollars. Bight!
Say ! Next time you back a dream, give some of
the other books a turn, will you ? We pass ! ”
As I grabbed the notes and turned away, Fred
was at my elbow.
“ Well!” he gasped. “The Lord is good to a
fool sometimes, isn’t he ? ”
“Ye-es, Fred. But I don’t think we’ll tempt
Providence again by backing a dream.”
How was it ? Why, in the crush and noise,
the bookmaker thought I said Ranger instead of
Stranger, when I handed up my money.
“ B.B.” ■ The fire which destroyed, half
Longreach (Q.), and did <£50,000 worth of damage,
originated in the pub. of a most violent opponent
to the laying-on of water from the bore to the
town. The bore yields 327,000ga1. per diem, the
water, at emergency, having pressure 971 b. to the
square inch. Had it been available the fire could
have been confined to the building in which it
started.
Premier Reid has made two grants of £lOO
each to help along Wragge’s observatory at Mount
Kosciusko ; also the reports therefrom are being
printed in Sydney Govt. Printing Office, at
N.S.W.’s expense. Wherefore it seems that
Wragge has got Beid firmly by the wool, or the
coat-tail, or somewhere. Yet the question about
the usefulness of high-level stations in meteor-
ological work seems no nearer a settlement. These
stat ons were tried for a long time in the United
States, and abandoned by the U.S. meteorologists
as useless. For a while the solitary, or almost
solitary, survivor of the species was the Ben Nevis
station in Scotland. It never produced any
visible results of a useful character, so far as the
public knows, and it was much scoffed at even
in Britain, except by Wragge and a few others.
Now even Ben Nevis has closed up. And the only
news that comes from Kosciusko is the invariable
report about how B. Ingleby got lost in the snow
while humping the tucker from Jindabyne.
A Reciprocal Situation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bonnivarde picked up an open envelope .md
removed its contents.
He read the curious, tangled writing to the end
of its four short lines. He passed his lean fingers
through his hair, replaced the note, carefully
putting it back exactly as he had found it, and
left the room. He went into the garden and
smoked three cigars—the one after the other. He
was evidently thinking hard.
* * * *
The river-wind bent the roses, and a beautiful
woman stood in the doorway of the house. Bhe
had dark hair and dark, slanting eyes. Her
fingers were without rings, her wrists without
bracelets. Presently she went in again.
* * * *
Bonnivarde’s wife was going to run away with
her brother-in-law. Bonni varde knew it.
She was going to run away that very night. Ho
knew that likewise.
The letter had told him all this, and yet lie did
not turn white with rage, or kick the gravel about.
On the contrary, he smiled, and blew fresh to-
bacco-smok e.
To tell the truth, he was Glad.
For, did he not like his brother’s wife uncom-
monly well ? So much nicer than his own. So
much plumper. Besides, she was fair, and did not
fly into devilish rages like Buna.
* * * *
Bonnivarde thought hard and wasted a great
quantity of matches. He would go at once to his
brother and offer a swap. Offer to give Buna in
exchange for Mae.sie. On second thoughts he
decided to leave that to a later date.
He would first frighten them.
* * * •
Bonnivarde’s brother, in his letter to Buna, had
said a quarter to eight.
* * * *
Bonnivarde put the clock on fifteen minutes.
So that at half-past seven the little side window
above the bed of heavily-scented wall-flowers
creaked slowly up, and the head of Buna showed
itself black against the yellow house-side. A man
signed to tier from the shadow of a huge pitto-
sporum, and in another moment she sank deep to
the ankles in the soft, noiseless earth of the
garden. Bonnivarde (for it was he) sprang to her
side.
She uttered a hasty exclamation and dropped
on her hip in the wall-flowers.
Then there was a sharp report and Bonnivarde
pitched head-foremost on to the gravel.
Meanwhile a third figure, which had been
creeping over the narrow lawn, turned and fled
into the darkness.
• * * *
A week later Bonnivarde had quite recovered
from his trifling wound. And Mrs. Bonni-
varde, writing to her brother-in-law, said, “ It
is well, perhaps, that he thinks I imagined myself
to be shooting you and not him. He says that he
has only himself to blame. I am now a paragon
of virtue in his eyes, and it will be hard to pull
me off the pedestal. Dear Wally, Max is going
out to-night. I shall be so lonely.”
* * * *
Bonnivarde knows that his wife recognised him
when she fired ; also, that she fired to kill. Never-
theless, he praises her fidelity.
Besides, Bonnivarde gets letters from his
brother’s wife, saying: “ Dear Max, Wally is
going out to-night. I shall be so lonely.”
Vic.
HUGH R. M'CRAE.

AH EXTRACT.
“The first night I had slept a little from sheer exhaustion. The
next was terrible. I vomited incessantly; I was chilled to the bone, and
could not warm myself. I suffered horrible pain in the head, and through-
out my whole body. Next day, of course, I was a wreck. They told me I
had been delirious half the night I remember nothing of the day; but
next night I got a couple of hours’ sleep, and was rather better in conse-
quence. But vomiting went on steadily, and the fourth night I was again
delirious. The next day I was as bad as ever; but, thanks to a good
dose of Beecham’s Pills, I had an excellent
night, and I truly believe that they saved my life. I knew well where I
picked up this fever—in the marshes of Mwenzo, and near Kituta —and the
killing thing was being unable to stop to cure it; the ten days on the lake
especially—often without food for twenty-four hours—nearly finished me.
I felt certain at the time that I was done for; and I very nearly was.”— See
“ Three Years in Savage Africa ,” by Lionel Decle — Methuen & Go., London.
Mr. Lionel Decle covered a distance of over 7000 miles at one
stretch, between Capetown on the South and Mombassa on the East coast of
Africa. It is worth noting that this ‘‘ unique journey ” would probably
have been cut short; and, to quote the introduction by Mr. H. M. Stanley,
M.P., “ its vivid record ” would never have been penned had not Mr.
Lionel Decle provided himself with a supply of
BCECNAM’S PILLS.

case, with Commentary and Plan of Scene of alleged Outrage.

TESTIMONIAL.
Adamstown, September 11th, 1896.
BONNINGTON & CO., Manufacturers of Irish Moss, &c.,
179 Harris Street, Sydney.
Dear Sirs,— Being a sutierer for years from fibroid phthisis and bronchitis, I de-
sire to add my testimony in favor of your Irish Moss. As a soother of irritating
cough, with much expectoration, I have found it to be the most effectual of anything
I have tried. It loosens the mucus, which, therefore, comes more readily away, and
the cough is thereby considerably pacified.
I remain, Yours respectfully,
THOMAS STOKES.
